THE BED PAGE OF VILLON.
WHEN Swinburne was making translations
from Villon, and came to the‘Balladeof the
Ladies of Old Time,’ he noted that this had been
‘ incomparably ’ rendered by D. G. Rossetti, and
P cssed to the ‘ Lords ’ without venturing another
version. One might think it as daring to attempt
rivalry with Swinburne as with Rossetti, yet in
the late and lamented ‘Savoy Magazine ’ Theo-
d'>re Wratislaw had so much hardihood. He
turned into English the famous Ballade of the
Gibbet, and indeed the result has justified him,
though Swinburne’s version remains supreme.
14 ere are the two for comparison with the text.
(The illustration—imaginative, surely?—of the
f<mrehes patibulaires at Montfaucon, whence the
bodies of anti-social persons swung year after
year, is also owed to ‘The Savoy.’)
Swinburne seems the truer in form and feeling
as well as in phrase,: he is a better interpreter of
Villon’s strange soul. Wratislaw paraphrases as
much as he translates; and the beginning of
paraphrasing is as the letting out of waters.
Thus he has lost ‘cinq, six ’ from the first stanza,
and in replacing them in the second he has lost
‘se vous clamons.’ And ‘hideous shapes’ is
nowhere in Villon, And—so on. Swinburne has
varied the original in one important place only:
for the image of bodies pecked by birds till they
are dented like thimbles he has substituted bodies
pecked like fruit on a wall—which is an excellent
simile, yet not the original, and questionably as
good as the original. Probably to lessen a little
the force of the Swinburnian comparison, Wratis-
law has adopted an alternative envoy for which
there is authority. But there does not seem any
authority for his
Have naught to say to them that with us vie—
which, indeed, is an incomprehensible line in any
case. What does ‘ vie ’ mean in such connection ?
The ballade text here given is that of P. L.
Jacob (pub. Jannet, Paris, 1854), and it is evi-
dently that which Swinburne followed. (Villon
seems throughout his work as corrupt as the most
corrupt classic, with a fine field for philologers’
emendations.) But neither Jacob, Swinburne,
nor Wratislaw has suggested an envoy text which
the authorities warrant, and which yields the best
meaning of all. In this the lines
Hommes, icy n’usez de moequerie,
in the third stanza, and
Ne soyez done de nostre confrairie
envoy, would bes transposed. The ‘done’ of ‘Ne soyez done’ follows more naturally on the recapitulation of ills in the third stanza; the
v,- I n -t r< v ni j k l 6 .°PP 0! j lt, o n of Prince Jesus and ‘Homines’; and various nuances of suggestion come as buttresses of the transposition—
which, be it noted again, actually occurs in some authorities.
THE EPITAPH
In form of a ballad, which Villon made for himself and
his comrades, expectin,g to bp hanged, along with
them.
Men, brother men, that after us yet live,
Let not your hearts too hard against us be ;
For if some pity of us poor men ye give,
Thfe sooner God ahail take of you pity.
Here are we five or six strung up, you see,
And here the flesh that all too well we fed
Bit by bit eaten and rotten, rent and shred,
1 And we the bones grow dust and ash withal)
Let no man laugh at us discomforted,
But pray to God that He forgive us all.
If we call on you, brothers, to forgive,
Ye should not hold our prayer in scorn, though we
Were slain by law ; ye know’ that all alive
( Have not wit alwaj to walk rjghteoualy ;
Make therefore intercession hekrtily
With Him that of a virgin’s womb was bred,
That His graoe be not as a dry well-head
For us, nor let Hell’s thunder on us fall;
We are dead, let no man harry or vex us dead,
But pray to God that He forgive us all.
The rain has washed and laundered us all five,
And the sun dried and blackened ; yea. perdie,
Ravens and pies with beaks that rend and rive
Have dug our eyes out, and plucked off for fee
Our beards and eyebrows ; never are we free,
Not once, to rest, but here and there still sped,
Drive at its wild will by the wind’s change led,
More pecked of birds than fruits on garden-wall;
Men, for God’s love, let no gibe here be said,
But pray to God that He forgive us all.
Prince Jesus, that of all art lord and head,
Keep us, that hell be not our bitter bed ;
We have nought to do in suoh a master’s hall.
Be not ye therefore of our fellowhead,
But pray to God that He forgive us all.
A. C. Swinburne.

THE EPITAPH
In form, of a Ballade, which Villon made for himself
and his companions when expecting to be hanged
■ with them.
Brothers who yet are living, mortal men,
Speak not of us with wrath and bitter tongue,
Since if your souls for us are filled with pain
The more will God’s grace fall your hearts among.
You see us here upon the gibbets hung:
The fl sh that we too much did glorify
Has longr been putrid and devoured : and dry
As dust and ashes now our bleached bones be.
Let no man I hen our hideous shapes decry,
But pray that God may show us all mercy.
Brottiers speak not, we pray you, with disdain
Of us poor five or six by law’ upstrung.
It is not every man who has his brain
Clear and well seated, as has oft been sung.
Make ye then intercession for our wrong
To Him whose death from Hell our souls did buy,
Saving us from the flames that never die,
That fresh may flow the fount of His pity.
We are dead: let none to vex our spirits try,
But pray that God may show us all mercy.
Our bodies have been washed and drenched by rain,
Dried up and blackened by the sun ; a throng
Of ravens and of crows our eyes have ta’en,
And pluckt the brows and beards whereto they clung.
Never are we at rest, forever swung
By every wind that shifts and passes by,
Pecked by the sharp beaks of the crow and pye
And dinted like a thimble, as you see.
Have naught to say to them that with us vie,
But pray that God may show us all mt rcy.
Prince Jesus, Lord who reignest in the s y,
Grant that to Hell’s fierce mouth we draw’not nigh:
Tow aid such a place no love or wish have we.
Men, mock i ot ns because we hang so high,
But pray that God may show us all mercy.
Theodore Wratislaw.
Francis Villon, ‘ student, poet, and house-
breaker,’ was born at Paris in 1431. The date of
his death is unknown ; but his life was doubtfully
as short as it was merry and miserable. In disso-
lute, reckless fashion, he let it run whither it
would, and occasionally it led him whither he
would not—if he could have helped himself. But,
having set the ball rolling gaily, he had no right
to whine when it rolled over him. He did whine—
a good deal; sometimes, maybe, with his tongue
in his cheek, but oftener earnestly enough, as he
had reason.
**♦-**
Not much is known of Villon, and for most of
that there is only his own word to take. It doesn’t
always look as reliable a word as it might be.
These things are fairly certain : at 17 he became a
student at Paris University, and at 21 he was
Master of such Arts as the University possessed,
To them Villon added many owed to his own
ingenuity. He had a native knack of verses, and
just enough scholarship to sharpen it. When he
wasn’t writing verses he was studying women—as
closely as he could ; and the memory of a fearful
thrashing he got through one of his subjects lasted
him for life. Also, he knocked a priest on the
head with a big stone, and killed him ; he was
memberof an attractive gang of thieves, and helped
to rob a church ; and before he was thirty
he was as near hanged for his ill-deeds as
is consistent with not being hanged after all. And
all the time he was weaving his experiences into
verses and more verses. All his experiences—
with a wealth of detail when ladies are in question
which makes Robert Louis Stevenson blush for
him, and Swinburne substitute stars for words
when translations reach the crises,
Because Villon wasn’t hanged he was banished
—out of Paris; and he got into trouble in the
country just as in the city. A bishop of Orleans
put him in a big holer—deep, damp, and damnable
—and fed him with proper episcopal economy. If
Louis Eleventh had not begun to reign just then,
and set a few prisoners free to rejoice in his good
fortune, there would have been little left of
either Villon or his poetry. But they let down a
basket into the big hole, and up Villon came and
started to put his masterpiece on paper in consecu-
tive form—no doubt he had meditated or written
scraps of it before. And the rest is silence.
* * * *
Villon is easier read about than read. His
French is old French; his text, as beforemen-
tioned, is delightfully corrupt ; and he was such
a treasurehouse of thieves’ slang and brothel-slang
that even Frenchmen query him. But it is well
worth while puzzling him out of context and
glossary. He is splendidly alive—tingling with
vigor and hearty devil-may-carelessness; and his
scholarship comes to aid his experience in all
manner of flavoury quips. He is the ‘prince of
all ballade-makers’—nobody else has ever got such
force out of that .stiff form. And he has humor
and pathos, and that popular, come-home-to
everybody philosophy whose staple is eloquent
regret that wild oats don’t bloom perennially.
MillP. Dreyfus, of Melbourne, by the- inexact
summary of Villon’s work in her recently-pub-
lished ‘ Lectures on French Literature,’somewhat
helps the impression that much of her criticism
oomes less from original study than per the
primrose paths of Saint-Beuve and Nisard and
similar classical commentators. She says (p. 79)
that ‘ Irrespective of his ballads, this roystering
poet wrote two other pieces: ‘ The Little Testa-
ment,’ in 1456, which contained his farewell and
his legacies to his friends; and his ‘Great
Testament,’ composed in the maturity of
his powers and of his life,’ etc. Which
is obscure, at least. For the best of Villen’s
ballads are set in his ‘Grand Testament’ as
stones in a brooch, and merely change the form
of the verse to give more force or variety to a run-
ning argument. This Great Testament was
written or collected when Villon came out of the
big hole, and it enshrines all the best of him.
There is a previous Little Testament, written
before he went into the hole —less in quality as
well as quantity. Evidently, however the hole
hurt the man, it did a lot of good to the poet.
Our songsters and such nowadays often bury
themselves in solitary remoteness to beat the
music out of the reluctant flesh : like the wild
animals they are. when the huntress Muse has
wounded them they yearn to leave the tribe and
die alone. Thus, at the time of Hubert Crackan-
thorpe’s disappearance, his friends thought he had
gone into the wilderness to be preyed upon.
Couldn’t something modern be done with a big
hole? Even if the damp did kill a Nocent Non-
sense-spout or half-a-dozen ?
* * * *
To the Testaments great and small there comes a
codicil, and a very good codicil it is; there are
‘Poesies Diverses’; and there are a number of
‘Poesies Attributes’ to Villon—with more or less
justice or injustice. So Mme. Dreyfus is decidedly
obscure, at the least.
* * * *
A complete translation of Villon into English
is done by .John Payne, but is not (nor is the
original) in Sydney Public Library, though a de-
ceiving catalogue makes one think it is. The
translations on this page give some idea of Villon’s
merit, but none of the scope and plan of his work,
Villon’ s improprieties may be agreeably read of in
Stevenson’s ‘Men and Books.’ S. lingers lovingly
oyer his most improper ballad, and salves a here-
ditary Calvinistic qualm by dubbing him ‘the
sorriest figure on the rolls of fame,’
TO DEATH, OF HIS LADY.
®eath 1 of thee do I make my moan,
Who hadst my lady away from me,
Nor wilt assuage thine enmity
Till with her life thou hast mine own ;
For since that hour my strength has flown.
Ld 1 what wrong was her life to thee,
Death?
Two we were, and the heart was one ;
Which now being dead, dead I must be,
Or seem alive as lifelessly
A 8 in the choir the painted stone,
Death (
D. G. Rossetti
THE BALLAD OF DEAD LADIES.
Tell me now in what hidden way is
Lady Flora the lovely Roman ?
Where’s Hipparchia, and where is Thais,
Neither of them the fairer woman ?
Where is Echo, beheld of no min,
Only heard on river and mere—
She whose beauty was more than human ?
But where are the snows of yester-year ?
Where’s the learned nun,
For whose sake Abeillard, I ween,
Lost manhood and put priesthood on ?
(From Love he won such dule and teen 1)
And where, I pray you, is the Queen
Who willed that Buridan should steer
, Sewed in a sack’s mouth down the Seine ? .
But where are the snows of yester-year?
White Queen Blanche, like a queen of lilies,
With a voice like any mermaiden—
Bertha Broadfoot, Beatrice, Alice,
And Ermenu-arde the lady of Maine—
And that good Joan whom Englishmen
At Rouen doomed and burned her tin re, —
Mother of God, where are they then ? . . .
But where are the snows of yester-year?
Nay, never ask this week, fair lord,
Where t hey are gone, nor yet this year,
Except with this for an overword, —
But where are the snows of yester-year?
D. G. Rossetti.

[ FOR THE BULLETIN.
The boy went out from the ranges grim,
And the breath of the mountains went with him.
With a song in his heart and a smile on his face,
And a light in his eyes for a foremost place.
And the good green earth, and the salt sea spray,
And the soft blue skies, they were his that day.
And, like Eden, ringed with a golden fire—
Afar rose the Land of His Heart’s Desire.
The boy went down to the city’s strife,
And his face was lost in the surge of life.
But a Power that he did not understand
Had nerved his brain and his fighting hand.
And he strove and failed, and he rose and won—
And he failed again ere the fight was done.
But he battled on when the days were dire
To win to the Land of His Heart’s Desire.
And there, in the heart of the stress and din,
’Mid want and labor and wealth and sin,
The strong man struggled with shining eyes,
And forced a passage, and grasped the prize.
And he cried to the Power who had lent the fire :
“ Lo ! Fame is the Land of My Heart’s Desire !
“ Give the cup to me with a beaded brim.”
And the Power that he knew not gave it him.
But the air is keen on the Cliffs of Fame,
And the shafts that fly have a deadly aim !
With a foothold scarce, and a sleepless dread
For the gulfs below and the heights o’erhead,
He cried to the Power who had steeled his hand :
“ I am outcast yet from my Fairyland !
For Fame is a land where no strength may tire,
But Love is the Land of My Heart’s Desire ! ”
Then there came to the man all his dream of
Love,
With the brow of snow and the eyes of a dove,
With the glint of the sun on her wavy hair,
And her soul as pure as her face was fair.
Like a living lily to him she came,
Till his eyes were wet and his soul was flame,
And she called to him, with an outstretched hand,
And they entered into the Promised Land.
But there came a day when he asked his soul,
“ Is this the land, and is this the goal ? ”
In his heart there lay what his lips denied—
The pang of a hunger unsatisfied.
“For Fame,” he said, “ and for Love I wrought :
They are not the things that I should have sought.
’Tis to boundless power that my dreams aspire—
And Wealth is the Land of my Heart’s desire ! ”
Then the Power that he did not understand
Gave him ships and houses and gold and land,
And the man’s power grew with each passing
year;
But his thoughts were vexed with a sleepless fear,
And his hair grew gray with the iron strain
Of the dread of loss and the lust of gain,
And he bowed his head on his hands and said :
“ All things are mine, but my heart is dead ! ”
And he thought of the boy from the ranges grim
With the breath of the mountains over him,
With a song in his heart and a.smile on his face,
And a light in his eyes for a toremost -place.
And the good, green earth and the salt sea spray,
And the soft blue skies that were his that day,
When, like Eden, ringed with a golden tire,
Afar rose the Land of his Heart’s Desire.
Then clear on his startled ear there fell
A voice like the sound of a silver bell: —
“ To each is the work that he best can do,
But you turned from the work when' it called to
you.
And you sought instead for the vulgar praise,
For the lips of love and for prosperous days.
And with all that the world can give you here,
You have lost the thing that you hold most dear.
For who hears the word that the Gods inspire—
In his work finds the Land of His Heart’s Desire.”
GEO. ESSEX EVANS.
A certain trustee of a Melb. cemetery pro-
posed, the other night, that the sleeping-ground be
lit up with fairy lights and open to the sorrowing
public on Jubilee night. He thought the effect
would be just splendid, and talked about the soft
light, &c., on the white advertisements, and
wound up a long harangue by sajfifrg—“ think
how happy our relations and friends would be if
they could get up and see it !” This genius only
got beaten by 14 votes for Parliament last year.

inform intending patentees ‘that on account of his
satisfactory arrangements with leading patent
agents in all the Australasian provinces, the prin-
cipal countries of Europe, America, and South
Africa, he is in a position to take out patents,
register trade-marks, companies and copyrights,

expert patent agents, and which will be within the

of cost of drawings, he will obtain a Certificate of

On one of the Darling harbor (Sydney)
wharves the other day the writer noticed an alto-
gether surprising amount of exertion on the part
of a number of men engaged in unloading a vessel.
Enquiry elicited this:— t( See that first fellow)
he’s first every time ; he’s what is called a pacer ;
he gets Is. 3d. an hour, and the others Is. ; if they
fail to make as many journeys (keeping at his
heels) as he does, well—they quit at once, and
make room for fresh triers. It’s the only way to
*et work out of them.”
Insect plagues in the North have evoked
umsiderable ingenuity. At Cairns the multitu-
linous grasshopper was thus exterminated: A
hece of calico 15ft. by 3ft. was nailed to a wooden
frame and saturated with kerosene. Two boys
then held the frame in a slanting position and
valked slowly over the hopper country. The
usects, of course, rose, and, on coming in contact
with the kerosene, died instantly. Acres thus
C 1 ired at a cost of a few shillings.

you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every

to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with A us-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission

taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-

paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions ivill be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, JUNE 5, 1897.
Direct Taxation.
When the land and income tax policy was
introduced in New South Wales, it was
generally hailed as a great democratic
triumph. It certainly was a democratic
triumph of a sort, but that is about all that
can be said for it. So far as could be con-
trived, all semblance of democratic principle
was left out of the new order of things,
and it is as nearly as possible a money-
grinding, revenue-making device, pure and
simple. There is no special Liberalism about
direct taxation, simply because it is direct,
neither is there any special Toryism or re-
trogressive tendency about indirect taxation
simply because it is indirect. It is quite
possible for the latter to be so arranged as to
be mainly a tax on wealth and to be based
on the most democratic principles, and for
the former to be mainly a tax on poverty and
to be as Tory as the most aggressive Fat
Man could desire. The two words, "direct
taxation,” imply no more in themselves than
any other two words—say "whale oil” or
" galvanised iron.” The result of too much
trusting to mere terms of speech is that, of
the five provinces which have adopted direct
taxation, New South Wales has got it in the
most utterly illiberal and stupid and hope-
lessly Tory form, and its principal effect has
been to shift a burden off one very wealthy
and useless class, the Sydney importers, and
shift it on to a larger and, in some degree, a
less affluent section of the community.
* * ♦ 9
There are five provinces—New South
Wales, Victoria, South Australia, Tasmania,
and Maoriland —in which income and land
taxes are collected. Of all these five New
South Wales is the only one where, apart
from the exemption of £2OO, large and small
incomes are taxed alike, and where the pre-
carious income that is derived from personal
exertion, and liable to stop short at any
moment when the brain or hand of the
worker gives out, is charged at the same rate
as the permanent income derived from in-
vestments, which goes on just the same
whether the owner lives or dies. Also New
South Wales, unlike Maoriland and South
Australia, makes no difference, apart from
the exemption, between the large landowner
and the small one, and it comes down on the
stupendous land-grabber and the struggling
farmer, on the citizen and the absentee syn-
dicate, in almost exactly the same propor-
tions. As a mere revenue-raising scheme,
to enable Premier Reid to dispense with
Customs duties and placate his friends, the
importers and aggregate Calico Jimmyism of
Sydney, this is good enough. As a means of
“shifting the burden on to the rich ” it is
seriously deficient.
In Victoria, South Australia, Tasmania,
and Maoriland the income-tax is built on
strictly democratic lines. Victoria starts
with an exemption of £2OO a year. After
that the first £lOOO of each income derived
from personal exertion is taxed at the
rate of 4d. in the £ ; the second £lOOO
is taxed 6d., and anything beyond that Bd.
in the £. But the rates levied on incomes
derived from property are exactly double
these figures ; the man who has a revenue of
£6200 a year from investments pays at the
rate of Bd. in the £ on £lOOO ; Is. in the £
on £lOOO, and Is. 4d. in the £ on £4OOO.
South Australia allows an exemption of
£l2O, but only on incomes not exceeding
£3OO. Its rates are in the £ up to £BOO,
and 6d. in the £ beyond that for incomes
derived from personal exertion, and double
these rates on incomes derived from pro-
perty. Tasmania has practically the same
system as regards exemptions as South Aus-
tralia, and it also charges extra rates upon
incomes derived from property. Maoriland
grants an exemption of £3OO to the resident
but no exemption at all to the absentee ; and
whereas the tax upon a private citizen’s in-
come of £lOOO is only 6d. in the £, on any-
thing over that the rate rises to Is. Com-
panies and syndicates, however, pay Is. in
the £ all through, consequently a resident
with an income of £lOOO a year pays £ 17 10s.,
and a foreign co. with the same income pays
£5O. New South Wales alone makes no dis-
tinctions between the very rich man and the
man of moderate means. It charges the
same rate to the man who has no accumula-
tions, but makes his £6OO a year by hard
toil, and whose whole working capital
vanishes when a brick falls from an un-
finished building on his head, and to the
non-producer whose t'6oo a year represents
£20,000 invested in State securities. Apart
from the exemption, it places the man with
£lOOO a year and the man with £lO,OOO on
exactly the same level, and it treats the
absentee just as well as the man who spends
his income in the country from which it is
derived. As a democratic policy this kind
of income-tax is a very abject failure.
* * * *
In South Australia and Maoriland the
land-tax is graduated according to the value
of each estate, and in both these provinces
the absentee pays a much heavier tax than
the resident proprietor. In Maoriland the
small landholder is exempt up to £5OO, while
the large holder has no exemption whatever.
Also, the rates vary from Id. in the £ on
small estates, up to 3d. in the £ on the very
largest, and on top of even this the absentee
pays 20 per cent, extra taxation. Large
special exemptions are allowed where a
small estate is the sole means of support
of a proprietor who is incapacitated by old
age or sickness. New South Wales allows
an exemption up to £240. Beyond that it
charges the absentee and the resident pro-
prietor, the man with £lOOO worth of land
and the man with £lOO,OOO worth, on
exactly the same basis, and its land-ta* is,
therefore, about as hopelessly undemocratic
a thing as could have been invented. It
does nothing to discourage the obnoxious
race of absentee landlords who merely
derive their income from New* South Wales
and spend the proceeds in London or else-
where ; therefore, absenteeism is worse in
New South Wales than in any other province.
When the absentee gets exactly the same
treatment as the man who spends his income
in the country, and who contributes to the
local liquor and tobacco and postal and railway
revenue, and who, by spending his revenue
locally, furnishes employment for the native
inhabitant, then New South Wales is as
good a place to be away from as any
other place. The cornstalk system doesn’t
even profess to discourage the accumula-
tion of huge estates, and, as a natural
consequence, 50 per cent, of tiie privately-
owned land in New South Wales is held in
areas of over 10,000 acres, the average of the
679 landowners who own half the private
land of the province among them being
30,819 acres apiece. Another 4482 people
own an average of 2640 acres apiece
The very large estates are steadily increasing
in number, and are swallowing up the coun-
try three times as fast as the small ones, and,
unless some special legislation is adopted to
meet the case, there seems to be no reason
why they should not continue doing so as
long as any land remains available.
* * * *
If the income-tax is to be a live measure
for shifting the burden of taxation upon the
rich, it needs to be so altered as to dis-
tinguish between the eminently precarious
income acquired by toil and the much less
precarious one derived from investments,
and to penalise the very wealthy, and
especially the wealthy absentee, more than
the people of only moderate means. And, if
the land-tax is to be anything better than a
scheme for grinding out revenue, it requires
to be put on an entirely new basis. In the
first place, the exemption should be increased
to at least £3OO ; nowadays, in many parts
of New South Wales, the agriculturist
with a good deal more than £240 worth
of land is often a miserably poor indi-
vidual, who has to eke out his precarious
existence by road-mending and shearing, and
tank-sinking at sweated wages, and other
casual foil. In this land of low prices, long
distances, free imports, bad roads, drought,
rabbits, and other curses, the idea of treat-
ing any man with more than £240 worth of
arable land—as the Government’s valuations
go —as a bloated person who requires special
taxation to keep him in subjection is
rather grotesque. Further, the tax should
steadily increase as the property grows
larger, so that the holding of estates of
very high value in the hands of a single
individual would become unprofitable ; the
holder of agricultural land who lets his pro-
perty should be more heavily taxed than
the one who cultivates it himself, in order to
discourage the formation of a new squirearchy
and the corresponding class which British
fiction describes as the "loyal tenantry ” ; and
the absentee—not the Australian of the other
provinces but the European variety—should
be taxed over and above all others, as an
individual whose extinction is a matter of
urgency.
• • » *
The present system of direct taxation in
New South Wales is no more democratic than
the semi-protective, semi-revenue-tariff sys-
tem which immediately proceeded it. Even
assuming that Protective duties do fall on
the consumer, which is eminently uncertain,
the former system taxed the rich and the
poor equally on some points, while on many
others it taxed the rich only ; very many of
the productive duties in the late tariff were
distinctly duties on luxuries with which
the poor are almost wholly unacquainted.
The present system exempts some of the
poor—those of the towns—and falls heavily
on a class in the country, many members of
which are now very indigent indeed. It was
passed by a curious alliance of conflicting
elements. The Labor-party supported it
because it had somehow come to be under-
stood that direct taxation must be a demo-
cratic idea, and that any kind of direct
taxation, however bad, must be better than
any kind of indirect taxation, however good.
The Sydney importing interests were, on the
whole, favorably inclined for three reasons :
The land-tax touches them very little ; as a
rule they are not land-holders on any scale
worth mentioning, so this impost was some-
body else’s concern. The income-tax is
merely a tax of 2J per cent, on their profits,
and as the protective duties were a much
heavier tax on their gross takings, the bar-
gain was an excellentonefor the Great Shoddy
Interest. Also, the absentee-tax touched
them not at all, for there was none ; and as a
very large proportion of importers are merely
representatives of foreign firms and com-
panies this was at least a negative virtue.
In other words, the importers had to choose
between a series of imposts which taxed
them specially and heavily as a wealthy and
objectionable class, and two series, one of
which hardly touched them at all, while
the other only fell on them in common
with members of every other branch of
industry. Naturally, they chose direct
taxation as far the less of two evils.
Through want of attention to detail, and a
weak belief in mere names and shibboleths,
it has come to pass that the reform which
was to shift the burden oft’ the poor and on
to the rich really shifted most of it off the
richest class in New South Wales, and re-
distributed it, partly among another class of
rich men, partly among the middle class,
partly among a class which is very poor
indeed. The Reid variety of democratic
taxation is mainly a sop to the wealthy class
which supports Reid ; otherwise, it is no-
thing in particular.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A “Genuine Antique"
1 he suggestion that ministers of religion might
with propriety, and even profit, countenance
such dramatic entertainments as “ The Sign of
the Cross ’' has brought blinking into the light
of day a tine specimen of Lhe true evangeli-
cal hog born 600 years behind his time.
The play cannot, in the first place, be of
any value as a moral stimulant, contends
this monumental prig, so long as its pri-
mary object is, not so much to teach or
preach anything, as to put coin in the pockets
of its producers. Which argument cuts beauti-
fully both ways, since - as the ecclesiastical ex-
pounder is no more disposed toexercilale gratis
than the dramatic one—the lessons of the pulpit
cannot, upon this showing, be of any greater
value than those of the stage. But, putting
aside this particular play, the singe as a whole
we are gravely assured, “ was never designed
to suggest or stimulate devotion to Gun
which, if it means anything, means that
whatever is not so specially designed is
an accursed thing, to be ‘ eschewed by
all true believers as rigorously as this
surpassing snufHer—to be consistent—would
himself have to eschew a picnic or a
picture-gallery ; a game of chess, a gallop in
the Centennial, a dip in the briny, or anything
v ' hatever that is iuiquitously conducive to the
mere relaxation and delectation of man. “ There
is always,” it is admitted with much groaning
“a certain percentage of church-goers who be
lieve in the theatre,” but these, it is com-'
placently inferred, will in all probability be
effectually damned “because they are out of
sympathy with evangelical religion.” “The
Christian who every day studies the Scriptures,
prays fervently, and undergoes careful self-
examination is one whom the theatre would
make sick ” ; but he is also one who would pro-
bably make the Founder of his alleged religion
sick ! The line of cleavage between the Chris-
tians who go to the theatre and those who don’t
is simply, it is not very obscurely implied, the
same old line between the sheep and the goats
—though theatre-goers as a whole, it is casu-
ally observed, would in any case be assuredly
booked for Tupliel, because the stage is con-
stantly ridiculing and caricaturing religion. And
in tins weary oh! misuse of the word “ re-
ligion ” lies the crux of the whole question.
Religion—as distinct from the sacerdotal super-
fluities, the mechanical pietism, the humbug
and hypocrisy and imposture generally, which
have crystallised round the term —is a thing
at which few' intelligent men care to sneer;
whereas, so long as the world is convinced in
its inmost heart that any one man is just as
sacred as any other, it will continue to scoti
at the priest, and pillory the pietists, and hold
up to wholesome contempt the holy humbug
of every sort and size.
The Vague Parson Person.
Pakson Edgak, almost the only witness before
the Vic. Old Age Pension Commission who had
a definite scheme to offer, suggests raising the
needful funds by a tax of 1 percent, per annum
on the value of lands used for racing purposes,
a tax of 10s. per head on every horse entered at
a race meeting, Id. on every person attending a
football or cricket match, os. on every dog
entered at a coursing match, Id. on every
theatre or concert-ticket issued, by a gun tax,
by the profits of a State pawnbroking business,
by a special tax on land, and by using for the
purposes of the pension fund a part of the pro-
ceeds of the taxes on liquor and tobacco. One
feature of the case is that some of Parson
Edgar’s taxes would yield so little as to barely
pay for collection, and the tax on people attend-
ing cricket and football matches would, in parti-
cular, be very hard to collect at all in many
cases. And, like everybody else, Edgar shirks
the problem ; about half of his idea is that ihe
State should give up a portion of its revenue—
the liquor and tobacco revenue—and that the
Treasurer should be left to find new sources of
income to fill the gap. A man whose idea is
simply that some one else should find the need-
ful ideas doesn’t throw much light on any sub-
ject. The question at issue is this : A really
efficient Old Age Pension scheme would cost,
in Victoria or N.S.W., somewhere between
.£500,000 and £1,000,000 a year, so far as can
be guessed at present, and this amount must
all be raised by new devices. The Treasury
can’t make ends meet even now, and the
proposal that it should provide part of
the pension-fund out of its present resources
is blank insanity ; instead of being in a
pos.tion to give up part of its drink
and tobacco revenue it wants a great deal
more drink and tobacco and other revenue for
its own purposes. The tote might be made to
supply a part of the needful funds. A good
deal more might be raised by the state being
its own “ Tattersall ” ; and taxes on foreign
insurance-offices, absentees, bookmakers, and
patent-medicines might possibly make up the
rest of the sum needful. Rut the pious element
of the community, while admitting the desira-
bility of a pension system, repudiates with
horror the idea of raising the necessary funds
by totes and lotteries, and it never by any
chance suggests a means of raising the money
without them. Its one great idea is to shunt
three-fourths of the responsibility on the state
Treasurer, which isn’t solving the difficulty,
but merely passing it on to a person who is
already about the most tired individual in the
country.
The Bushy Park Scandal.
1 hat clumsy, Highland person, Minister
M‘Kknzie —the man who aimed the pickle-
bottle at Fish —is fast making trouble for the
M.L. Government. The story of the present
stench is briefly as follow's : The Bushy Park
estate was one of the assets of the Bank of New
Zealand. It was transferred, at one of the
many reconstructions, to the B.N.Z. Assets
Co. A board—of which Premier i Seddon is a
member—was appointed to release these assets,
and it realised on Bushy Park by selling it to
the sons of Seddon’s colleague, the M'Kenzie
aforesaid. The price at which it was sold is
unknown, so are the terms of payment, and
Minister M'Kenzie, when interrogated on the
subject, merely w’aves two large hairy fists in
the air and refuses any definite information.
At one public meeting lie went so far as to
oder to show the title deeds to any person who
wanted to see them, but when Hutchinson,
M.P., promptly accepted the offer, the large
M'Kenzie crawled out and the deeds were not
forthcoming. Rumor has it that the M'Kenzie
family got the property for £5OOO less than
another purchaser was willing to pay ; also,
that whereas the other purchaser offered cash
the M'Kenzies got Bushy Park on credit—that
they didn’t even pay a deposit. Another theory
is that they paid for the stock and improve-
ments, and the land was generously thrown in.
Anyhow, there is a fine, large stench in pro-
gress, and opinions are divided as to whether
M'Kenzie is a particularly clumsy public ass,
who manages to arouse suspicion against him-
self by sheer clumsiness and obstinacy, or
whether lie is helping to conceal a very fishy
transaction which is next-door to a fraud. Also
the fat man is rising all over the seven provinces
to say that this is a conclusive evidence of the
evils of a State Bank—which is putting the
matter exactly upside-down. The M'Kenzie
family stench is solid evidence of the advan-
tages of State banking. The same kind of
tiling, as is alleged truly or untruly in the Bushy
1 ark case, happens in the great majority of pri-
' ate banks, but when the managers and directors
help themselves to the funds of a private bank
the fact—as is shown by the history of almost
every private bank which has gone under in
Australia—never conies to light until it is too
late. It only transpires after the burst —never
earlier. But in the case of the State institution
the M'Kenzie family’s little transaction was
hardly put on paper and signed and sealed and
blotted, before half the country rose to ask


father M'Kkszik for an explanation; and
unless that hairy Caledonian offers a better
one than he has presented up to date the
Government to which he belongs will get a
shock from which it won’t readily recover. The
charge levelled against him—mostly by the
Opposition papers—is one of plain, vulgar
fraud, and until an adequate explanation is
forthcoming people will keep on calling it a
vulgar fraud indefinitely. The Ministerial
organs mostly keep silence, though one of them
advances the suggestion that MKenzie has a
perfectly good explanation to offer but is too
bitterly obstinate to offer it. Altogether it is
a delicate situation, and the Minister—vvho is
mainly a man of brawn and stoush —hasn't the
diplomacy that the occasion requires.
The Priestly Taboo.
The “touchiness” of Roman Catholicism re-
veals itself absurdly in connection with a Mel-
bourne amateur performance of a feeble comic
opera— “ Lelamine,” to wit. The local (R.C.)
Advocate has “no hesitation in saying that
• Lelamine’ is a vile, demoralising production,
a slander on the Catholic priesthood, and an
insult to Catholics generally. ... We could
wish that there were no Catholics engaged in
‘ Lelamine,’ but that shame we are spared.”
To the average Protestant, who was unable to
discover the drift of the poor little opera afore-
said, these ravings appear pitiable. They are
easily explained, however. Among the more
or less inaudible mummers in “ Lelamine ” was
one who played a priest, and the priest was
dimly understood to be an uncelibate soit of
person, addicted to visiting a convent by a
private subway, or something to that effect.
In short, “Lelamine” was an “insult to
Catholics” because it put forward, iu a farci-
cally exaggerated form, the suggestion that
a Catholic priest may be quite as giddy
as a Protestant parson who “carries on
with the sanctimonious girl adherents of his
little church or chapel. And because one
grotesque priest was here supposed to do
exactly as hundreds of parsons have been con-
victed of doing, atone timeor other, official Roman
Catholicism gets its holy hair off. The “ Lela-
mine” notice for a Melb. weekly paper was
written by an Irish-blooded representative of
the ould faith, and the protest of this indig-
nant critic sent the Catholic bawl rolling.
Archbishop Carr, upon being appealed to,
thought it “ regrettable that the plot of any
public opera should be based on incidents
which had no foundation in fact , and which
appeared to rely for their success on the anti-
cipated prejudices of certain of the audience
rather than on the merits of a plot.”
The celibate Archbishop took an even
more foolish view of a trivial, inci-
dent than his reverent “ son,” the
Milesian pressman, had taken in the first in-
stance. His Grace hinted that the giddy priest
on the stage was a dirthy heretical device for
bringing an immaculate priesthood into ignor-
ant contempt. Such utterances put the all-
round unbeliever out of patience. An outsider,
honestly convinced that all religious denomina-
tions claim too much holiness for their spiritual
professors, naturally objects to being asked to
swallow the R.C. claims without a grain
or two of salt. Numerous stage satires on
Protestant parson cant are founded on fact,
and laughed at by Protestants accordingly. For
instance, Dr. Cantwell and Mawworm in
“The Hypocrite.” But the R.C. priest and
the R.C. boarding-school for girls, or convent
—they must be held absolutely sacred. Why
in the name of scandalous revelations, ancient
and modern !—why is the priestly denial of
human nature to escape ridicule ?
His Brother's Keeper.
Why is it that the professional philanthropist,
when he isn’t a self-advertising humbug, is so
often au unspeakable ass ? It’s only too likely
that, when the old-age pension question comes
under formal investigation in N.S.W., the local
“ charity organisers ” will be to the fore with
much such “ expert evidence ” as that which
set forth to the Victorian Royal Commission
that about 70 per cent, of the existing poverty
is due to strong drink, and that the old-
age pension system, by relieving men of
anxiety about the fag-end of existence, would
produce yet more tippling, and by consequence,
more pauperism. Which balderdash serves
only to show, in the first place, that no man
should be accepted as an “ expert” in indigence
who hasn’t himself undergone a severe attack
of empty pocket; and, in the second, the true
inwardness of alcoholism remains still a sealed
book to everybody who hasn’t been more or less
“through the mill” in person. The well-fed
dilettante humanitarian who hankers so hugely
(upon paper) after the rehabilitation of his
broken-down brother is too much accustomed
to take his opinions cut-and-dried to get a
glimmer of the facts that the drink-
habit is just as often the effect of misery
as the cause of it, and that to assure a
man’s future is often the very best way to
sti'aighten-up his present. The most im-
practicable drunkard is the despair-drunkard ;
tlie most utterly reckless spendthrift is, para-
doxically, he who has little to spend and less to
hope for. And, in any case, if it is true that
prospective provision conduces to present im-
providence, the Great Gospel of Thrift is but
sounding brass and a tinkling cymbal, and the
“ expert witness,” himself—for the most part a
wearisome apostle of that directly anti-Chris-
tian cult—should proceed forthwith to scatter
to the winds his treasure upon earth, lest
by the knowledge that he has treasure, and
that his future is thereby assured, he too
should be seduced into the broad road that
leadeth unto booze and begging and general
burst-up. Ou which melancholy highway,
before he should have travelled far, he’d have
discovered that he hadn’t even begun to realise
the intricacy of the problem about which lie
and his kind are always so complacently ready
with the rashest of opinions.

Hot Indebted!
Fd. Bulletin,— The approach of the “ diamond
jubilee” junketings has tilled the air with such
mouthings about “loyalty,” “fealty, alle-
giance,” ami so forth, that a plain word upon
the subject seems badly wanted. To be loyal,
broadly speaking, is to think and act towards
another as that other has a right—real or
imaginary—to expect ; and upon the validity or
otherwise of this right turns simply and
directly I he distinction between the loyalty a
man owes and the loyalty he doesn’t owe. To
one’s friend there is a personally-assumed duty
to be true, as there is a congenital obligation to
be true to one’s family, and to that larger family
which is the state. But loyalty to the state—
to the best interests of the community—so
far from involving “ attachment ” to any
“ throne and person,” is actually incompatible
therewith ; and, exactly in proportion as a man
loses sight of the national idea in the personal,
he is apt to mistake the most contemptible of
feelings for the noblest, and to fancy himself
an ardent patriot when he is no more than an
enthusiastic Hunkey. There have, indeed, been
individuals—imperial, royal, and what-not—
w horn the most ardent democrat could admire
without sacrificing the tenth part of a prin-
ciple. The late Emperor of Germany, for in-
stance—to stick to our own times—was such a
Man at all points that his paternity of the
regnant mountebank offers a curious study in
heredity ; and even sanctimonious old swash-
buckler William was not altogether devoid, in
his pipeclay dignity, of that heroic interest
which cannot by the wildest stretch of imagina-
tion be extended to the dismally stolid and
commonplace G uelphs. And there is, of course,
no necessity to revile, on account of positions
they do not adorn, persons who happen to have
been thereunto fated :
Spurn not the “ nobly born,”
Nor treat with virtuous scorn
The “well-connected.”
They boast an equal claim
With him of humble name
To be respected 1
Vi< TORIA-by-the-Grace is as much entitled to
t he ordinary courtesies of life as any other re-
spectable old lady, and it is sheer futility to
blackguard Ti mmy Wales qua Wales and not
qua Ti mmy. People who prance round after
this fashion themselves generally possess a
latent vein of snobbishness. The really
self respecting man will accommodate him-
self, within certain limits, to things as
they are, and won’t imagine for a moment
that by blatant asininity they can be any the
sourer made what he wants them to be. But
it is precisely this common-sense part of him
which renders him, on the other hand, totally
unable to comprehend how any presumably
reasoning being can have the faintest glimmer
of fervor concerning perhaps the most utterly
uninteresting personality that ever sat upon a
throne. And, foolish as it is to go into public
hysterics over the fact that Victoria R.I. has
had her august hand rather longer in the
national cash-box than any of her predecessors,
it is foolisher still to raise the cheap howl of
“ disloyally ! ” against any man simply because
he can find nothing to go off his bead about in
a stout little old lady with an uncertain temper
and an uncommonly hard grip of the bawbees.
—Sardonyx.
The rent of Melbourne’s most flourishing pub.
(You no and Jackson’s) was lately raised to the
extent of £I(JOU per annum by its absentee
owners. The local agents for the property
reckoned that an extra £5OO per annum on the
new lease would be a fair thing, but the Hing-
lish ladies (sisters) who control the situation
stood out for four figures, and got them. Cir-
cumstances over which the original purchaser
of “ Young and Jackson’s corner” exercised
no control whatever have made the situation
valuable for any business purpose, and especially
valuable for the licensed sale of liquor. A
license in this ease practically represents a large
annual Government grant to the leaseholders,
of which grant a large percentage leaves Vic-
toria every half-year in the form of unearned
increment for an English family that contributes
nothing towards the Vic. Government s support.
The political aspect of the Young and Jackson
case is worth considering. Socialism might
justly demand that hotel licenses shall be re-
stricted to premises whose owners reside
within the province. Every trader works
for the benefit of his landlord—sometimes
himself, another fellow generally—and a pros-
perous business means an increasing rent \ alue,
hut a licensed victualler differs from other
traders in that he works for a landlord who
enjoys a measure of State protection. In
Sweden the State is the only licensed vic-
tualler, and every pub. belongs to the Govern-
ment. In Australia this desirable consumma-
tion is hoped for by all good democrats and
friends of the public stomach. Meanwhile,
private landlords reap the first fruits of the
liquor-trade monopoly, therefore an Act com-
pelling the personal attendance of all landlords,
and part-landlords, at the court on licensing
dav, would seem not unreasonable. It would
at'least prevent the English ladies aforesaid
from being perpetual absentees. In fact, such
an Act would make the lot of an absentee pub-
owner too irksomely travelsome for endurance,
and the smaller the lot of absentees that this
country has to endure the better for our
Federal constitution—when it arrives.
Parson Isitt, the flaming Maorilaud teetotaller,
is now on the loose in Australia, and holding
forth in his wonted combustible language about
the Accursed Derrink, and the need for its
total abolition. The Bulletin doesn’t really care
much whether the Derrink is abolished or not ;
all the thirst it has got can he quenched 111
other ways. But it respectfully asks brotliei
Isitt for the several-dozenth time, how he is
going to make up the £2,500,000 revenue which
will be sacrificed when his policy is adopted
throughout Australasia ? It has asked this
question of many oilier shrieksome teetotallers,
and not one has supplied an answer. Lhe
revenue can’t be dispensed w ith. It is no good
saying that the Lord will provide ; it is neces-
sary to indicate how He will provide so that
the Treasurers will know where they are. It
is no good saying that the increased affluence
of the people will make it easy to fill the gap ;
what the public wants to know is how' a sum-
cient amount of that increased affluence can be
extracted from the people—whether by an in-
come-tax of ss. in the £, or otherwise—to nil
the gap. It doesn’t want to be shunted off by
vague x’emarks about the alleged savings
that will be effected in police and gaol ex-
penditure ; if all the gaol and police
expenditure could be saved by the aboli-
tion of drink it wouldn’t make up, or
nearly make up, for the loss of revenue. If
the typical teetotal lecturer would saw the air
less and come down to business-like particulars
a little more, it would do the cause a great deal
of good. For instance, one province (S. A.) has
a teetotal Treasurer, and in all probability he
would be glad to come down on the Derrink
with all his might if the wild-eyed Isitt would
only show how it can be done without reducing
the province to immediate insolvency. If Isitt
proposes simply that liquor should be extin-
guished, and that as a consequence the interest
on the public debt be unpaid and the country
go in for repudiation straight-out, why doesn t
he say so, and take the responsibility like a
man ?
Dear Bulletin,— Mr. Ashton remarks :
Or, take a Colored Races Restriction Bill. Victoria and
N.S.W. might vote solidly in favor of stringent restric-
tion, and Tasmania, S.A., and W.A. against. In the
Lower House voting would be 82 to 25 in favor of the
restriction ; in the Upper House, 24 to 16 against. And
the policy of Australia with regard to colored races would
be determined by an insignificant fraction of the people,
&c., &c.
Singularly enough, this is precisely what did
occur in the United States under the system of
equal state representation in the Senate. The
Senate, chosen on this vicious principle, was
directly responsible for the Civil War. The
House of Representatives showed itself reason-
ably democratic, and did its utmost to stem the
approaching calamity. During the 29th Con-
gress the Lower House passed an Act admitting
the territory acquired from Mexico to the Union
on condition that slavery should exist
therein. This Act being passed on to the Senate,
the latter did not even vote on it, and it fell
through. During the 30th Congress a Bill pro-
viding territorial government for California
and prohibiting slavery therein was also
passed bv the House of Representatives.
The Bill having been sent to the Senate, that
body refused even to consider it, and it re-
mained dead on the table. On the introduction
of the famous Oregon Bill prohibiting slavery
in that territory the champions of slavery in
the House of Representatives agreed to support
the Bill if a clause were added having for its
object the obtainment from the House of a
recognition of the right of slavery to extend it-
self in -all territory below 36’30 north. The
House, however, rejected the amendment and
passed a no-slavery bill by a majority of 99.
The Senate, in the last day of its session,
simply laid this Bill on the table from which it
never rose. The Senate finally devoted all
Mexican conquered territory (except California)
to slavery by the following trick. In order to
make the measure pass the Lower House ten
million in a stock bearing 5 per cent, was to be
paid the State of Texas out of the U.S.
Treasury. As the Representatives held many
bonds they would hesitate to defeat the Bill.
It was, however, first defeated by a majority of
eight Representatives, and finally passed by 11
votes. Suddenly Texas bonds rose 85 per cent.,
and members became rich. Pretty reading,
isn’t it ? And so much for equal state-repre-
sentation. —P. Luftig.

As a sample of calm, unblushing perjury or
misrepresentation or Ananias or something of
the sort, the following, from a recent S.M.
Herai d leading article, has a lot to recom-
mend it: —
The volume of the “ Statistical Register,” just issued,
for 1895, shows that the manufacturing works in N.S.
Wales in that year employed 1132 more hands than in
1894, and 4965 more than in 1893 under the condition of
Protection. If this increase is not as large as might be
desired, it is to be noted, first, that it is widely different
from the wholesale closing up of works that was to take
place under Freetrade ; and, secondly, that it goes on
simultaneously with an immense expansion of country
production, by which much labor has been drawn off
from the town. These indications prepare us for trade
results strongly at variance with the dirges of dead indus-
tries and perishing trade which so largely enter into the
dreary political litany of the Protectionist party.
It sounds very beautiful, of course, to hear that
in 1895, under Freetrade, N.S.W. industries
employed 4965 more hands than they did in
1893 under Protection. It also sounds very
convincing, and the people who take their facts
from the Respectable paper at the foot of
Hunter-street, Sydney, are much impressed
thereby. Where the magnificent effrontery of
the matter comes in is that 1895 was not a
Freetrade year. It was as much a Protectionist
year as ’93, for the new tariff did not come into
operation until Ist Jan., 1896. 1 herefore, if
the increase of employment, as compared with
’93, was the result of the fiscal system in force,
as ti e Herald implies, it was one of the good
effects of Protection. The Herald, in its early
days, was dreary and veracious. Now it is
dreary and wretchedly ignorant, or else dreary
and utterly unscrupulous, and lost to that
decency which is supposed to keep politics from
degenerating into one part clumsy perjury and
one part Billingsgate.

The system of exacting subscriptions from the
local representative, which, in Australia,
threatens to defeat the political purpose of
“ payment of members,” is said to be driving
even' moderately fat men from the House of
Commons.
“Some time ago,” writes G. R. Sims, “ I was con-
versing with a member who had resigned on the plea ot
ill-health. He looked exceedingly well, and told me
frankly that the ill-health was merely an excuse. The
real reason of his retirement was that the demands made
upon him for money by various bodies in the borough he
represented had become so great that he was compelled
to give up the seat. . . . The system is a bad one and
should be at once reformed out of existence. It should
be made illegal for any candidate or sitting member to
contribute to any local club or charity, because so long as
he is allowed to iio so, so long will he be made to do so,
and the man with ‘ plenty money and no brains will
always in certain constituencies have a pull over the man
with ‘ plenty brains and no money.’ It is not at all a
pleasant reflection that a man’s fitness for Parliamentary
honors can only be decided by the amount he is willing to
subscribe to the local football and cricket-clubs and local
charities generally.”
If a mild Tory, of the Sims type, can take this
view of the situation in Great Britain (not
reckoning Ireland) where members get no
salary at all, and non-capitalists have not more
than half-a-dozen English borough seats within
the possibility of capture, it is time for Aus-
tralian democracy to cry out against the black-
mailing of the men it professes to champion.
Unhappily, the blackmail system in this country
is rooted in Young Australia’s sportive char-
acter. Whereas a wealthy British M.P. is only
expected to promote the cause of muscular
Christianity with coin, his needy cousin out
here is called upon to give time as well as
money in the character of president to this,
that and the other club and society. The
“ popular representative” of the future will he
a mere whisky-sw'elling dilettante in the arts of
Saturday afternoon pastimes, unless Parliament
hurries up with a Members’ Protection Act, on
the lines suggested by the Cockney playwright.
“ Rus Roberts” to The Bulletin in re Gent
One :
The decision of the Supreme Court, that names of solici-
tors called upon to “ answer affidavits ” shall not be
published till the matter comes on for hearing on the
application to make the rule absolute, has caused con-
siderable adverse comment which is based on a misappre-
hension of the real state of affairs. A solicitor is called
upon to explain his conduct to the Judges solely because
he is an officer of the Court, which exercises over him a
peculiar jurisdiction—a jurisdiction to which no other
class of persons is subject. It is his conduct as an officer
of the Court only that is brought into question on such
an occasion, and, that being so, why should not the same
course be followed in his case as would be followed in,
say, the Civil Service ? If the Public Service Board receives
a complaint about an officer, does it publish that fact,
together with the charges, to the world at large before
holding an inquiry, and before the officer has an oppor-
tunity to answer for himself ? Why, then, should the
Supreme Court act in that manner with regard to
its officers—the solicitors? The only charge that can
be made against a solicitor before the Supreme Court
is one regarding his conduct as a solicit/ r, and the court
then decides w hether he, as an officer, should be punished
or not. When this point is understood it will be seen that,
save as above, the Supreme Court has nothing to do with
the conduct of its solicitors. They may be guilty of all
the crimes in the New gate calendar, but unless it is done
by him as a solicitor the Court will not, and it is sub-
mitted has no right to, take any notice of it until a con-
viction has been obtained against him. Hence the
Supreme Court does not imprison a solicitor for embezzle-
ment- it strikes him off the roll as unfit to be one of its
officers ; but a jury must say whether he has committed a
criminal offence. Not so long ago a solicitor was
charged before a jury and acquitted, yet he was
struck off the rolls; not because he was guilty
of the crime charged, but for “ conduct not
becoming an officer,” &c. Hence there is no analogy
between the case of a solicitor called on to “ answer
affidavits ” which no other member of the community can
be called upon to do, and the case of a person charged in
the police-court- a misfortune to which every person is
liable—solicitors included. The striking of a solicitor off
the rolls does not operate as a bar to future criminal or
civil proceedings, as one would imagine from so much
that is written on the subject. In conclusion, a solicitor
is liable to every punishment to which an ordinary citizen
is, and, in addition to that, he is subject to an extra-
ordinary jurisdiction of a court which can, without his
having been found guilty of a criminal offence, and with-
out a trial before a jury, not only punish him by fine, but
can ruin him utterly by taking away his very livelihood.
New South Wales Assembly recently called on
Meteorologist Russell to explain why the
general rain he expected this year had, so far,
failed to turn up. But no one dreamt of de-
manding a report front the bosses of the
churches as to why the rain they prayed for
failed to turn up, and if anyone had suggested
such a thing he would have been outwardly
howled down as an indecent blasphemer and
inwardly laughed at as a fool. Yet Russell
merely said that, going by what is
known of the very much undeveloped science of
meteorology, a science hardly 100 years old yet,
it was most probable the drought would
break up this year, and the year isn’t
half gone yet. The clerics say that their
science is 6000 years old, that it is fully
developed, and that answer to prayer is a cer-
tainty—not a matter of speculation; and
almost an immediate certainty at that, dhere-
fore if Parliament had ever taken the humilia-
tion and prayer business seriously it would
have asked for a report from the cocksure men
of theology—not from the avowedly-uncertain
man of science. Also, if “the GoD-fearing
Ministry ” believed in the churches it wouldn’t
keep Russell at all. One indignant individual
has even suggested that the province should
hire a new meteorologist—the idea plainly
being that it might possibly get one who
could foresee rain better than the pre-
sent official. But no one has suggested
getting a new bishop who could pray for
rain better than Smith, because nobody has
any hope at all of getting a bishop any where
who can bring dow'n rain better than Smith,
and as Smith can’t bring down rain at all that
means giving up the whole case. Nobody ex-
pected anything off Smith’s church. , Nobody
in this instance got anything oft Smith s church.
And no politician dreams of asking Smith or
his church for an explanation or a report,
because Smith's church panned out just as
everybody knew it would. So did every other
church.
These remarks of the N. Y. correspondent to
S.M. Herald should be remembered when the
N.S.W. Protectionists again attain power:
Theoretically, ad valorem duties are alone equitable ;
but, even with sworn invoices, the foreign exporter and
his agent or consignee hire clerks to do the false swearing
for them, and under the Wilson Bill Uncle Sam has been
defrauded of scores of millions of dollars by very con-
servative, sound-monev, respectable men- men, too, who,
except in this life-and-death matter of getting their goods
into a paying market, and keeping their hundreds or
thousands of work-people busy and happy, are the very
soul of honor. No one blames them much ; indeed, most
men who have a kindly sense of duty to those to whom
they stand as captains of industry would venture fully as
much or more against the letter of the Ten Command-
ments. Yet Uncle Sam’s duty is, of course, to enforce
obedience to the law': and the easiest w r ay to do this is to
decree a duty of so much a pound or yard, not so much
per cent., upon dutiable imports.
Even “ theoretically ” there is a great objection
to act cal or eta duties, besides the fraud and per-
jury engendered. Foreign-made articles are
formidable to home manufactures because of
their low' price, and the cheaper they are the
greater the need of Protection. Under the ad
valorem system, the protection diminishes
with the price—ni’ore especially when the
foreigner reduces rates and sells at a loss so as
to smash up the protected industry. Thus an
ad valorem tariff is least effective w hen most
w'anted for protective, not revenue, purposes.
In America, Greeley and others favored w hat
is called a “ minimum ” —that is an ad valorem
tax down to a certain price— beyond which all
articles, irrespective of invoices and declara-
tions, are taxed at the same rate. It must also
be remembered that it is in the low-priced
articles that a protective tariff, especially a
young country, should produce the most benefit.
