Farley’s second venture at Sydney Opera
House is the “ Bohemian Girl,” put up for a fort-
only. Probably that is about as much as the
Girl ” will stand nowadays, but she seems calcu-
lated to do payable business for that brief period.
Devilshoof is one of Farley’s best characters; as
the reckless, bass gipsy he is even more
convincing than in the gorgeous cavalier boots of
Don Jose. Miss May Glendinning’s Gipsy Queen
is an admirable piece of work, and Maud Lita fills
the part of Arline very satisfactorily. Percy
shannon makes a respectable Thaddeus ; there is
nothing brilliant or especially notable about
Thaddeus, but his eminent respectability hits the
audience like a brick; and a certain amount of
moderate tail brings up the rear of the pro-
cession. Still, the co. gets along very smoothly,
and » • moun ting is good, and the “ Bohemian
Girl is excellent value at the money.
** * *
The Brough Comedy Co. is further fare-
welling this week in the “Village Priest.” After
the tangled riot of the “ White Elephant,” be-
ginning nowhere and ending by a shattersome
collision against nothing, the fine old classic is
peace and rest and balm and beer, and everything
else that is commendable. It is a good many
years now since Titheradge first started to be a
dreamy celibate in the old French garden, and to
blow the caterpillars off the flowers, and he re-
mains just as good an advt. for the Holy Roman
church to this day. Titheradge, drowsing among
his blossoms, looks like a saint who had left his
halo hanging on the hat-rack when he came in,
and he suggests that the church he belongs to
must be a gentle, blameless institution that will
feed out of the hand. The old miracle, when a
leaf of the Bible turns over for no visible reason
and a ray of light comes down straight on the text
which settles the question, still goes well. Stage
miracles, as a rule, are none too convincing, but
this one is at least as good as the average. Brough
doesi his former tragic part of Jean Torquenie as
forcibly as of old ; and though he has done it so
often his excellence still comes on the public—
which never can quite realise that Brough is any-
thing but a very comic comedian—as a surprise.
The co. goes on farewelling in the “Village
Priest ” for the rest of this week, and on Saturday
it gets along a stage further with the painful duty
of tearing itself away by putting up “The
Solicitor.” The “ Village Priest” has a matinee
on June 4, and Titheradge takes a benefit on the
afternoon of J une 16. Also a ‘ ‘ Mrs. Tanqueray ”
matinee is promised for the 19th. This is the
first time the Broughs have gone in for the matinee
business.
* * * *
The unlainenfced “White Elephant,” by
the way, got through with all due solemnity till
near the end of its unlovely career, and then it
grew hilarious quite unexpectedly, for reasons that
were not in the bill. Carey, as the stout, gouty
old gentleman, kept up his obesity by means of an
artificial stomach worn underneath his moral black
suit, and one night a rat lodged in this article.
The beast of prey presently broke out, and for a
dreadful hour it roamed over Carey underneath
his clothes, and casually chewed that comedian in
places. Uncle Tweed being a heavy and somno-
lent character, who remained in one place and told
stories of 40 cattle that were fed entirely on oil-
cake, there was no chance to squash the animal by
backing himself hard against a wall, and no one
could smack such a venerable-looking personage
hard on the rat and disguise the act by saying,
“ Hello, old cove ! ” Once Carey fixed the wild
animal under his arm, but when Brough found
him insensible on the floor and lifted him up it
escaped and rushed around him more madly than
ever. Then at last the curtain came down, and
two seconds later an undressed comedian was
wildly aiming boots at the uncouth monster and
objurgating.
** * *
Just at the appropriate moment, when
the first novelty might possibly be wearing off,
the expected religious controversy started to rage
around the “Sign of the Cross.” At a certain
matinee the astute management announced that
the clergy would be admitted free on presentation
of card at the door, and the church rolled up and
presented its card with a unanimity that was
beautiful to behold. The vestibule was darkened
with long-tailed black coats streaking in to the
great free show, and the black hats fled up the
stairs like locusts, and long rows of men with
shaved upper lips sat down to consider the beauty
of Berenis, the pagan. Then the controversy
broke out and has raged in moderation ever since.
Most of the clerics approved, but one, at all events,
came down on the purple-robed Caesar with an
awful thud and flattened him out. Still the
reverend gentlemen who left that great, unani-
mous card at the door were, on the whole, a great
deal less vigorous than The Bulletin expected,
which further convinces this paper that a lion
should put his head round the corner and roar in
the last scene, and gnash a fine set of shop teeth
at the Christians. That final touch of realism
would have certainly aroused some indignant cor-
respondent to use language that would have made
the drama run an extra week.
** * *
Sydney, after all, has not seen the last of
Mrs. Potter and the one transcendent Bellew.
They return shortly to do a one-week season, con-
sisting of two nights of “Romeo and Juliet,”
while four other plays not yet announced will be
put on for one night each. Then the amazing hat
of Mrs. Potter and the hyacinthine locks of Bellew
will vanish seaward in real earnest.
** * #
The Flying Jordans left at the end of last
week, and Sydney Royal is temporarily deserted.
On Saturday night, however, and two nights
afterwards, the Sydney Opera Co. will wrestle
with the “ Grand Duchess,” a piece which has had
a long rest and should be about due for revival.
The Palace has also gone under for a time. The
hard frost which settled down on the Gourlay co.
right from the beginning threatened at one time
to thaw —in fact, as reported in last week’s issue,
it did thaw considerably for a night or two. Then
it froze again as badly as ever, and John Gourlay
departed in search of more appreciative audi-
ences. A tendency to severe frosts seems peculiar
to the Palace, and so far the gods in particular
haven’t taken kindly to the new establishment.
Possibly its magnificence overawes them some-
what. The surroundings don’t lend themselves to
cheerful, off-hand profanity, and the remarks
which the god loves to make don’t go well with
the furniture. Likewise the gilding and the
artistic tints rise up in reproachful judgment
against him when he tries to say “ blanky,” and
seem to destroy the flavor of that useful execration.
** * *
The novelty this week at Sydney Tivoli is
the newly-imported Mr. Wootwell. The gentle-
man with the awkward name is a singer of a kind,
has a considerable capacity for comedy, and is a
wildly-reckless dancer, whose legs will probably
win a great deal of favor. The “ silver-coin ” co.
at the Royal Standard is doing the “ Ticket-of-
Leave Man ” with respectable success, and pur-
poses following it up with “ All for Gold.”
** * *
Three-act farcical comedy has been waning
in Melbourne ever since the golden sun went down
upon the boom-time and husbands grew virtuous
for lack of overdraft to invest in razzle-dazzle.
Sassiety used to roll up religiously to any sort of
farce that wasn’t quite proper, but it now holds
aloof from “A Night Out” at the Princess’s.
The plot is rich with matrimonial giddiness. It
contains three beds, four nightgowns, one rope-
ladder and a gimlet, and when an evil-thinking
waiter forms himself into a social purity league,
and bores the gimlet through a partition, the point
penetrates Paulton’s back. All the elements
—save one—of a first-class, shocking success are
crowded into “ A Night Out,” and yet the
dress-circle is very moderately attended. The
one thing wanting is a sense of reality ; but the
same thing was wanting in a dozen other rapid
farces that found great favor among the more
naked class of theatre-goers in the naughty days
of old. “ A Night Out ” has arrived too late.
Toorak is not taking any, nor does the multitude
rush the show. Nevertheless, and notwithstand-
ing the gloomsome evidence of an aged character
whose stutter convinces nobody, business might
be a lot worse.
* ♦ • •
Paulton seems to spread his style further
than he did on the opening night, with the result
that he catches a few more laughs from those
people who can’t see a joke until it is thrust
under their notice. Miss Alma Stanley, being a
very large lady in very loud dresses, is always an
obvious humorist, and her smile scores every time.
Also the fair young occupants of the four night-
gowns are a refreshing spectacle, and the lively
niece (Miss Stafford) who leads Hans Phillips
astray is calculated to disturb the moral principles
of any Christian boy on this planet. The two
waiters at the unspeakable restaurant are satis-
factory, but Cecil Ward’s colorless treatment of a
colorless part deserves a mild reproof. Last
week’s notice said that Cecil and the gentleman
with the stutter were deserving of a good word,
but the writer has changed his views on both
subjects.
** * *
Next Thursday (June 10th) the theatrical
and musical fraternity will contribute a matinee
show in aid of the Melb. Fever Hospital and the
Jubilee blow-out for the local poor. The Firm
gives the use of the Princess’s Theatre, and the in-
tention of the promoters is to beat their recent
Dramatic Benefit Fund record, if possible. Every
leading artist in Melbourne, likewise the “Lela-
mine ” amateurs, will appear at some point or
other of the entertainment. Mrs. Potter and
Bellew have promised to make a last farewell ap-
pearance on this great occasion only, and a souvenir
programme is to be specially illustrated in honor
of the Queen.
** * *
At Melb. Royal a change of bill ! One
speaks of a new bill at Bland Holt’s house with
the respect due to a circumstance that doesn’t
happen every week. “In Sight of St. Paul’s ”
has held the floor since Easter Monday, and The
Bulletin cannot lightly pass over the fact that
to-morrow (Friday) will be the last night of that
vast production. Such last nights are as
milestones on the road that leadeth to de-
struction; or semi-colons in the story of a
misspent life ; or we may say of them
deah friends but no matter: “A Life of
Pleasure ”is the forthcoming event. Bland Holt
has not yet stated why he held back this dazzling
melodrama until June. He keeps his loyal motive
up his sleeve in the meantime. He is saving it
for a grand jubilee revelation. “A Life of
Pleasure ” is an indirect allusion to the Longest
Reign, and a joyful reminder that our Gracious
has never permitted the cares of monarchy to act
injuriously upon her liver.
** * *
It appears that certain Melbourne amateurs
who didn’t get profusely buttered for their per-
formance in “ Lelamine ” (we are, 0 , so tired of
“ Lelamine ”) have found a satisfactory explana-
tion of the insult. “Several, if not all, of the
critics,” we are gravely informed, “ had orders to
praise ” three favored mortals, and restrain their
enthusiasm as regards the others. “ But why,”
asks a lady correspondent on behalf of the slighted
amateurs,’“ did The Bulletin follow in the same
strain ? ” It is not this journal’s custom to reveal
secrets of a damaging character, but, as a special
concession to the aggrieved parties, The Bulletin
will admit that it was bribed. A man with a
mask over his face came into the office and pur-
chased an unfair notice of “ Lelamine.” Our terms
were £5OO cash and the balance in cigars.
** * *
Mrs. T. R. Andrews, the daintiest of lec-
turesses, protests gently that a Bulletin para-
graph re her discourse on poet Shelley admits of
unpleasant misconstruction. To anyone ac-
quainted with her “ womanly woman ” sentiments
the chaffing tone of the paragraph in question
would sound as harmless as it was intended to be.
But, lest Mrs. Andrews’s enemies (a charming
little matron never lacks enemies among her own
sex) may have chosen to assume that she made
excuses for “ the more shocking portions of
Shelley’s life and writings,” and sympathised
with the sexual giddiness of “ Laon and Cythna,”
The Bulletin now rises, with its hand on its
heart, to exonerate Mrs. Andrews from all sus-
picion of having said aught that could turn the
palest curate pink. So far from defending Shel-
ley’s free-love doctrines, she referred to him as a
painful “illustration of the evils to result from
the system he advocated,’’ and she said of “ Laon
and Cythna ” that “ the lawlessness of the human
passion is revolting in the extreme.”
** * *
Miss Elsie Adair and her “New York
Vaudeville Co.” took possession of Melb. Bijou
on Saturday, and a crowded audience sat out the
proceedings with admirable fortitude. Miss
Adair’s Co.—they have worked their way up-
wards from Adelaide and Broken Hill—includes
half-a-dozen persons actually apparent in the flesh,
and one invisible warbler, whose maudlin ballads
are illustrated by photographs on a sheet. Also,
the same sheet, or a larger piece of calico, is
devoted to the melancholy reflections of a
shadow pantomime, wherein a male shadow
appears to spit fluid at the face of his lydy
friend. The artists engaged in this choice item
don’t appear on the programme. Probably they
are chosen from 11 members of the working staff,
and, maybe, it is an usher or the deputy-
assistant electrician who spits as aforesaid. Con-
sidering the size of the entertainment, the mana-
gerial expenditure on working-staff amounts to
reckless prodigality. Miss Adair’s entertainment
is bounded on the north by a “ dramatic idyll ” (a
mawkish, but well-written sketch, introducing two
men and a child), and on the south by a rural
Yankee comedy, composed chiefly of a rural
gal sitting on a kitchen-table with taters
in her lap, and two nice legs dangling in space.
Why, 0 why, does Yankee comedy lay so much
stress upon taters ? The middle portion of the
programme is a succession of individual turns by
the three principal vaudevillers. These turns
would be more satisfactory if cut shorter. The
ventriloquist, Hugh Emmett, is quite up to the
average, but unduly drawn out. Percy Clifton
sings three similar comic songs without a change
of costume, and Miss Adair, besides singing
sundry ditto in bright style, gives five “gorgeous
dance creations ” with limelight effects. Her
dancing is of the monotonous serpentine order—
an agitation of flowing drapery by two sticks.
Although the limelight eventually broke down
from exhaustion on Saturday night, Miss Adair
got plenty of chance to suggest that her under-
drapery displays, plain and colored, were not more
.striking than the Gurl of Yarmouth’s efforts on
the same lines. In fact, Yarmouth, being tall
and thin, and pretty strong in the arms, was
probably the best vision with two sticks that Mel-
bourne has seen, and the city has had enough
serpentine, anyhow.

At Poverty Point.
Sydney organists are furious over the remarks made «
the handling, by some of their number, of the Town Hail
organ during Wiegand’s absence in Europe. The Town
Hall officials seem unanimous in deriding the idea of
local organists being a “ draw.” The town-clerk charae
terised the affair as “an utter failure.” The Mayor went
further: ‘‘The receipts hardly paid lighting and
blowing expenses.” ® s '
Dear Bulletin,— David Lee, the late Melb. city organist
was not “ a large, athletic person,” but a small tallowv
man. It is true that Best, when in Melb., refused to
touch the old-fashioned instrument, which, he said was
fit only for a bullock to perform upon. David Lee how
ever, managed to get over the difficulty—whatever it mav
be—without bringing much muscle into play. He pulled
out the noisiest “ stops ” seemingly, and thumped the
keys with moderate vigor.—W.
A lady writes stating that she has composed a waltz in
commemoration of the Queen’s Record Reign and will
feel grateful for a notice of its forthcoming publication
by Allan and Co. Ye gods ! A waltz to the glory of a
14st. monarch who has almost lost the use of her Rovai
legs 1
Haresfoot: “ How opinions differ ! I thought Darlev’s
Mercutio greatly inferior to Cecil Ward’s, but Ward
marred the Queen Mab speech by over-acting it. These
purple patches of Shakespeare must not be acted, or the
performer is distracting attention from the matchless
language to himself. Thus with the Seven Ages, Beliew
in Romeo's speech about the apothecary, gave Ward the
proper line. Same with the clergy reading Scripture and
trying to paint the lily and gild refined gold. All’they
have to do is to be merely water-pipes. Otherwise it is
impertinence.”
Harry Paulton takes occasion to quarrel with ‘‘Mrs
Tanquera.v,” for her dying behind the scenes. Just gives
one occasion to remark that the French have a stage
morality of punctilio on items wherein the English are not
squeamish. For instance, no French audience will endure
Othello strangling Desdemona. It was a question whether
Mrs. Tanqueray would not offend French prudeiw
In short, what Lamb and Hazlitt, and even Thackeray
write, in an over-strained way, about the innocence of
the often vile work of Wycherley, Farquhar, and Van-
brugh, not to speak of Etheredge and Rochester, applies
better to modern French comedy, like Paulton’s “ Night
Out”—that it is conducted in an imaginary and unreal
world, not amenable to everyday morality.
Melb. : “ A St. Kilda livery-stabler was enjoying the
fire-scene of ‘ In Sight of St. Paul’s,’ this week, while his
own extensive premises were burnt down. Once a father
and mother, in North Melb., locked up their house with
three children, all they had, and went to a funny play.
When they returned, the children were all burnt to
death.”

Some amusement was caused at a Sydney tneatre on
Queen’s Birthday night by Inspector Lenthal'l, the officer
having control of places of amusement, gazing profoundly
at the stalls, where there were some five seats filled ; then at
a small knot of people in the centre of the dress-circle and
a smattering round the gods’ rail. Having satisfied him-
self that there was no danger from overcrowding, the
official took his departure with the air of a man who had
done his duty.
Harry Rickards, in the course of an interview In Lon-
don, states that in future, when engaging* expensive
artists, it will only be for four months certain, with the
option of a further couple of months if they “ take.” Pre-
vious engagements have been for six months, and
Rickards finds that some big artists are only a paving
commodity for about four months, as all the likely people
in Australia will have seen them in that time, and they
will have to be exceptionally good to “ draw’ ” thereafter.
Brother Bill Rignold tells how, when playing Ratcliff to
Barry Sullivan’s Richard 111., at Sadler's Wells, he re-
ceived special instructions to stand, in the fifth act wait-
ing for his cue—“ Give me another horse; bind up my
wounds,” &c., with the king's sword held across his
palms, and present it thus at the end of the soliloquy
> r L terested in t- he scene, he forgot these directions,
and held the sword hilt upwards over his shoulder, and so
entered and presented it to the king. This was no sooner
done than Barry Sullivan, swearing and howling w’ith
pain, threw it down and roared, “ Where the devil have
you been holding that sword ? ” Then Brother Bill found
he had been unconsciously heating the hilt in a gas-iet
while waiting to go on. J

Gladstone Wright’s “ Concert Direction, Entertain-
ment and General Agency ” made a successful start with
a concert at Melb. Town Hall, on Queen’s Birthday night.
The agency, copied from the latest London models, has
an office at 77 iSwanston-street, Melb., and seems likely to
flourish. Gladstone Wright, who is somewhat of a
Church and Stage Guild in himself, undertakes to supply
living pictures, soloists for church-choirs, stage-fittings,
and vocal material for first-class concerts, public or pri-


vate at short notice. Also, a school of singing is
attached to the agency, whereat soulless warblers may
get instruction in the art of throwing out their chests
and trilling a lay with expression.
Furniss’s “lectures” are not Radical intone. Those
neople— mostly bald at one end and gouty at the other—
who have been accustomed to divide between the Bible
and Punch the responsibility of England’s greatness totter
up and bring their families. Beresford and Chameleon
Chamberlain received rapturous applause in the first lec-
ture ; Bradlaugh never a “hand.”

A Frenchman is showing at Lyons a piano-plaving
ourang-outang. The instrument used is a specially-
manufactured one, and the ape performs the first half of
the “ Marseillaise” by ear with creditable execution. The
owner is sanguine of adding to the monkey’s repertoire.
“Lelamine” is being re-written. In the new edition
Pkre Joseph will have no connection with the church, but

the piece. The opera, they say, is to be given at a

Daisy Chamier, daughter of Geo. Chamier, C.E., of
Sydney, has gone to Leipsic Conservatoire for further
study. The young Australian student has already dis-
played conspicuous talent. She studied the piano under
Madame Charbonnet Kellerman, obtaining the gold medal
at Madanie’s Conservatoire, and also gold medal for piano-
forte playing at the Australian Musical Association’s
exam, in ’95. One of her compositions has been accepted
by a London publisher.
Leon Caron is about to visit Paris. His wife (Miss Elsa
Sherwin) and children will accompany him. A move-
ment is abroad in musical circles for a benefit.
Harry Paulton, of the Firm’s new comedy co., was mis-
taken for Billy Elton, skirmishing about the back of the
d.c. on several recent nights. The resemblance is
marked—size, figure, face, and the same perky, bird-like
movements.

Melb. Presbyterian Church, and, of course, a “ teacher of
the pianoforte and singing.” The production of his opera,

Haresfoot: “‘Lelamine’ suggested to me how inevitably
superior the amateur chorus must be to the professional
in the important point of being natural, while its vocalism
has the charm of freshness in the voices, and spontaneity.
What the irritated tutor is always trying to drill into the
professional, the amateur does of herself —that is to say,
takes an intelligent interest in the proceedings. Of
course, the demoralisation of the ‘ Lelamine ’ maidens
began, in a histrionic point of view, from the very first
night, after which they mostly began striving to appear
careless and unconcerned, in emulation of their weary
sisters who work for a few shillings a week.”

has unearthed. Mavis, Darley, Spencer, Ralfe, Rebottarc,
all marked for more than a little distinction, in light
music.”
Shakespeare, who was down upon a good many popular
frauds, didn’t forget to cut his little joke at palmistry.
Shylock’s servant, Gobbo, gazing at the palm of his left
hand, says : “ Go to, here’s a simple line of life 1 here’s a
small trifle of wives : Aas, fifteen wives is nothing ; eleven
widows and nine maids is a simple coming-in for one man ;
and then to ’scape drowning thrtce ; and to be in peril of
my life with the edge of a feather bed ; here are simple
’scapes.” Gobbo was born 300 years ago, but he’s not
dead yet.
Thomas A. Edison, the great inventor, has
just produced the “ HOME ” phonograph, which,
as the name implies, is intended to supply the de-
mand for an instrument for private use having all
the vital points of the expensive exhibition phono-
graph combined with a low price. This new
" talking machine,” costing only =£l2, will soon be
found in every house in the country, as it affords
infinite amusement and instruction, and is also in-
dispensable in an up-to-date office. The manager
of the Edison Electric Parlor (162 Pitt-st., Sydney)
reports a strong demand.*
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The South Australian loan just floated in Lon-
don gives Treasurer Holder a high place among financial
imbeciles. S.A. had a large amount of local Treasury
bills (bearing interest at £4 11s. 3d. percent, per annum)
to redeem, and it was necessary either to persuade the
owners to renew or else float a conversion loan. Holder
first of all offered the owners the option of taking 3 per
cent, stock at par in exchange. There was no great rush
to accept, and Holder straightway offered a £1,000,000
3 per cent, loan in London at £95. That is to say, he
offered to sell the S.A. Government securities to John
Bull at £5 less than the price at which they were tendered
to the S.A. holder of Treasury bills. As for the general
public of S.A., it wasn’t offered a chance even at par.
Moreover, as floating the loan in London means paying
12s. 6d. per cent, stamp-duty to the British Government
and at least 7s. 6d. per cent, in other expenses, Holder
was really offering to accept from J. Bull less than £94
for the securities which he wouldn’t offer in Australia
below £lOO.
Holder’s explanation was that the money would
have to be remitted to London to pay charges there, and
allowing for cost of exchange it would be as profitable to
get £9B 10s. in London as £lOO in Adelaide. This argu-
ment only shows his utter incapacity to see the bearing of
things. Some time or other the money must be repaid
by sending it from S.A. to London, and, therefore, the
cost of remittance must be paid sooner or later. Conse-
quently, he doesn’t save the cost of remittance as he
alleges; he merely postpones it. Also there are other
items to be taken into consideration. Floating a loan
locally means saving the cost of sending the interest to
England every half-year for, perhaps, a few centuries ;
and it means that part of the interest payments will come
back to the State as income-tax. These two items, in the
course of years, would far more than make up for the one
immediate saving in cost of remittance which Mr. Holder
was so anxious to effect. Further, local flotation would keep
£30,000 a year interest in the country, and as there is far
more than a million lying idle and earning absolutely
nothing in the local banks it would be £30,000 a year
saved with no loss whatever to set against it. And, in any
case, the fact —if it had been a fact —that £lOO in Adelaide
was only equal to £9B 10s. in London was no explanation
of the unspeakable foolishness which fixed the net mini-
mum in Adelaide at £lOO and in London (after allowing
for expenses) at £94. Thirty shillings isn’t £6.
The fact that a loan was going to be offered in
London was not definitely known in Adelaide. The local
capitalists probably imagined they would be allowed a
chance to come in, and so they waited for their opportunity
and were left. How suddenly they were left may be seen
in the fact that holders of Treasury bills were allowed till
25th May to accept stock in repayment, yet the loan was
actually offered in London on the 24th. And when S.A.’s
financial bungler had put his loan on the London market
at a minimum of £95 (or less than * 94 net), he was rushed
in a single day with offers of 1560,000 in Adelaide, where
there would be practically no expenses, at £9B and £9B
10s. Other offers came in later on. This covered the
whole amount immediately required ; the other £440,000
isn’t wanted for about a year. Judging by all appear-
ances, therefore, it would seem that the S.A. Government
could certainly have got the balance locally when it was
wanted, and thus have saved a year’s interest as well as
expenses. The net result was that the amount of the
loan was increased needlessly to £1,060,000, so as to give
some of the local tenderers a show. The S.A. people take
about half of it at £9B and £9B 10s. John Bull takes the
rest at something like £95 10s., after deducting expenses
—the exact figures are not yet available. The country
pays about £12,000 extra in interest through doing the
flotation in one lump instead of getting the money in
Adelaide as required, and loses a lot more through selling
to Bull at a reduced rate. And a fine chance to make
S.A. independent of the British money-lender has been
stupidly muddled away.
• '* • *
Westralia is now spending loan money at the
rate of about £1,350,000 a year, which, in proportion to
population, is about the same as if N.S.W. spent
£10,000,000 a year or Victoria £9,000,000. This is abso-
lutely the biggest, wildest, maddest borrowing orgy that
even Australia ever saw. And the difficulty of raising
money is becoming very great. A few months ago Wes-
tralia got out of a tight place by selling £480,000 worth of
Treasury bills to the banks at 4 per cent., and it is said to
be arranging for the sale of another £500,000 worth at 4J
per cent. ! These rates show what the banks, at all events,
think of Westralian credit. Also, a third issue of
£500,000 worth of Treasury bills is rumored for the latter
part of this year or somewhere early in ’9B. When the
borrowing has to stop, or at least to greatly diminish,
what a historic collapse there will be !
• » * *
Item from the report of the London committee
of A.J.S. Bank depositors : “ The committee were desirous
that a specific amount of the capital of the bank should
be written off to provide for estimated loss, but they
eventually considered it better to leave this important
matter to the decision of the new board of directors, to
which the depositors will be entitled to elect three mem-
bers.” This is a specific intimation that, in the com-
mittee’s opinion, a part of the capital has been lost, but
how much isn’t even hinted. The Bulletin suggested
the same thing a good many months ago when it pointed
out that the old reserve-fund, set aside to meet losses,
stood in the balance-sheet at £573,000 at December, ’95,
while at December, ’96, it had vanished altogether. This
seemed to imply that the losses were rolling in very fast,
and when the reserve becomes exhausted the only way to
cover any further losses is by writing down capital. The
A.J.S. has only about £1,200,000 of shareholders’ funds
altogether, and even a moderate writing-off, as these
things go nowadays, would reduce it to the rank of a very
second-rate institution.
• * * *
The New York Life Insurance Co.’s 1896
balance-sheet shows a premium income of £6,407,011,
being £168,000 more than last year. Interest and rents
(£1,646,396) are £BB,OOO more than last year. The income
thus reaches a splendid total of £8,053,407, and of this
£2,621,523 is added to the accumulated funds, making
them £38,513,663, and there is a surplus of assets over
liabilities of £5,490,122. This surplus, however, is arrived
at by estimating that the accumulated funds will return
4 per cent, per annum permanently. All the three big
American offices doing business in Australia estimate on
the 4 per cent, basis (so do most Australian offices,
by the way), and if instead they estimated future
interest at £3 12s. 6d. per cent., as is done by the A.M.P.
Society and the Maoriland Government Life Assurance
Department, or at £3 10s. per cent., as the A.M.P. and
Mutual Life Association are both preparing to do, it would
make a very large hole in the three sensational surpluses.
The New York earns less than 4)4 per cent, inter-
est, and the margin is very small according to Aus-
tralian ideas. The weakest point about the New
York Life, however, as with the other two “ American
giants,” is the expense rate. This has been largely
reduced of late, but even now expenses absorb 25
per cent, of premiums or 20 per cent, of total income. In
1895 (the last year for which complete returns are avail-
able), the A.M.P. spent less than 9 per cent, of its total
income in expenses ; Maoriland Government Department
about 13 per cent., National Mutual 18£ per cent,., and
the Widows’ Fund, Mutual Life Association and Colonial
Mutual each a fraction under 20 per cent. All these, ex-
cept the A.M.P., are very much smaller and younger than
the New York Life; and even the A.M.P. is also much
smaller. By comparison, especially with the A.M.P. and
Maoriland Government Department, the expense-rate
of the American office looks wildly extravagant.
The best feature about the American offices
is that their securities are reported, by an independent
state auditor, to be absolutely sound and worth
every penny of the value set against them, and if a
similar officer gave the same guarantee about the Aus-
tralian offices it would be very much better for them. The
Maoriland Government Department, which is not only
State-audited but State-guaranteed, is the only one which
is distinctlv ahead of even the American institutions in
this as well as every other respect. The biggest surplus
shown by any American institution as a guarantee for its
policy-holders is the Equitable’s—£9,6oo,ooo. The Maori-
land institution’s surplus is just about £lso,ooo,ooo—in
other words, it consists of the entire assets and credit of
the province. « * »
An agreement has been arrived at among the
fire insurance companies to greatly increase rates in and
around Melbourne, and a feeble kick among property-
holders naturally ensues. Fire insurance in Melbourne
doesn’t pay to any extent worth speaking of at present
rates The only way to make it pay is to increase rates
or to reduce expenses. The latter are the chief source of
trouble ; in 1895 the Australian fire and marine companies
spent £330,000 in expenses to earn £1,071,000 in premiums.
There are far too many companies in the business, there-
fore there are too many offices and managers and directors
and staffs and separate sets of furniture and stationery to
maintain. Also the competition makes it necessary to
pav away far too much in commission to get business at
all And as new English, French, German, Austrian,
Swiss Italian, Chinese and Japanese companies keep
opening branches in Australia, the competition gets
worse instead of better, and as it gets worse the expense
rate must get heavier and insurance rates must grow
larger There are only two visible remedies. Either
the Government must levy special taxation on the
foreign companies taxation heavy enough to clear
a large proportion of them out and thus reduce
the ruinous competition. Or else it must clear all
the offices out, and make fire insurance a state monopoly,
and insure everybody’s property by compulsion. This
would entirely do away with the heavy charge for com-
mission, and economise offices ; where there are now 23
fire insurance offices in Pitt-street, Sydney, alone, one
would be sufficient, and the other 22 rents would be
saved. Also, the abandonment of 22 big offices in one
street would make a grievous slump in rents, consequently
the property-owner is distinctly hostile. A State fire-in-
surance department on the compulsory principle could
do business at the present rates and make a handsome
profit out of them ; and the present rates could be made
to pay—though not nearly so well—if a wholesale eject-
ment of the foreign companies were effected. Otherwise
rates must go up and the man who wants to protect his
house or his tables and chairs from combustion must
stand it the best way he can.

The Bank of Australasia has notified its staff
throughout Australasia that the 10 per cent, reduction in
salaries, made in October, ’95, has been restored—on the
strength of last balance-sheet, and a steady improvement
in business prospects.
Another step in the direction of municipal
socialism is about to be taken in Vic. Delegates of the
various metropolitan councils have agreed on the prelim-
inary details of a scheme of municipal fire insurance.

It is said that some of the interprovincial steam-
ship co’s are about to take action curtailing commercial
travellers’ “concession” privileges. A deduction of 20
per cent, on steamer tickets is allowed drummers, but the
privilege has been so much abused —anyone said to be
connected with a wholesale house, or having a friend
there, travelling at the lower rate—that the steamer co’s
have at last kicked.
* * * •
For every <£l paid in mining and ore-dressing
wages by the B.H. Junction Co. last half-year £2 12s. re-
presented the shareholders’ profit. But never again—
with the ore at present developed, anyway.
On the last half-year’s work the Junction made
a profit of £1 3s. 4d. per ton of ore ; Block 10, 12s. 7d.;
Block 14, 4s. lid. But as the Junction used up so much
of its high-grade stuff the comparison, so far as it is con-
cerned, is not a fair one. The gross value of the Block 14
product was £2 9s. 3d. per ton of ore.
What the still-inefficient sulphide treatment by
concentration means in the way of machinery may be
judged by the statement of the Block 14 directors that
they have spent £52,103 in machinery in the last three
years.
Block 10 has made a capital recovery ; in the
six months to the end of March £20,000 was paid in divs.,
and £2OOO spent on machinery, and liquid assets in-
creased by £l3OO. And as more ore is now being treated
and the plant is running more regularly, no difficulty
should be experienced in keeping this up—assuming there
is no further slump in silver. The financial position is,
for these times, uncommonly strong.
The general-manager of Block 10 claims that
his concentrating plant for sulphides is doing as good
work as, if not better than, any other on the Hill. And
this is what it did : The quantity of crude ore treated was
41,519 tons, assaying 19'08oz. silver, 20'06 per cent, lead,
and 29'41 per cent. zinc. This produced 9969 tons con-
centrates, assaying 33'170z. silver, 64'84 per cent, lead,
and 9'57 per cent, zinc; also 2100 tons slimes, assaying
28oz. silver, 21 per cent, lead, and 28 per cent. zinc. The
total result thus shows a recovery of 49T6 per cent, of the
contained silver, and 82'66 per cent, of the contained lead.
But the slimes are of comparatively little value, on ac-
count of the high percentage of zinc (due to the very in-
timate association of the zinc and silver), so that for all
practical purposes the recovery of the metals is consider-
ably less than that stated. Practically, in order to elimin-
ate two-thirds of the zinc and some gangue, three-fifths
of the silver and one-fourth of the lead are sent away with
the tailings ! There is, obviously, still room for the in-
ventor.

A Sydney syndicate lately sent up an expert to
Mount Browne district, in the far north-west corner of
N.S.W., to report on a number of quartz claims placed
under offer. “ Don’t touch ” was the advice ; the claims
that are any good are, it seems, too far apart, and holders
were not modest in their estimates of value.

Louis Janin, jun., son of the world-renowned
American metallurgist, returned to Australia per ’Frisco
mail. He took a prominent part in organising the
English co. which owns Fort Bourke mine, Cobar—now
known as the Cobar Gold Mines Ltd.—and will be in time
to see manager O’Gorman’s 100-head battery start. The
huge dam is complete, and awaits water. The mill will
be ready in a few weeks.

In the wild-boom days of Coolgardie, when
cargoes of gold and champagne were imported from Eng-
land, every opportunity for a razzle-dazzle was taken.
One of the chief dazzlers was a well-known mining “ cap-
tain” whose razzle-preliminary formula was : “ Genelmen,
fill yer glasses and drink with me, as man to man, the
’ealth of the cappittalists who ’ave ’onored us with their
presence ’ere this hevening.”
Says a paper printed at Hannan’s, W.A.: “If
the disposition of the financial world towards West Aus-
tralian mining were influenced by developments, there
have been, during the present year, sufficient to create a
far greater boom than any yet experienced. The rich
telluride discoveries that have been made in almost a
dozen mines, the marvellous yields from the ore sent to
South Australia from the Brown Hill and other properties,
the wonderful strike of almost pure gold in the Great
Boulder,” &c. But booms don’t follow on the heels of
each other. The boom has been and gone, and a very
long time must elapse before Kalgoorlie or any part of
Westralia sees another.
Coolgardie has decided on holding a mining
exhibition at a cost of £lO,OOO. It will enable Coolgardie
to make an exhibition of itself. As a place which has spent,
within a radius of three miles, the most money on
mines with the least result, Coolgardie would take first
prize against the world.

A flow of natural gas was tapped the other day
at Glenariff bore, Rockwood (N.Q.). When a match was
held over the bore a great jet of flame illumined the
country for far around.
« « » *
Peak Downs is the oldest and most prolific Q.
alluvial goldfield. It was first found in ’6l, and has for
36 years since turned out its respectable annual quota.
Till recently only shallow ground was worked, but now
the deep country has proved rich and things are booming.
The Deep Lead gives wash-dirt from a depth of 70ft., is a
mile long, and two claims broad. But Clermont is over-
rushed at present.

Sharebrokers on the Towers are looking blank
and miserable: business is flat, stale and unprofitable.
The “ seven-weeks-delivery ” business keeps the small fry
going, and brokers generally are turning their attention
from scrip dealing to specking the Towers double at the
coming annual.

Curious explosion at Alliance Sunburst mine,
Gympie (Q.), the other day. Mine had been idle for
years, and work had just been resumed. The manager
and a director were exploring the 220 ft. level. A lighted
candle burned all right, but when Johns held it up to
show the reef, something exploded, and both men were
badly burnt. Could it have been fire-damp, with that
lighted candle ?
« * • »
Another attempt is to be made to work the
Downman coal property, about 1)4 miles from Logan
Village (Q.), and consisting of about 542 acres. Nominal
capital, £5OO. A contract has been taken to sink an air-
shaft 300 ft. Highly necessary, for the writer remembers
what most people seem to have forgotten—the fatal issue
of the last attempt to work this property some years ago.
Two men worked in it safely all the morning, went above-
ground for lunch, passed back to work with a naked
light—hroar! Both killed in the explosion.
• * * •
As anticipated, the Victoria and Caledonia
Block, Charters Towers, bottomed on about 10 inches of
stone, and shares, which had been declining owing to a
report that the Victoria level had tested f he block ground
with unsatisfactory results, rose to 6s. The reef at the
time of discovery was estimated to be worth 20z., and
several knowing ones on the Towers are sorry they spoke.
The Victoria reef has been phenomenally rich in the P.C.
ground, and a new lease of life, with satisfactory results
to the shareholders, may be expected in the Block and
contiguous leases—that white elephant of John Bull, to
wit, the Carrington.
* • • »
If a pick had been put in the ground contained
in the lease of the Victoria No. 1 West, Charters Towers,
shares in the co. would probably be worth £1 a-piece. As
it is they were at latest advices quoted, buyers fid. A
crushing completed on 21st May gave the poor return of
14oz. from 51 tons. From the shaft of this mine a small
boy could throw a stone into that of Clark’s Worcester,
the 3000 odd oz. cake wherefrom (31oz. to the ton) is now
on show in Brisbane. The Victoria No. 1 West, although
surrounded by proved payable mines, a near neighbor of
the Victory, Kelly’s Block, Brilliant, Brilliant and St.
George, and other big guns, has always proved a veritable
stringer. Yet some Towers men have faith in the ven-
ture. “ Joe ” Connolly, chemist Griffith, A. G. Hillyard,
and others are persistent stickers, and go on smilingly
waiting for the something which never turns up.

More big money to go into Mt. Lyell smelting
plant, and to refund the profit—£sl,66o already absorbed
in that direction. One share for every 8 now held to be
issued to the shareholders at £2, and the balance, 1262
shares, to be sold for the good of the co. This money will
complete the five furnaces, and initiate a further exten-
sion of the plant. A4s. div., amountingto nearly £50,000,
is promised in July. For mining enterprise before divs.,
Mt. Lyell has no equal in Australasia, and few in any part
of the world. There are, however, quite a number of
mines working in Australia to-day that have exhausted
close on £lOO,OOO without ever paying a div. Why doesn’t
one of the daily papers give a list of them ? Papers are
very fond of publishing the big div.-payers, and thepublio
should occasionally see the other side of the picture. To
the writer’s knowledge, George Lansell has alone paid over
£lOO,OOO calls into one mine on Bendigo.

SOCIETY
The situation of some of the Northern
squatters just now is truly desperate. Prices
have fallen steadily for some years; they are
going through almost the most cursed drought
on record, and, to crown all, the Govt, has drawn
a tick quarantine line round them and i orbidden
the sending of cattle to market. In a fet ’ months
there will be no cattle to send.
» * » *
“ Veldt ” to The Bulletin :
In the Riverina things are about as bad as they can be.
Lambing has begun, and thousands of lambs are being
killed to save the mothers. The former must die, any-
way, as the ewes have no milk. Last week I saw two
cows bogged in about 6in. of mud ; one died in her
struggles, the other was dug out. On Eunonyhareenyha
station men are employed cutting down box timber to
feed the sheep. Other stations are just as badly off, many
of them feeding their flocks on barley and wheat-straw
and treacle. For miles around not a vestige of grass is to
be seen, and as soon as the cold weather sets in the mor-
tality amongst stock will be awful. I have seen during
the past few days cattle (or rather skeletons) eating fresh
horse-dung. The farmers also are in a very bad way ; a
SOME PRETTY DESIGNS IN FIREWORKS FOR THE APPROVAL OF THE
JUBILEE COMMITTEE.
little ploughing is going oil, bat the cost for ploughshares is
enonuous, and the clods turn up as big as a bag of wheat,
On Gobbagomblin farm som? seed was sown two months
ago ;it still lies in the ground has not germinated. The
only irood the drought cun do is to exterminate the
bunnies and give a harvest to the carrion crow.
. * .* * *
The tick question is a queer one to settle,
In N Q. you will see in some parts grass as red as
a field of rusted wheat with the young ticks. A
bird alighting on the herbage carries off the bud-
ding pests in thousands. A swaggie’s blankets
swarm with them. The only wav of blocking
them will bo to cut off a strip of quarantine
eountry on the south ; dip every swaggie who
passes in croton oil or sulphuric acid, and shoot
every bird that flies overhead. The Boers almost
go as far to check the rinderpest.
* * * *
A Brisbane (Q.) paper was called upon
everybody within its hail to drink (at each’s own
expense) a Birthday toast to Her Gracious, “by
virtue of her maternity and pre-eminent widow-
hood.” Grovel in black crape, with a mug of beer
at its mouth ! “ Pre-eminent widowhood ! ” The
writer knew a woman who, at her last widowhood,
had lost her fifth husband. She’s now got another,
She was more eminent in widowhood than Queen
Victoria, who, if report speaks truly, would have
married again but for her Cabinet Ministers’
advice not to chance further possibilities. The
six-times married woman didn’t fret much over
her fifth ; she merely said—“ Well, that’s another
decent man gone. Well, well; they turns up un-
expectedly, and then they turns up their toes
unexpectedly. I suppose Ben, down in the
pocket, will be axing me next. He was too late
last tini“, poos feller.” That’s something like
“pre-eminent widowhood !”
-m o T, ttt i * i * „ .
From New South Wales alone something
like 400 illuminated addresses are being sent to
England to be about three cubic yards of waste
paper on top of Queen Victoria’s soul on her 60th
anniversary. At this rate the chances are that
the illuminated addresses will reach 40,000, quite
apart from telegrams and letters. The only
possible result is that Victoria’s secretary will hire
Fix or eight or ten supercilious understrappers
who will cackle derisively about the grammar of
the various grovellers (including N.S.W. Par-
liament) and send each one a circular of per-
functory thanks. No one expects any tired old
lady of nearly 80 to look at all the mass of rub-
bish. The best that can happen to it is that it
will all be put away somewhere in packing-cases
and gnawed by rats, and the worst is that it will
be combusted under the boiler on washing-day.
Possibly a list of the senders may be placed before
Her Gracious, and anybody who has ever read
right through the Melbourne directory for amuse-
ment will understand exactly what will happen
to it.
• • * »
Practically no money is being spent in
Record Reign illuminations in Adelaide, either
publicly or privately.
* * * •
Essendon (Melb.) Town Council proposes
to celebrate the Record-Reign by “ building a lake,
and erecting an island in the centre of it.” The
man who is so glad because an old lady he never
saw is still alive, that he must dig a hole to give
vent to his happiness, is a fine specimen of the art
of rejoicing with difficulty. The Bulletin sug-
gests, as an amendment, that the Essendon coun-
cillors rejoice by each carrying a mangle to Pascoe
Vale and back. The sort of man who must give
vent to his gladness by humping something heavy
would find a lot of mental outlet by carrying a
mangle.
• • • •
‘' Considerations for the coming J übilee
and the Queen’s superstitious apprehensions have
influenced the * dailies ’ to almost pass over the
explosion in the Underground Railway,” writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent. “The
bomb had been placed on the floor of a first-class
, V , i , ~, , • , ,
carriage the only Ist on the tram, and empty
and exploded just as the engine pulled up at
Aldersgate-street, a large station open up to the
sky excepting for an iron roof 40ft. above the
rails. Those waiting on the platform received the
force of the explosion, which blew three compart-
ments clean out, walls and roof, scattering the
fragments and broken glass like some hugefou -
gasse in the faces of the unfortunates. One man
had an eight-inch javelin of glass through his
collar and stuck in his neck. The scene of anni-
hilated wreckage presented half-an-hour after the
occurrence convinced your correspondent that no
gas cylinder explosion had done the damage,
Where it is supposed the explosive lay a jagged
gap yawned in the solid diagonal flooring of the
carriage, and the frames of two shattered windows
and a crumpled hat-rack were all that was
left of the three compartments. Had the ex-
plosion taken place 50 yards earlier in the tunnel
the holocaust would have been hellish.”

Clearly, the Melb. section of the Harmy
takes no interest in local politics. At the opening
of its Workmen’s Metropole (where customers
can get a square-meal, with or without salvation
for a penny), ‘‘Commandant” Booth referred
to Attorney-General Isaacs as “His Honor Sir
Isaac Isaacs, Chief Justice of Victoria,” and
the multitude accepted the statement quite
seriously. Bv the way, there was a wholesome
snub for the Presbyterians, if they would only
take it, in the aforesaid Harmy function. Booth
carelessly mixed up the identities of Jewish
Isaacs and Roman Catholic Madden, whilst two
other Jews, brother Johnnie Isaacs and Zox,
stood around taking a hand in the inauguration of
a practical Christian work. A suitable moment for
the Presbyterian missionary to come forward and
preach the blessed terewth to that trio of public
Unbelievers.

a young lion of the tribe of Judah, opens the

“ It is not surprising,” writes ;i
Bulletin correspondent, “to hear
that one of the female practitioners
bo highly eulogised after a year’6
service at the big Melb. ‘ mixed ’
hospital lately applied in vain for a
position at the Women’s Hospital,
where patients, as a rule, are accou-
chement cases. This temple of ma-
ternity is run by women of the well-
dressed persuasion, whose own little
interesting events were doubtless con-
ducted under conditions which streng-
thened their early belief in the man-
doctor, or, rather, inclined them natu-
rally to the idea that an accoucheur
is quite as ‘proper ’ as an accoucheuse,
if not more so. Any Australian
mother of 20 years’ standing, having
brought forth children at a time when
the medical business out here was re-
stricted to men, is slow to admit the
hideous humbug of her old idea.
Regarding herself as a paragon of
matronly modesty, she can’t see why
girls should object to a man’s medical
attendance in the most delicate of
cases. In other words, she considers
lady doctors quite unnecessary to
society, even if they be not actually
improper. Women are either warmly
in favor of the woman-doctor prin-
ciple, or distinctly opposed to it, and
the bulk of the opposition springs
from the same sentiment that moved
the tailless animal in the fable to
advocate a sacrifice of tails all round.
I don’t impute tailless animal senti-
ment to the Melb. Women’s Hospital
Committee. I merely say that most
elderly women, and all elderly mo-
thers, have acquired a taste for male
doctors, or say, rather, a distaste for
the female ditto.”

The story of the fatal shooting-
by-accident case (Melb.), in which
two middle-aged men appear to have
played at duelling together, is anothei
illustration of the fact that truth can
be a lot stranger than fiction. Old
Coroner Youl, with his long experi-
ence of suspicious-looking narratives,
saw not the slightest reason to doubt
the evidence given at the inquest.
I tie deceased journalist died through
fooling with firearms, a folly upon
which he had no doubt moralised in
print a hundred times.

At Melb. District Court, recently, one Ah
Din was fined 10s. 6d. for working on April 7 last
U.e., for not taking the prescribed half-holiday).
Ah' Quay, presumably because he worked harder,
was fined <£s. As neither Chow paid, both were
ordered to be imprisoned, which provoked Ah
l)in to give his opinion of Vic. law to the senior-
constable. “Man no wuck he go gaol,” he
pleaded, “man wuck he go gaol allee same,
[nglishman law no good.” Then they were
inarched off.
It is seriously printed that permission has
oeen given to Chinamen in Cooktown and Char-
ters Towers (N.Q.) to run their gambling lotteries.
At any rate, while the smirky Tozer and the
devout Tommy Byrnes ran George Adams out of
Leperland, the pak-a-pu and fantan industries in
the Northern towns mentioned are openly coun-
tenanced by the authorities.
* * • •
The April list of patients in Cairns (N.Q.)
hospital showed —out of a total of 33 males —four
Indians, three Polynesians and one Chinaman.
Twenty-five per cent, of alien is a pretty good
allowance—even for Mongrelia.
* • * •
In consequence of the conflicting provi-
sions of the Merchant Shipping Act and the
Chinese restriction legislation of N.S.W., sea cap-
tains commanding vessels manned by Chinese
crews have frequently a hard row to hoe. If a
slant-eyed tar commits a breach of discipline
the captain cannot threaten him with the terrors of
the Merchant Shipping Act, as the authorities
ashore will not take the recalcitrant alien into
custody unless his <£loo poll-tax be paid. The
sea-faring Chow knows the weakness of the law
and takes advantage of it. And this is the con-
dition of affairs which led to the bastinadoing of
two Chinese sailors on the Tsinan and the subse-
quent fining of the captain and chief-officer.
* • * *
The Cairns Council—Cairns, as already
stated, is the place where every white man doffs
his hat to all Chinamen—met, the other day, and
decided not to interfere with the joyous little pro-
ceedings of the 37 Japanese ladies setted in the
“beautiful city.” It was alleged, in justification,
that unless the salacious Kanaka were allowed
some outlet for his exuberance, Cairns women
would be exposed to the same dangers as their
sisters in Bundaberg and Mackay. How the alien
curse is working ! The Kanaka is made the plea
for the admission of the Japanese prostitute ; the
Jap prostitute brings the Jap male bully : the
bully sends for his relatives—and so on. One
hopes that the Cairns Council was not also con-
sidering the interests of certain swell whites who
patronise the local Yoshiwara.
* » * *
Writes “Warrumboo” from Mount Mag-
net, Westralia: —
Dear Bulletin, —Re your remarks about the importa-
tion of Japanese women to the Murchison. We have
had 20 of them (with six males only) located here for the
last three months, and it looks as if they intend to stay.
One party has opened a fancy-goods store right in
the middle of the town. Shortly after their advent here
they reduced their tariff 50 per cent, all round, including
washing. In the washing line they have it'all their own
way, as the following fact will show. A few w*-.eks ago a
respectable white woman (whose husband was inutile hos-
pital with fever) tried to support herself and young family
by taking in washing and ironing, but could not get
enough to make a living, as all the hotels, private fami-
lies, and most of the single men in the town send their
washing to the Japs., who do it at rates that can t be
competed with by white women. This is a goldfields
town, too, and there is nothing in Westralian law i<> pre-
vent them from settling in any town as long as the.i take
out a business license and open some sort, of supposed
business. “ Australia for the Australians 1

A Towers Chinaman was recently asked in
court “What religion are you—Christian or
pagan? ” To which the guileless one responded :
“Me blow out matchee.” He was sworn mime
diately. Another gave his occupation thusly:
“Me swear and chop wood." And they say the
Chow has no humor.

alleged to have got away with
some thousands, and is now in
gaol awaiting trial, was a shin-
ing light in the sinner-saving
business, and led the choir regu-
larly. It is not known whether

levanted was a leading Sunday-

long ago, two churchwardens

the same church, and got clear

apiece. Soon afterwards, the
Presbyterians lost a shining
legal light by a self-inflicted

a little fortune. Only the
goody-goody get the chance to
do such things.

The latest trick of the sneak-thief : A
gorgeously - dressed individual, carrying a large
Gladstone, calls re board and lodging, or a room,
and in gay and haughty manner requests to be
shown the apartment. While he hums and haws
and tries to make up his mind, a hideous uproar at
the back gate draws the landlady’s attention, am!
sometimes she begs to be and hies to in-
vestigate. Then the gorgeous one fills the bag and
departs in haste, while the dam -' vainly tries to
get the confederate out of the Backyard, where
he vigorously stuffs her with enough lies to sink
a Sunday paper.
* * * *
A Sydney hard-up relates that after arising
from a sixpenny bed the other morning he got in
Sussex-street, for threepence in all (his sole capital)
a cup of tea with bread and butter ; a shave, and
a paper-collar.
WHEN THE HEART IS YOUNG.
“ What shall we plant in the fruitful soil
Of hearts quite young and fresh ?
Something that needs but little toil,
Since Pleasure is our profesh.
What shall we plant in our youth-time strong,
Ere the sun of its gladness sets ;
Something to last us a whole life long ? ”
The Devil said : “ Plant regrets.
“ Feebly at first they may seem to grow,
For the surface soil is light;
But their roots strike deep in the ground below ;
They get along then all right.
Clusters of berries galore they bear,
Whose flavor one never forgets ;
What shall you plant in your youth-time fair? ”
Cried Nicholas : “ Plant regrets.”
Melb.
E.F.

Georgina River, W.Q., I found one old fellow who always
prostrated himself at sunset and sunrise,” write “The
Australian aboriginals prostrate themselves at sunset and
sunrise.” JamesO. (H.): Yes, wedorecommend him..

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
»r tv „ Sydney, June 1, 1897.
Mr Deab Mtke—
The University Sports Union ball in Padding-
ton Town Hall, on Tuesday of last week, was an-
nounced to be in honor of the visiting boat-race
crews irom Melb. and Adelaide, but, as the Melb
crew had left for its native seat of learning in
Gar] ton the day before, it was a somewhat belated
level. It was a youthful festivity, chaperons were
dotted at rare intervals along the walls, and each
Jarge matron seemed to be chaperoning at least
half-a-dozen pretty girls. The officials were
haunted by fear that the crop of wall-flowers
would be heavy, as the women’s tickets sold far
outnumbered the men’s, until the morning of the
event when the market for men's pasteboards
prew brisk, and numbers were about equal. Late
in the evening most of the magnates, who had been
at the Govt. House festivity, arrived, and brought
with them an impression of diamonds and gorgeous
apparel, and gave the requisite tone to the party.
Consumptives are a fashionable craze just now
among a few dozen other charities. To raise
money for the proposed hospital nearly every
sassiety person is hard at work thinking up anew
scheme for entertainments at so much per head,
the proceeds to be given to the pet object,
the thinking generally results in urgent letters
demanding professional assistance. I have seen
many replies, nearly all in the affirmative. The
reallj' generous persons in the case are the profes-
sionals who give time, talent, and trouble gratis,
and cheapen their own future efforts. The pro-
fessional musician or performer of any kind who
is engineered by an energetic sassiety worker into
showing for chanty ”is his own most formidable
rival and nobody knows that fact better than the
pro. himself. And yet the fact rarely stands in
the way of generous professional assistance freely
given to every charity-boom. A committee is
working up a Consumptive-hospital appeal for
. line rejoicings. A theatrical carnival and a mon-
ster concert are both in the plan of campaign.
<< eu nC u-* 1 ? r raid , for charit y i s organising in aid of
the distressed poor of Sydney and suburbs.” It
will be called the “Diamond Jubilee Charity
Carnival, to take place in August, and the pro-
gramme shows that every known form of enter-
tainment will be offered in order to collect coin
from the most diverse pockets. “ A Jubilee Doll
bhow ought to be a success. Think of the
opportunity of dressing up a miniature wax-works
show of the whole royal family. Or, really in-
teresting models of the fashions of sixty years
ITI -?wi b( ' mado ’ T a little effigy could be adorned
with the collars of the G.0.M., or a happy family
group dressed, showing all the Australian premiers
waited on by royal. flunkeys, the doll-footmen in
the gaudy St. James livery. A doll-show is a
decidedly bright idea.
Special appeals just now include the “Queens
Jubilee Fund ” for distressed women. The his-
tory of this fund proves that cliqueism kills
charity. Begun under Govt. House auspices
ten years ago in the loyal outburst of that
time, money rolled in plentifully at first
i he r e was a distinctly toney flavor about
the offerings All the officials were chosen from
among the Nicest only, and the charity was kept
so very select that it gradually dwindled and
pined from anaemia, and the mangles and sewing-
machines distributed to “ deserving cases ” had to
be wrung reluctantly from a limited circle. Now
when the fund is anxious to make a fresh start, the
barriers of caste a,re broken down, and the com-
mon, vulgar public are begged for subscriptions,
and everybody s money is welcome, and, to make
the interest revive, the scheme is to be put on a
popular basis. When high society is administer-
ing the fund it is called “ the Queen’s Fund ” •
when the plebs have to be drummed for money*
they call it “the Distressed Women’s Fund.”
, Judging from the disputes which raged arouncf
the journalists’ “At Home,” given on behalf of
the Society of Artists, at the Gaiety Theatre, last
Saturday evening, there doesn’t seem any prospect
of establishing a Press Club in Sydney. What
the row was about nobody now seems to have a
very clear notion, but it raged furiously all the
time the preparations were making, culminating
in one_ daily s withdrawing itself in cold haughti-
ness from the proceedings and consigning all
notice of them to oblivion. The press clique
wouldn t join in with one another, or perhaps, as
most of the women-writers’ names were on the
list, the men fought shy of it, for the average press-
man does fight shy of the average presswoman.
A Journalists Club, including both men and
women, would never be representative, for a man’s
club without whisky and tobacco is unthinkable
and a man-and-woman club with whisky and
tobacco would probably be lively while it lasted,
but would end abruptly in about three months
with reverberations. The men who join a woman’s
club are proved by experience to be nice, neat
young men with a charming smile and a
little axe which they grind so industriously that at
the club meetings the whiz of the grindstone
drowns all other proceedings. But they aren’t
pressmen—the real pressman is gruff and untidy,
and not at all a ladies’ man.
The journalists and authors who were running
the at home ”on Saturday evening at the Guild
-tlall provided a good programme, including an
original fragment recited by Mr. J. S. Evison, a
few remarks by “Hop.,” and songs by Burns-
Wabker and Mr. Woodhouse; Miss M. Wynne
lifted her voice m S. Hoben’s “ Ave Maria,” with
vio in accompaniment by Mr. Stevenson, and
tlall ihorpe gave lightning caricatures. A short
play Lights Out,’ was given, and the evening
wound up with a dance. Mrs. Creed (Louise
Mack), Mrs. Aronson, and Mr. J. Ashton
received a party from Govt. House, and the com-
mittee was distinguished by white bows with
unstained quills stuck in them. There was a
large attendance of guests.
Usually a theatre audience consists of three
women to one man, but in the Opera House, the
old-fashioned operas performed by E. Farley’s Cos.
attract three times as many men as women. Men
are the much more sentimental sex at heart, and
consequently the pathos of “ The Heart Bowed
Down ' and ‘ When Other Lips ” hasn’t been
worn threadbare for them by repetition. It
is interesting to see the old operas re-staged, if
only to note how subsequent productions have
plagiarised from them. “ Carmen ” and “ The
Yeomen Guard ” both owe ideas to “ Mari-
«?TD a Y a P d or three characters from the
Bohemian Girl ” have been abstracted
bodily by “The Pirates.” On Saturday
night three songs were interpolated. Devils-
hoof was encored for “ Will o’ the Wisp ”
with realistic effects like the flying match-
trick at the Jordans ; Miss Maud Lita, a pretty
Selina, warbled “ Good Bye,’’ and Miss M. Glen-
~en£in£ aid her turn before a mysterious black
cloth with faint murky lettering like an advertise-
ment-hoarding in Tophet. The ladies of this co.
are decidedly taking—perhaps that accounts for
the well-manned house—even to baby Arline, a
c f , ' m a pd placid infant who never wails even when
the firing is on. The costuming is bright but
scrappy. A chorus man lurking mostly up stage
m a brown rug and fat white stockings made
people blush at the mere idea of what would hap-
pen if he were to relax the tight clutch he kept on
bis garments.
How the nicest ” economise. A seamstress
writes me that a well-known family in Sydney
society circles ” lately employed her as dress-
maker for six weeks at a merely nominal wage.
She understood that her engagement was for
female work only, and was considerably surprised
when she was asked to make a couple of pairs of
trousers and a frock coat for Mr. . She pro-
tested, and was promptly told that if she did not
care to do it there were plenty of others. The
coat and pants were duly made to the complete
satisfaction of the wearer—an old tailor-made suit
doing duty as a pattern. “ And of such are some
Governmentouse habitues,” adds the poor seam-
stress.
The well-trained pupils of Cleveland-street
School performed in the Town Hall last week
before the crowned heads of Sydney in aid of the
necessitous tuberculous of the future for whom the
new hospital is to be instituted. The paths of
learning in that school are pleasant ones; as a
grown-up I can only wonder when they find time
for the three It. s. The scholars sang part-songs
and danced intricate gavottes and practised with
bar, bells and single-sticks. The juvenile prima-
donna accompanied by a midget violinist, had
the self-possession of five-and-twenty and the
short frocks of fifteen. A silver-bell ballet, directed
by Professor Read, jangled satisfactorily. The
concert ended in a loyal outburst of song ; they
saved the Queen, blessed Albert Edward, and
the audience filed out with the comfortable feeling
that all Heaven nas been annexed by the British
Hempire and the Union Jack is flying aoove the
pearly gates.
The Y.M.C.A. hall was crammed on Wednes-
day evening of last week at an invitation concert
given by Mr. L. Asioh to his violin pupil, Miss
Minnie Gunn. The youthful fiddler played to
great applause, but she isn’t a very great gun, and
would lay her hearers out more effectively were
she to fire a little lower. Sarasate is a trifle be-
yond her in some passages. The audience were
cloaked and bonneted and had a far-away appear-
ance, as if they had come on a long ’bus journey
from Annandale and Leichhardt and other dis-
tant regions. *

Prices didn’t run high enough to please the ar-
Gsts at the Society of Artists’ auction sale on
Thursday and Friday last. The buyers were
chiefly dealers and newly-married men anxious to
pick up some pretty things for the walls of their
villas. G. Lambert moked aoout the most satis-
fied man of all. b.d Long’s poetical fancies went
dirt-cheap at a guinea or two ; and even the news-
paper correspondence didn’t bring up F. Mahony’s
Centaurs to their adequate value. Brains are
going cheap in Sydney just now. The final day
of the show was given over to the students, who
hung their works on the walls, and the girls—clad
m flowing white- fluttered about classically with
light refreshments for the visitors. Some clever
caricatures in the tea-room were surrounded all
the afternoon ; and Mr. Hellings sang some comic-
songs which, strange to say, really were comic.
An Australian man, now at ’Ome for the
Jubilee, has kindly written me his impressions of
London spring fashions. A man’s descriptions of
millinery are always pathetic, but I gather that
hats are positively enormous. Blue is the favorite
tint, and it often glares in company with red or
yellow. So quiet tones are not “in ” for celebra-
tion headgear.
At a tea-party last week two ladies present wore
name-brooches—one had a small diamond inscrip-
tion giving the name of a waltz which she had
composed. Another vivacious, black-eyed girl
had the name of her musical effort in large-sized
gold lettering across her chest. The next com-
poseress, should call her waltz the “ Woolloo-
mooloo,” and then she could hang the name round
her neck, like one of the new-fashioned gold-
chains.
Shop-windows are still vacant of allurements in
the Way of jubilee mementoes destined to attract
coin from loyal pockets. Except for anew tie in
an aggressively unbecoming hue, labelled “royal
purple,” there is no gew-gaw exhibited as yet.
These loud puce bows can never suit any man of
any complexion. A dark visage would glower
above the crude tint like a thunder-cloud, and a
bibulous nose would shine, by contrast with the
tie, a bright petunia,
Every fact about the royal house of Brunswick
is disinterred in recent magazines and periodicals.
Somebody has padded out an article in a recent
picture paper about the “Queen’s Aunts,” and
there seems to be so little known about the numer-
ous daughters of George 111. that the baffled
authoress owns herself puzzled at the complete
oblivion which has overwhelmed them. She can
only immortalise the eldest by quoting Fanny
Burney against her as “ a shocking bad dresser ” ;
t' ie Princess Royal married a king of a sort, but
the other princesses were all secretly married to
equerries and lords-in-waiting, and the reader is
quite grateful to discover the fact, because it puts
some incident into their dull careers.
Who was it said that “ the English govern by
humbug and let everybody into the secret ”? Here
is the latest instance. A par. about the forth-
coming jubilee announces:
The Queen will head the procession in a carriage, in
which she will be seated in a sort of rocking chair, which
will enable her automatically to appear to return the
salutes of her faithful subjects. Her Majesty’s landau,
which will be used in the procession, is being gorgeously
redecorated in vermilion, pointed with gold, and rubber
tyres are to be added,
Victoria R. and I. will be borne m her gorgeous
landau, and her venerable body will be jerked
backwards and forwards by a hidden spring
as if she were a toy mandarin ; and this is to give
pleasure to her “ faithful subjects,” who have the
device carefully explained to them beforehand. It
W °i U , d u be less ehildi . sh - and Just as effective, to
publish press-notices in the “ friends-please-accept-
this-intimation ’ style, stating that no return
salutes would be given by H.M. on account of
oodily infirmity.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—

Chief Justice Way, the South Australian
Burdett Smith to whom flattery is as the breath of
life, is being so greased and stuffed in England
that he may now be expected to explode at a very
early date indeed. Another potentate apparently
liable to explode is Colonel Lassetter, of Sydney
a small but ferocious personage who, according
to the cable, has been saying that Australia glories
m Britain s isolation, and talking of how a united
Empire could defy the world. Also, Maoriland
1 remier Seddon who, on his arrival in London,
was met—cable—-by “ three Royal carriages ”
and Royal flunkey trimmings, and driven to the
Hotel Cecil to get Royal free board-and-lodging,
may also suddenly burst. * *

The Stickit Minister—Ex-Premier Dibbs.
Adye Douglas was 82 and Soft-goods Sar-
good 63 on May 30.
“W. J. Clarke had a thoroughly Royal
intellect—neither inferior nor superior.”
Furniss. hatted, has an excellent double
in Melbourne in the person of a popular pressman,
“ Teddy ” Finn.
Gov. Brassey shows a touch of ingenuity in
referring to the late W. J. Clarke as “ a greatly
good man.”
There are now five ragged schools in
Sydney. The first of these schools was established
by Premier Reid’s mother.
The Hawkesbnry natives still continue to
live long and strong. In Windsor last week died
a Mrs. Jones at the age of 95 jears.
Dibbs (ex-Republican, now K.C.M.G.), to
Queen Victoria, on presenting a stick turned from
tfee wood of an old pump : “ One good turn
deserves another.”
Bart. Clarke’s learning won him a Doctor
■of Lawship in an English University. His
doughty share in the military deeds that won the
Empire caused him to be conveyed to the grave
on a gun-carriage.
Botanist Baron Von Mueller is to have a
decent memorial, after all. The Vic. Government,
seemingly, will head the list of subscriptions with
a .£5OO grant, and various scientific societies
abroad are likely to contribute.
The ’Frisco press has shut down so hard
on butchervangelist Varley that he can’t get an
advt. into print, even at 500 per cent, advance on
ordinary tariff. But Henry says he can wait —
Hell will square all that up in due co use !
HEDGINQ A BIT.
HE : “ I thought you said Miss Wheeler was one of the fastest women in
Sydney.”
SHE : “ Well; so she is—on her bicycle !”
Rev. J. M. Curran, geologist, who has
played moth to the N.S.W. Government candle
for many years, has at last doffed his independence
and settled down as a graded civil servant. The
Public Service Board gazetted him last week.
Oscar Wilde is not yet in Australia, but
perhaps he is coming. A very dear friend cf his,
whose name figured prominently in ante-triai
events, arrived in Westralia the other day under
an assumed name and was cordially received by
local society.
Some of the Melb. doctors have presented
to the local Morgue a portrait of comic coroner
Youl, who makes suicide funny and sudden
death whimsical. He is the Yorick of the grave,
the Joe Miller of the cemetery ; he digs Sorrow
in the ribs and slaps Grief on the back.
Before N.S.W. Full Court—Justices Dar-
ley, M. H. Stephen and Owen —John Norton
made an amusing slip. Speaking of a recent de-
cision re contempt of Court, he said that “ Lord
Owen, of Kilrussell, had decided, ’ &c. Justice
Owen laughed loudest and longest of the lot.

Vic. Presbyterian Minister John Mathew,
M.A., is one of the few white men adopted into
an Australian aboriginal tribe. When up on a
run on the Mary River watershed in the Q.
Bunya Bunya country, some years before he took
to the church, he was adopted into the Kabi
tribe, and wrote down in musical notes two of
their corroboree chants, afterwards published in
Curr’s “ Australian Race ” with a long account of
Kabi manners and customs.
A weak “ squib ” re Chief Justice Madden,
printed for private circulation only, represents
that smiling dandy as having received his judicial
appointment from Premier Shiels, whereas the
lamentable job was worked by the late J. B.
Patterson. Gov. Hopetoun was about to trip to
Maoriland and the Melbourne banking crisis was
close at hand. Patterson and G. D. Carter wanted
a cheerful Acting-Governor with a fluent tongue
who would talk “confidence” and endorse their
compulsory bank - holiday scheme. And that s
how Madden came to spring straight from the Bar
to the head of the Bench. Patterson, the reckless
blowhard, in order to secure an unwise Acting-
Governor, created a frothy Chief Justice.
Cabled that Rev. George Douglas Halford,
M.A., has been appointed to mushroom Long-
reach, a township in the neighborhood of the
Barcoo. Many years ago a learned University
parson was appointed to a cure in that region,
and in a few months was so overcome with climate
or something that he married a local widow and
started a pub.
Vic. : “ I thought Peacock’s guffaw was a
journalistic myth, like Gladstone’s huge collars,
till I heard it punctuating an entertainment last
night. The sound is raucous, as of a foghorn,
reminding me of the premonitory groans or puffs
of a steapa organ with which an American circus
once tortured this city. P. seems to he proud of
and to cultivate the thing, like the lady whistler.”
Melb. flunkeydom is much exercised just
now as to which is correct—“ Dovvager Lady
Clarke” or “Janet, Lady Clarke.” Burke and
what Oscar Wilde called “ the best work of fiction
the English have yet produced ” are in much de-
mand. By the way, can the word “ dowager ”be
used in connection with a territorial title where,
as in Victoria, the right of dower is expressly
abolished? ■ ,
“The Earl of Onslow, ex-Gov. of Maori-
land, has now erected in his grounds at Clandon
Park a native whare, which he had shipped from
Wairoa before the stringent laws against the re-
moval of Maori relics were passed in M.L.,” writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent. “Vis-
count Cranley, his eldest son, is employed in cut-
ting eyes out of haliotus shell for the hideous
figures in the carvings that decorate the wall and
roof ends of the house. The whare is to be a
museum for the many Maori curios and weapons
the earl has collected, and will include the pre-
sents made to his boy, Huia, by the chiefs of the
Ngatiahuia tribe on his reception into brother-
hood by them. Huia is a delicate-looking child of
six years, more like a pretty girl than a boy, with
long, flowing curls of light gold. Onslow is favorite
for next Governor-Generalship of India.”

Not long ago a
convicted prisoner, as-
tounded at the severity
of the sentence just
passed on him by
N.S.W. Judge Docker,
exclaimed, “ Why don’t
you hang me?” “I
can’t,” snapped the
genial Judge. The other
day an “ old hand,”
caught with the pro-
ceeds of a burglary in
his bag, handed to
J udge Docker a written
statement in which he
admitted that his char-
acter was a bad one,
and acknowledged that
he should consider him-
