with 10 years’ penal ser-
vitude. The J udge ob-
liged him at once: Ten years.
Rumors that David Syme is, by his jubilee
munificence, aiming at a peerage, give added vigor
to the following words of Francis Adams: —
Of all the types I have taken, he is far away the most
typical—the tall, coarse, strong-jawed, greedy, pushing,
talented man, with his secularised religion and his com-
mercialised democracy. ... If England can strike a bar-
gain with him, even something not unlike Imperial Federa-
tion may, despite everything, yet become a fact.
Noel Buxton, son of Governor Buxton
(S.A.), was recently inveighing to the leader of
Adelaide Hansard staff on the iniquity of the
Australian squatters in shooting down aboriginals
suspected of killing their cattle. “Well,” was
the reply, “ supposing the stories you have heard
are all true, it is not very long ago that in England
white men were hanged for stealing sheep, and,
after all, a bullock is worth more than a wether,
while a nigger, even in your eyes, is not quite so
valuable as an Englishman.” The subject then
changed.
Apropos of the proposal to raise a statue
to Baronet Clarke, The Bulletin moves that it
should be made absolutely illegal to erect in any
public place any graven image to any person until
20 years after his or her death. If enough
enthusiasm remains after 20 years, then there is
just a fair chance that the deceased deserved his
statue. This idea would give the late lamented
time to simmer down and find his true level in
history. When he is just gone he looks very large,
for the same reason which makes a penny, held
close to the eye, look bigger than Mount Kosciusko.
Twenty years later he has got into line with the
rest of the great caravan of the departed, and his
height can be better estimated.
Editor Bulletin, — Re literary obscenities.
In 1863 the Kapunda (S. A.) Northern Star, then
run by George Massey Allen (an impulsive Irish-
man ; an ex-soldier; a trenchant penman some-
what Thady O’Kaneish in style) published some
verses which, from the acrostical standpoint, were
grossly indecent. The paper had been got at by a
treacherous contributor. Allen, who was agin
Governments in general, had, for some good while
previously, been attacking Treasurer Reynolds
(“ Teapot Tommy ”) and his colleague Strang ways
in most truculent fashion. Yet, notwithstanding
the fact that Allen was persona ingratissima to
ministers and that Pharisaic goody-goodyism
eagerly urged the Attorney-General to prosecute,
Stow, to his credit, flatly refused to take a
mean advantage of an innocent offender. —J. S.,
Parramatta.

The fair Flora Shaw admits personally
urging on the Jameson raid, as Times colonial
editor, and seems rather proud of being “the
woman at the bottom of it.”
‘ ‘ ‘ Doctors differ, ’ but electricians never
agree, and Marconi’s telegraphing without wires
has many rabid opponents and enthusiastic parti-
sans,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent. “ Professor Silvanus Thompson oonsiders
it merely the slightest improvement on the Herz
electric spark-generator across space, while Post-
office Preece is loud in the new system’s praise, and
will not let the Italian boy, Marconi, leave his
side. The writer, after thirteen years’ electrical
experience, sides with Preece, and from what he
saw the instrument do believes it will revolutionise
telegraphy. Wealthy syndicates are offering the
inventor big sums for the English rights, much to
the annoyance of the War and Admiralty offices,
a committee from which including “ Tor
pedo ” Brennan —saw and was conquered by the
scheme’s possibilities. Briefly, electrical waves
generated by an induction coil are directed—as
the rays of a searchlight are—at a ‘ receiver ’ seven
miles away, where they work an ordinary tele-
graph ‘ sounder ’ and signal messages by means of
dot and dash ’ combinations.”

The O’Malley, M.P., during the S.A.
recess has been absent in the Forrest country in-
suring the lives of typhoid-threatened miners.
The first heard of him for months was contained
in a telegram from Coolgardie during May ad-
dressed : “ Brother Wilton, chief of Advertiser
staff, Adelaide.” It cost 35., and it ran thus:
“Unable comprehend why terrible earthquake
allowed to occur during my absence. Will ques-
tion Government early date.”
The rumor stood awhile that Q. Govt,
meteorologist Wragge wanted to go with the well
advertised, self-advertised Borchegrevink on a
South Polar expedition. Wragge denied it at
long last in the Brisbane dailies, and said he would
like to go if the Royal Geographical Society of
Britain wanted him to go, and' if the Q. Govt,
thought that it would be of public advantage that
he should go. Whereupon Borchegrevink, Esq.,
dips his pen in gibes and writes to Brisbane
Courier :
Both Mr. Wragge’s age, physique, and the fact that he
for years has lived entirely in Queensland, and that, as
far as I know, is unable to run on snowshoes, would, “in
my opinion, render him quite unfit for service on my
staff.
On Borchegrevink’s “staff” ! Now, who is this
man Borchegrevink ? And what is the value x>f
any credential of his, not self-given ?
“ Advocatus ” writes :
Ed. Bulletin,—You lately printed an item which may
well bear elucidation. As I was concerned in the last
official act of the drama, I think it only fair to expand
your par. The name of “W.,” the hero, was Wheeler:
he was an inspector, or sub-inspector, of native police.
He had frequently been annoyed by gins being brought
into the native-police camp. He threatened to flog the
next gin he caught in camp. He caught one. He dele-
gated the flogging to the troopers. You know what
bloodthirsty brutes they are. She was flovged, with
stirrup-straps, and died_ of her flogging. TFie matter
leaked Out, and the Aborigines Protection Society of tjie
day (early fn the Seventies, of even help re) clamored for''
Wheeler’s life. He was duly attested and'cohmiittedlor
trial, and the only available witness theh (a white man,
the sergeant under Wheeler) was bound over to give evi-
dence against him. Application was made in Brisbane
(Wheeler had been committed in Rockhampton) to the
late Judge Lutwyehe for bail, and the Judge allowed it,
Wheeler himself in a large sum (Supreme Court Judges
always open their mouths wide) and two sureties of t lltOG
each. The late Billy Pattison and Captain Hunter, of
Rockhampton, became bondsmen. At that time the Cir-
cuit Court sat in Rockhampton at very long intervals.
While out on bail, Wheeler came into considerable money.
He is said to have paid each of his bondsmen the £lOOO
bail and then “ cleared.” He made for Italy, and when I
came into the matter he was living among the Sabine hills
near Rome, across the Campagna, on the farm, or near the
farm, which tradition still points out as the farm of
Horace, the Latin poet. In due course the Judge came
up on Circuit; the case “Regina r. Wheeler ” was duly
called on —and, of course, there was “no appearance,
your Honor.” Application made by Mr. Attorney to
have the bail “ estreated.’ Billy Pattison and Captain
Hunter called up; each “shelled out” with apparently
long faces. For years the farce was kept up. Every
Circuit Court saw the repetition of the comedy. In 1879
(or 1889) the then Attorney-General Pring entered bv me
a nolle-prosequi on the first Circuit of the late Mr. Justice
Harding, of happy memory. *
Another correspondent:—
There has been at least one other case where bail has
been accepted in Queensland on a capital charge— viz.,
that of the landlord of a Normanton hotel, charged with
murdering a blackboy with a swingle-bar. Accused was
found not guilty by a Cooktown jury, but the expense of
the trial ruined him. By the way, “Wheeler”—an
assumed name—was seen and recognised by some Broad-
sound residents in England two or three years ago.

Dear Bulletin, -Some-

An extremely significant portion of the
prayer or anthem or something said aloud by the
whole congregation at St. Paul’s, at the Clarke
burial service, was as follows: —
Comfort us again now after the time Thou hast
plagued us, and for the years wherein we have suffered
adversity.
The church authorities might do worse than label
this part of the service, “ Prayer on the death of
a rich landowner.”

MOSTLY MELBOURNIAN.


LONDON, Sunday.—At tie military tour-

CARTOONLETS.


POLITICAL
POINTS
The notorious (N.S.W.) Suffieldcase is on
its way for another Parliamentary "airing. Will
Minister Gould gag enquiry yet again?.
If rumor speaks truly, there will be a
phenomenal distribution of “honors” in connec-
tion with the Diamond Jubilee. A cheap way of
rewarding and purchasing loyalty to the throne.
“ Gauds save the Queen ! ”
The failure of S.A. Labor-party to return
more than one of its candidates to the Leg. Coun-
cil leaves Fat. Man once more supreme. With an
“ absolute majority ” required to pass the extension
of the Council franchise, that reform is as far off
es ever.
It will be interesting to notice whether
the Privy Council will betray the same reluctance
to review the N.S.W. conviction in a late case
where the right of betting is involved, as it
always shows in appeals against convictions where
life is involved.
The doddering Vic. Law Commission is to
hybernate for a month or two before starting
work. A pity Higgins didn’t vacate the chair-
manship when he saw its rickety constitution. It
can only delay law reform and disgust people with
attempts thereat. (
N.S.W. Attorney-General Want adopts
most gracious and conciliatory language in official
and other communications with two or three dis-
tricts that return Opposition members. So sudden
and marked is the change of style that enemies
scent “ Jack ”on a near election tour. King Jack
wishes he were in the Assembly, to break the back
of Federation.
Wallace Nelson, tilting at the Brisbane
(Q.) Worker crowd, and those Labor-members
who scream for “ solidarity ” : “ The ‘ stupids ’ of
a party have no difficulty in maintaining absolute
unanimity. People who never think run no risk
of thinki.-.g differently. There are many ways of
doing something, but there is only one way of
doing nothing successfully.”
Q. Acting-Premier Tozer’s latest insinua-
tion is that the attitude of Nevermind Nelson and
his colleagues towards Australian Federation was
“guided by a desire for the help of their own
colony in the first instance, and, secondly, of Aus-
tralia as a nation.” Cunning dog, Towzer ! He
didn’t put it boldly as an explanation, but intro-
duced it suggestively as an innuendo.
Dear Bulletin, — I hear that, in order to
frove beyond doubt that N.S.W. is a whole-hog
'reetrade country, and is ready to pawn its
wisdom-teeth in the glorious cause of Australian
Federation, daddy Brunker—the Premier’s locum
tenens— is going to have the cornstalk unemployed
registered, so that the likes of us can’t come down
and sell our muscle in a free market.—Leperland
U NEMPLOYED.
Talmage predicted that Greece would cer-
tainly triumph, “because she is a Christian nation,
and has her quarrel just.” Tacitus (plagiarised by
Napoleon) said that Heaven always sides with the
strongest legions, and Tacitus has “ got there ”
again. Talmage’s mouth always was too wide;
but perhaps the reason why the Deity didn’t
respectfully adopt De Witt’s suggestion was that
Greek Christianity is like Talmage himself —“full
of sound and fury, signifying nothing.”
The death of old David Coutts left a
vacancy in the Vic. House of 48 Rich Landlords,
and it almost looked as if there was going to be a
fight for the seat, but the second man retired from
the contest, owing to a cold in the head or some-
thing, and the usual dreary walk-over resulted.
If only some Government would attach a salary of
£5OO a-year to each M.L.C.-ship, so that live men
would hustle each other for the billets, and thereby
arouse public interest in the matter, it would be
the beginning of better things.
“ Paddy ” Glynn, who beat labor-nominee
Buttery in the recent S.A. bye-election for North
Adelaide, is the most patient politician S.A. has
known. He was elected in ’B7 to S.A. Assembly,
and since then has been defeated four times, and
elected twice to different seats. He is an able poli-
tician and brilliant litterateur, and wiped off an
old score with the Labor-party, which had the
bad judgment to run a nominee against the man
who did more than any other two S.A. politicians
to secure the present system of land-values taxa-
tion.
In Maoriland the practice of charging
municipal rates on unimproved land values is
spreading. The advantage of it is that, if two
men have each a JvIOOO allotment, the man who
has a big, solid stone building on his bit of ground
pays no more than the man who has a ramshackle
wooden hovel let to ladies of loose character.
Therefore, the man who puts up a fine structure
isn’t penalised for improving the town, and the
owner of the hovel is furnished with a good reason
for pulling down that eyesore and putting up
something better in its place.
Hain, late of Vic. Tele-
graph department, said to have
been behind the late P.O. en-
quiry, is now leader-writer for the
Age, and is being persistently an-
nounced as a candidate for
Frank Madden’s seat in the As-
sembly. Frank will probably get
in, as there is another good man
in the field—Beckett, a young
lawyer, president of a council out
that way.
R. S. Guthrie, at whose in-
stance the Absent Voters’ Act
was placed on S.A. Statute-book,
did not benefit nearly as much by
its provisions as did his two chief
opponents in the late contest.
Premier Kingston was also a pro-
minent supporter of this measure,
but on being asked at the mail
steamer’s side if he had filled in
an absent voter’s form he replied,
“By Jove, no; I clean forgot,”
and appeared to be much annoyed
at his remissness.
The practice, indulged in
by two Vic. Judges lately in the
cases of Quinn and Phillips, of
asking the jury questions with a
view to the “mercy ” "jurisdic-
tion of the Executive afterwards,
is, if not strictly legal, one worthy
of being sanctioned and would
probably work gradually, with
the education of the community
from which the jurors are drawn,
in the direction of the abolition
of capital punishment. Under
this system the sentence is prac-
tically fixed by the jury.
Q. “ Railways ” Dickson,
the Pecksniff of the Nevermind
Govt. : “ Er, as a private member,
er, I have been called upon, er,
to criticise some of the, er, ac-
tions of the, er, Ministry in the
past ; but, er, I have, er, joined
them since on, er, an honorable
invitation and, er, acceptance —
yes, er, acceptance ; and, er,
upon, er, deliberate reflection, I,
er, am convinced, er, that, er, the
Ministry, with, er, the exception
of myself—yes, er, of myself—
possesses, er, the ablest men sit-
ting, er, in Parliament —yes, er,
the ablest.”
Now talk of summoning another “Imperial
Conference ” in London, to nobble the visiting
Australians and ethers as Griffith and Co. were
nobbled in ’B7. That Conference spawned the
Naval Defence Act, with ships (nominally) tied to
Australian waters ; at the next, it will be sought
to break even the nominal tie, and tax Australians
for the British fleet generally. Also, Britain badly
wants a Customs preference for her decadent
manufactures, in return for moonshine about
“ the Flag ” and “ the Empire ! ”

Duncan Gillies, who has faded out of
notice since his return to Victoria, will, it is said,
stand for Barwon at next general election. Levien,
of Mildura fame, finding that the Barwon seat is
slipping away from him, may possibly retire (al-
though he denies the rumor) in Duncan’s favor.

KNEW HIS FATHEE.
King Billy (to cockatoo): “Oh, you very plash in that tin
house belon[?] you. I know your poor farder well-
he got to live in plain fellah gum-tree ! ”
Great is the jubilation in the camp of
Capital over the Glassey oration in Rockhampton.
To hear the man, who was once a thorn in the
flesh of the Boodlewraith, merely slurring over the
iniquities that once made his blood boil was indeed
joy. To hear him confess with the utmost naivete
that he was an aspirant for office ; to note the
almost fraternal tone of his allusions to Nelson,
Tozer and co.; to be able to twit him with the
change and its all too patent reasons—was bliss
indeed.
The usual 24th -of - May
leader in the Age was this year
devoutly loyal, with the smug,
placid, oily loyalty of the fat
Jingo to whom loyalty is a re-
ligion and the monarchical in-
stitution is the highest effort
of gods and men. Hitherto,
David has been wont to speak
of the monarchy as a thing that
would possibly be smothered in
the course of 10,000 years or so,
but now he has no doubts, and
his one fear is expressed in the
piteous moan : “ What if the
Queen should die? ” David may
congratulate himself in the words
of the Wesleyan hymn, “I know
my title’s sure.” Possibly,
though, the Age man perceives
that a title is a valuable thing to
refuse.
Coleraine (Vic.) Board of
Advice has resolved to give
copies of Parson Fitchett’s gory
“ Deeds that ' A on the Empire”
to the senior scholars in the local
state schools, as a means of cele-
brating the record reign. This
is a piece of crass effrontery on
the part of the Board, which
“ Siniler ” Peacock should look
into. Only very mild, specally-
prepared history is permitted to
be taught in Vic. state schools,
the object being to keep off the
corns of all parties. Fitchett’s
blood-and-thunder chronicles are
furiously and strenuously parti-
san, their purpose being to show
that nothing is impossible to
Cod when Old England is on His
side. As history, they are fanati-
cally fictional ; as literature,
they are a cut above “ Black
Jack, or the Rover of the
Rockies. ” They only please the
sort of man who deliberately
reads the Bible to his innocent
children.

But there was a big drop all the

in 1893 was 8518 per cent.
Will some member of N.S.W. Assembly
ask the Minister of Justice to ascertain why Dis-
trict Court Judge Gibson recently sentenced a
man to three years’ hard labor (carrying with it
nine months’ solitary confinement) for stealing a
tin of baking-powder of the value of ninepence ?
According to the evidence of the local
Inspector of'Charities, given before the Old-Age
Pension Commission, one Melbournian out of
every 70 is in poverty, while m London the poor
are five times as numerous—one in 14. ihe disas-
trous effects of Protection again, no doubt.
A prominent N.S.W. Parliamentarian,
hitherto a staunch supporter of Bung, promised
last week that he will vote for a Prohibition Bill
if such a measure come before the House. “I
would exert my influence to carry the Bill,” he
said “ with the hope of thereby ending the career
of those living on the so-called temperance game.”
Salvation Army Booth, giving evidence
before the Vic. Old Age Pension Commission :
“ We find that nine-tenths of the people who come
to the Army for help are willing to work.” Which
is the straightest possible answer to the comfort-
able lie that the unemployed are mostly people
who don’t want employment.
A D. Telegraph correspordent, re Sydney
North Shore bridge: “Your wise suggestion to
have the undertaking carried out by private enter-
prise will meet with general approval. Private
enterprise has already constructed both a bridge
and tunnel at New York.” The only New York
bridge which has any bearing on the case is that
connecting the city with Brooklyn, and private
enterprise certainly didn’t build that. It was con-
structed and made a profitable speculation by the
municipality after private enterprise had refused
to tackle it. And the time will assuredly come
when Sydney will be darned sorry that it didn’t
do likewise.
S.A. Leg. Council elections, which re-
sulted in the strengthening of the conservative
vote by one, should be a lesson to the democratic
party. A victory for liberalism was considered a
certainty, with the result that a vast majority of
the workers did not trouble to vote, and in the
Central district, the labor stronghold, Tomkinson,
boss S.A. Tory, romped in by about 300 votes,
while in the south Kirkpatrick also was de-
feated. The National Ass crowd won the day in
the south by diplomatically running a moderate
liberal and thus scooping the votes of the pro-
ducers. Similarly, in N. Adelaide Assembly
election the Tories voted for Glynn (a moderate
liberal) in preference to running a hard-shell Tory
and courting certain defeat.
The N.S.W. Government having definitely
refused to move about the North Shore (Sydney)
bridge or tunnel, it would seem that “private
enterprise ” will get hold of the job after all.
Also, it is probable it will fix the matter this
session. Meanwhile, the Government is going to
spend more big sums on scrub-cutting, or sand-
shifting, or digging big holes and filling them up
again, or moving some other white elephant
around, in order to keep the unemployed more or
less quiet during the winter. Why not start the
North Shore tunnel as a relief work ? It would,
at least, yield a better return than the scrub-
cutting job. The objection raised is that it isn’t
certain to pay interest on its cost. But the
ordinary relief works are certain not to pay
interest on their cost.
Melbourne Age is against Federal railway
control because “ local interests would be liable to
be sacrificed ” and “ a Federal Parliament might
prove wanting in sympathy and knowledge on
these matters.” Victoria is the smallest province
in Australia, and it has the biggest railway system
in Australia. In proportion to its area it has four
times as much railway as N.S.W. and ten times
as much as Queensland. Yet the local Parlia-
ment had so little “ knowledge of these matters ”
that almost the whole of this immense system is
crowded into the centre of the little province, and
the rest of the country is left a blank. About one-
half Victoria can’t make its railways pay because
there is far too much line for the area, and the
other half gets its produce to market per bullock-
dray. If the Federal Government could make a
worse mess of things than the local one has done
its ingenuity must be appalling.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
Events in the year 1897.
There are always plenty of alarmists ready to
annually predict calamities, but happily very often
their prophecies are founded on the imprudent,
inexact and misunderstood scientific reports. The
events which interest us individually, each hearth-
stone, every home, are those that bear upon the
welfare and health of the family circle. The
greatest event to us, then, is the recovery of a dear
one from dangerous illness: and we have had
pleasure in recording many such events in these
columns from time to time during the year. Many
Australians gladly tell how the health returned—
happiness came back—how the world seemed
bright again, which to them was the greatest and
most important event of the year. Mr. George F.
Vaxey, Okura, Auckland, in his joy over recovery,
writes a letter that comes from, and appeals to,
the heart. He writes : “About four years ago,
I was taken ill with Bright’s Disease of the kid-
neys, and was in the hospital for a month, and
had been bad ever since—on and off. This month
I was laid up very bad with it. Living in the
country where no doctors were handy, I took it
into my head (and it was aching all the time) to
try Warner’s ‘ Safe ’ Cure. After four doses I
began to improve. In three days I was able to
get out of bed. I have taken six bottles, and am
better now than I have been since first attacked.
Am still taking it; and feel sure it will make a
permanent cure. ” The nation’s welfare is the in-
dividual’s welfare. The nation’s pulse is yours
and mine.. Past events prove the value and wis-
doni of being guided by such indisputable facts.*

That one story is good until another is told
,is again shown by the fading away of applicant’s
affidavits in the case Ray v. Crick and Kiely
(wherein N.B.W. Full Court was asked to “ deal
with ” the well-known Sydney solicitor and M.P.
and a professional associate of his, for certain
alleged malpractices), immediately the respondent’s
affidavits were submitted to the Court. Crick has
made a beautiful crop of political and legal
enemies, who would rejoice in his disgrace and
downfall, and are much chagrined at what the
Full Court regarded as his complete vindication
Apropos, the fact that the Law Institute didn’t take
up the case against Crick made it pretty clear in
advance that there was nothing in it.
Stock Report.—Pitt, Son and Badgery,
Ltd., report heavy supplies of cattle and sheep for
the month. A\ ith the latter, much inconvenience
has been caused through want of sufficient class-
ing and yarding room. Our ample drafting yards
with extensive accommodation for watering, &c.’
adjoining the sale-yards, have enabled our experts
to handle all consignments carefully and punctu-
ally, ensuring most satisfactory results. We have
sold through the yards during the month 4092
cattle, and 79894 sheep and lambs. Calf and pig
sales at the City yards have also been extensive
and satisfactory.*
After 3£ years’ litigation and 21 separate
appearances in court, the cases arising out of the
loss of the Alert off the Victorian coast are at last
settled. Two appeals to the Privy Council and
one new trial in the local courts (with more pos-
sible appeals to follow it) were still pending, so
the case might easily have lasted for another year
or two, but the defendant co. suddenly grew weary
and made a compromise. Therefore the Alert
cases are not quite such horrible examples of the
law's delay as they might be ; they only show that
a wealthy defendant can deny justice to an impe-
pecunious plaintiff for three years or so, whereas
if they had been followed out to the bitter end
they might have shown instead that he could do
more hVe yearS ° r more ~P erha P s a good deal

The Maoriland “religious” organs were
proud to proclaim that Bosher made his peace
with Cod, and expected to go to Heaven—and yet
they saw no impropriety in incontinently hanging
him for the sin God had forgiven.
-Re the Westralian cattle-boats : One of
them which left Newcastle (N.S. W.) a few weeks
back encountered very heavy weather crossing the
Btght and as the seas began to come aboard the
holds had to be shut down ; result—7o odd bul-
locks smothered. The whole of the bullocks of an-
other steamer stampeded whilst rounding Nobby’s
in the late S.E. gale ; the caretakers were unable
to stop the rush, as the cattle had become fairly
mad. When the next port was reached all the
bullocks were either dead or dying. Another
cattle-boat, a few months back, lost 200 out of 400
head in the Bight through rough weather. 1570
bullocks and 5840 sheep left Newcastle for Kre-
mantle during the last month, besides 170 stud
bulls (Durhams) for Cambridge Gulf; 210 bul-
locks and only 160 sheep were lost.

Public Notices.
pUBLIO BE R vfCE BO A R D.
oO \ oung-street, Sydney,
The Public Service Board hereby noufvtff the 8 next
COMPETITIVE EXAMINATION for JUNIOR CLERK
SHIPS in the PUBLIC SERVICE of the colony win be
held on the 28th JUNE next, and four followin/dav at
various centres, which will be fixed to suit the conveni-
ence of candidates. The estimated number of appoint-
ments to be made dunn r the year is 50. Candidates must
be over sixteen and under twenty-one vears of age on the
first day of examination.be of good moral character 'in
sound health, and have resided in the colony for at least
three years at the date of application.
Every person who desires to submit himself to examin-
ation must forward to the Secretary to the Board to
reaoh him not later than the 7th June, 1897, a statement
in his own handwriting-, giving- his full name and address’
and his age, and notifying in which of the principal towns
of the colony he wishes to be examined. He must also
submit, with his application, an extract from the register
in which his birth is recorded, or other satisfactory evi-
dence of his age ; a certificate to the effect that he is of
good moral character, signed by his teacher, if he is at-
tending school, or has ceased to attend school for a shorter
period than six months ; by a clergyman or Justice of the
Peace or other responsible person if it is more than six
months since he left school i and a certificate from a repu-
table person to the effect that the applicant has resided
in the colony for at least three years.
A list of registered candidates will be advertised, with
the times and places of examination ; and, in addition
every candidate registered will be person ally notified of the
time and place when and where he must present himself
Every candidate whose name is not registered by the
Board will be so informed, with the reason why registra-
tion is refused.
The subjects in which candidates will be examined are—
Handwriting, English (dictation, grammar, precis writing,
and composition), Arithmetic (full course), Geometry
(Euclid, First Book), Outlines of Physical and Political Geo-
graphy, and the Geography of the Australasian Colonies in
moderate detail, English History from the middle of the
Eighteenth Century to the present time, especially with
reference to the colonies and dependencies. Candidates
will also be allowed to take any two, but not more than
two, of the following subjects Mathematics, English—a
prescribed author—(the same as that prescribed for
the Junior Examination of the current year), Latin,
French, German, Elementary Chemistry, and Elementary
Geology. Every candidate who wishes to be examined in
the extra subjects must give notice of his desire in his
application.
The fee for examination will be 10s., which must be paid
to a Clerk of Petty Sessions or into the Treasury, and the
receipt lodged with the Secretary to the Board 10 days
before the examination. Every successful candidate must
produce a medical certificate to the effect that he is in
sound health, and free from physical defects, before he will
be allowed to enter upon his duties.
The regulations governing the examinations appeared
in a supplement to the Government Gazette dated Ist
April, 1897. Copies can be obtained from the Govern-
ment Printer.
By order of the Board,
9a48. J. W. HOLLIMAN, Secretary.

TALES OF THE COJSVICT SYSTEM


SPORTING NOTIONS.
The N.S.W. Full Court decision declaring cash-
betting on racecourses illegal is a serious blow, not only
to bookies, but to the racing clubs. The public won’t
turn up unless they can have their little bit on, and clubs
live on gate-money. There seems nothing better for it
than to hold a church or a charity bazaar at every race-
meeting, and have a totalisator —10 per cent, for expenses
and 10 per cent, for the charity. Some church or charity
might lend its name in consideration of a share of the
spoil—or why not start at once a Home for Destitute and
Decayed Bookmakers, and run race-course bazaars in
aid ? The public would stand 20 per cent, in considera-
tion of the better prices, and that percentage would keep
a good few bookies from starvation till better (cash-better)
times return.
» * * *
Despite the harassment of members by the
police, Tatt’s Club, Sydney, brought off a very successful
meeting at Randwick last Saturday. The two-year-olds
were in strong force in the Flying Handicap, filling the
first three places. The winner Kilt —he cut out the six
furlongs in lm. 54?4sec. with Bst. 51b. —improves every
time he runs, and is not far removed from the top of the
tree. The country horse Martineer (Padlock-Restless)
showed up well, and will win a race before long. In the
Winter Stakes, Projectile ran strongly with his heavy
burden, but was unable to give winner Messmate a stone.
Vanitas ran gamely enough, but he is getting stale and
needs rest. The well-bred, but hitherto moderate per-
former, Silvertone (Marvellous-Silver Bell), easily won
Tatt’s Stakes. Of the Welter Handicap candidates, Kelso,
Euglo and Sovereign may be expected to be returned
winners ere the close of the season.
* » * *
Christchurch (M.L.), with a population of
45,000, drawing on Maoriland’s scattered population of
750,000, gives £6660 in stakes at its chief meeting, an
average of £2220 per day. Sydney (A. J.C.), with a popu-
lation of 350,000 drawing on the Australian population of
8,000,000, gives at its chief meeting £9lOO in stakes, an
average of £55 per diem more than Christchurch.
Christchurch has eight days racing in the year and gives
£13,110 in stakes. Before Christchurch had the totalisa-
tor its stakes were under £2OOO per annum. The machines
are operated by the club sec., and, so far, have cost 10s. 6d.
and4-10thsof apennypercent. to work. Thegatesand cards
are also managed by the club, secretary Wanklyn being a
firm believer in standing the profit or loss on everything
that can be worked by the club’s officials. The profits of
the totalisator go to the proper persons, viz., “ the
owners.” If the A.J.C. had the totalisator and put
through three times as much as Christchurch does, viz.,
£260,000, they could add £25,000 a year to their stakes.
* * * *
Tattersall Adams is being strongly urged to
draw his sweeps after the races are run, to checkmate
horse-owners’ blackmail. On the face of it this looks like
a good move, but it must not be forgotten that drawing
after the race would prevent the holder of a horse from
hedging. It is not the “ rat ” owners who are keenest
after the sweep-money. At least two of the wealthiest
men in N.S. Wales insist upon their bit, and a big bit,
too, to nothing.
* * * *
Died at Bathurst last week, the stallion Forest
King, by Maribyrnong from Xarifa. What a memory the
names of Maribyrnong and Xarifa bring up in horse-
racing and yachting old-timers ! Maribyrnong it was who
sired such classic winners as Miss Jessie, Richmond, Wood-
lands, Bargo, Hamlet, Cap-a-pie, Bosworth, and countless
other good ones, and who, when mated with the Panic
mare The Fawn, proved such “ a nick ” that the progeny,
year after year, used to be started at a thousand
when put up for sale as yearlings. Xarifa was also the
name of a celebrated Sydney yacht in the sixties. Dan
Sheehy, of Woolloomooloo Bay, saw one of his boys sail-
ing a model formed by the section of the head of a cask,
and was so struck with the idea that he built the Xarifa,
on similar lines, to the order of Mr. Parbury. She proved
a great success, and afterwards went to the Islands as the
State vessel of King Thackambau. Mr. Dangar owned
her only rival, the handsome Mistral, and the many duels
fought between the pair are green in the memories of
Sydney old-timers.
* * * *
The London Stock Exchange proverb, “Good
information and cheap money will ruin anyone,” is just
as paradoxically true of a kindred branch of gambling.
See the latest two examples of how the Jugginses, who
easily and cheaply came into fortunes accumulated by
miserly relatives, squander it backing “ good informa-
tion ” obtained direct from the stable.
* * ♦ *
At the V.R.C. meeting, last week, punters got
another lesson re the danger of attaching too much im-
portance to the effort of a horse that “ makes up a lot of
ground at the finish” without troubling the winner.
Canoona, in the Bay View Handicap at Williamstown,
had put in one of these abortive rushes. Trent was
winning with ridiculous ease when the mare, coming fast
from the rear of the field, seemed to be “ rapidly over-
hauling the leader.” Two days later, in the V.R.C.
Birthday Handicap, mare Canoona met Trent on 161 b.
better terms, and was made favorite, but again Trent won
easily, whilst Canoona this time remained in the rear
from start to finish. Trent—by Trenton-Sumatra —has
been whispered of lately as a rod in pickle, and consider-
ing that the black gelding pulled off three nice little
handicap races within 48 hours the “whisper” should be
worth further attention.
* • * *
Adelaide Birthday meeting brought big divi-
dends and much joy to the bookmakers, for big dividends
mean outsiders. Eleusinian, who won the cup, belongs
to David James of Auraria Melb. Cup fame, and the
owner made a big haul with a dividend on the machine
alone of £26 odd. The favorite, Acton, was nowhere ;
in fact, the Melb. crowd lost much money at the meeting.
David James has now won a Melb. Cup, Australian Cup,
and AS. A. Birthday Cup—a good record. But a win for
James always means disaster for the public, who never
know about his good things.
* * * *
Eleusinian paid a ,£26 18s. dividend in the
Adelaide Birthday Cup, and yet there were only six dis-
tinctly better favorites running for that important race.
For the same event, run at Flemington or Randwick, the
winner would have been certainly not more than a 14 to 1
chance.
* * * *
Horseflesh is cheap in Bananaland. In illustra-
tion whereof a Bundaberg paper tells how a local newsboy
received £1 from his dad to buy a horse with. Such
beastly extravagance disgusted the boy. To give his
father a lesson in economy, the youth spent a morning at
the sale-yard studying the cattle, and finally came home
leading three horses and with half-a-crown change in his
pocket. And one animal turned out quite a passable hack
into the bargain.
* * * *
“Surprises”— i.e., sudden reversals of “form”
—“ in connection with big events in Sydney have lately
been common enough to almost pass without comment,”
remarks Melb. Arcus. The Sydney public, surely, must
be getting very sick of surprises. Never a first or second
favorite got home in either of the five events at Rand-
wick on the “ Glorious 24th,” and two of the winners were
very rank outsiders.
* • • •
Key’s victory in the Royal Handicap at Flem-
ington, the other Monday, was the first instance—as far
as the writer can remember —of a two-year-old winning an
all-aged race under top weight. Key carried 61b. more
than ancient Babel, and 341 b. more than four-year-old
Canal, who finished third.
* * * *
They say that Albert Miller is the sporting head
of the anti-tote party in Melbourne. He reckons that the
Redleap stable’s reputation for straight-going would
deprive them of all chance of getting decent odds about
their jumpers from the machine, whereas under the Ikey
Mo system a commission may be worked before the public
get to know which of the Miller’s little lot (for big races)
is the fancy one. Therefore, astute Albert has the wel-
fare of Alf. Josephs and Co. at heart. Brother Septimus,
on the other hand, is disposed to think that the tote, plus
a few bookmakers laying tote odds, would be a desirable
change. The stable to invest its money with the “books,”
of course, instead of helping to diminish the machine’s
dividend.
* • * *
Donald Wallace has an improving filly in Moon-
lyong, who may perhaps do a good turn for the old Car-
bine colors next season.
* * * *
The inglorious death of Rudolph from thirst in
the far-back drought country in S.A., recalls the fact that
he and his stable companions, Dreadnought, Singapore,
Sinecure, &c , with one exception, did no good after the
Kirkham break-up. Had Mr. White lived, these horses
would probably have still kept on winning. How is it
that in other hands they failed to catch the judge’s eye ?
Change of quarters, training, &c., may account for a
little, but not for all. A Sheffield ped. once explained to
the writer how the “gaffer” system interfered with a
runner’s record in public. He could get “ fit ” time after
time, and be repeatedly ordered to stand down for betting
considerations, so that when his turn came to “jig” at
last he might have become stale through overtraining,
or so disgusted as to have ceased persevering. A man in-
dependent of gafferdom could try every time he felt “ fit.”
Though the owner of Kirkham did not study the public,
who often backed one string while the stable won with
another, still he always meant to get home first, and, con-
consequently, a horse, when in the trainer’s opinion “ the
psychological moment had arrived,” got a loose head and
every chance, as far as condition went, &c., of catching
the judge’s eye. Through market considerations and the
accompanying shilly-shallying, the reputation of many a
Carbine and Abercorn has been ingloriously buried in
the “ bag.”
* * * »
Utter was the only one of White’s lot to do any
good on the turf after the Kirkham break-up. She fell
into the hands of another owner, who sent her to win
every time she was fit and well, instead of vainly waiting
for a better market. It is a remarkable fact that Oxen-
ham, who, as a bookmaker, admittedly lives on the game,
has been a thousand times straighter as an owner than
nine-tenths of the “gentlemen of position” and commercial
magnates who profess to race for pleasure only instead of
profit.
• * * *
At Melbourne, last Saturday, the N.S.W. horse
His Grace won a hurdle race, starting at 6to 4. With the
fairly light impost he is likely to get in Grand National,
he should be dangerous, though he will have a tough cus-
tomer to meet in his compatriot, Jack Hanwell.
* * • *
The A. J.O. holds a one-day’s meeting at Rand-
wick to-day (Sat.), when Favor and Faber will once more
meet over hurdles. The former should win the rubber,
but he will find a dangerous opponent in ASolus. The
Exeter Stakes should lie between Aurea, Syerla, King
Clovis, Mutineer and Glendennon, and perhaps the
mysterious Aurea may be returned winner. Now Glad-
stone has been schooled to face the sodwall, he should
have no difficulty in winning the steeplechase. “ Sweep-
money considerations ” will make a big field for the Elles-
mere Stakes. True Blue, Kallatina, Kelso, or Hordern’s
selected (probably Rebel Queen) should win, and, “ on
paper,” the former has the appearance of a good thing.
The High-weight Handicap may fall to Sovereign, whose
most dangerous opponents appear to be Euglo and Tri
chord. * * •

* * * *
At Q.T.C. Brisbane meeting last Saturday,
Battalion dead-heated Archie’s Daughter over a mile and
a-quarter, giving her no less than 291 b., and yet a
minutes after the mare gave the doughty gelding 21b. an
beat him by a neck over a mile and a distance, inis was,
indeed, a most extraordinary reversal of form.
* * » *
In sheer desperation for pictures, the Austral-
asian publishes photos, of the Inaugural Run of the
Harriers of Melb. district. The alleged harriers, dressed
in a cobweb and a flushed expression, afforded a moving
si°-ht for their sisters and cousins and aunts as they pot-
tered along the roads of Malvern, every jaw solemnly pro-
vided with its plug of chewing-gum. Harriers and cyclists
justify the national name of “gum-suckers.’ A miscel-
laneous lot of boys with little more than loin-cloths upon
them is a cheerless sight. They do not attain within one
million miles of the dignity of nudity—they are simply
naked little chaps going to be washed by nurse, and very
nervous about the result. Naturally, a large number of
charming young ladies crowded to watch the performance.
The M.L. golf championship has been won by
Pride, of Wellington. In the final round he beat Ross
Gore, also of Wellington, by 4 up and 2 to play. Gore
made a game struggle, especially considering he is practi-
cally a new hand at the game. Among the prominent
players was Arthur Duncan, a youngster of 21, who has
given up football, cricket, and tennis —in all of which he
was proficient —for the sake of this latest fad of hockey on
crutches.
* * * *
At least one Australian boxer is said to have
earned more money than Fitzsimmons. According to the
(London) Spectator, this was shown in a practical manner
three years ago, when the “ boxing kangaroo ” was in the
height of his fame. This animal was said to have earned
£20,000 in 12 months ; anyhow, it was admitted at the
Roval Aquarium that he had “ made more money ” than
any other animal—more even than the most celebrated
racehorses had earned, whether in training or after.
* * * *
Ed. Bulletin, —Be the Corbett-Fitzsimmons
fight, Corbett certainly had the best of it until the end of
the 7th round, and even after that date landed most of
the blows; but there couldn't have been much ginger in
them, or Fitz wouldn’t have come up so fresh after each
round, and you mustn’t forget that Fitz connected occa-
sionally. Hall also out-pointed Fitzsimmons in their second
fight, but, all the same, like Corbett’s, his cake was
dough. As for Corbett downing Fitz after the fight, that
is easily explained. Fitz was off his guard, probably re-
ceiving the congratulations of his excited friends. On
the other hand, Fitz had all the best of what roughing
took place in the fight, though this was supposed to be
Corbett’s strongest pointnext to his cleverness. Naughton’s
remark at end of the 10th round, that “Jim’s gone,”
about sums the fight up ; after that Fitz had a winning
hit left. Corbett appears to have been devoid of one.—
J.B.

upon man?J’ , * *

c-oes down before the red-headed Fitzsimmons of 35 A
few years ago and all the top-notch scrappers failed to
touch the quarter century. Corbett himself was chain-
rfion amateur of America at 18; Slavin fought Burke at
25 • Johnel Sullivan became world’s champion at 27, and
met his hardest nut in Mitchell just as the latter attained
his majority ; while Peter Jackson was a mere lad when
Farnan went down before him. To-day scarce one of the
first rankers is under 30. Of course a fighter who reaches
30 with an unimpaired constitution should be at his very
best in point of stamina, to say nothing of the advantage
accruing from experience. #
“ Retire Australia ! ’’—for once. San Francisco
Cali, sits down thus hard on the Australian baseballers.
«* The kangaroos are a jolly set of fellows, but they don t
begin to know how to play baseball, and the seriousness
with which they took their own efforts amused the spec-
tators as much as a three-ringed circus. They are butter-
fingered, can’t catch a little bit, and each player thinks
he r s got all he can attend to in looking after his own posi-
tion—in which he’s about right! ” And this, too, against
a local team “ who will never set the world afire with their
own playing.” “To what base uses,” &c.
The ’Frisco papers have attributed fearsome
slabs of skite to Harry Musgrove, manager of the Aus-
tralian Baseballers. Known in Australia as a shrewd man,
with a full appreciation of the advantages of saying little,
the Yankee press makes Musgrove assert that his team
left their home for the purpose of showing Americans
“ what baseball was.” “ You don’t know the game here,”
he is alleged to have told one imaginative scribe; “all
our boys are as good in one position on the field as an-
other, and we are just going to whip your baseballers off
their fields.” To anyone who knows Musgrove, the yarn
carries its own refutation.
* * * »
The professional cyclists turned up in force at
St. Hilda, on Saturday, to battle for tinpot trophies. The
meeting was a benefit for Have Kirk, whose spine was
dislocated while training last Nov. All the cracks took
the field, the big race of the day, five miles scratch, fall-
ing to Walne, a wheel ahead of Ken Lewis. Green, the
Englishman, had a cut at the flying start quarter, paced,
but failed to do better than 24 4-5. Walne established an
unpaced quarter in 27 dead, which will take beating.
Kirk, who nets about .£3O from the affair,
was a most unlucky cyclist. A rider of top rank,
he never won a decent race, for he fell every
time he started, and took several weeks getting ovet
his injuries each time. And he fell once too often —■
his tire suddenly slipped from the rim and down he
came. After six’ months in hospital, he comes out on
crutches possibly acripple for life. Also, as his acoident took
place on the training-track, he was robbed of the generous
response from the public that a big dramatic smash dur-
ing a race would have elicited. Clem Fisher, the long
Maorilander, was another man who used to fall in nearly
every start, but Kirk’s misfortune came as a warning,
and Fisher now looks on.
* • * *
The first motor-cycle made its appearance in
Melb. on Saturday—a clumsy, ugly affair with stunted
wheels. It travelled about 20 miles an hour when at top
speed, and created a sensation along the roads.
* * * •
University v. Randwick was the closest game
in the Sydney football field on Saturday last. At half-
time University was leading by 8 points to nil, but Rand-
wick steadily pulled up during the second half, and Barrie
potted a goal just in time to give Randwick the game by
9 points to 8. Wentworth beat Wallaroo by 8 points to
3 ; and the Pirates beat Paddington by 10 points to 7.

The lovely Larpent-Leaver slander-case, at
Sydney, has been compromised by defendant
climbing down, retracting all his charges, and
paying plaintiff s costs. Even Jack Want couldn’t
save him when Miss Larpent submitted herself to
a medical examination, which showed that the
charges of immorality were unfounded. So that
the evidence of the tram-conductor and co
who s wore with a great deal of precision as to
Miss Larpent’s condition when they met her in
Melbourne, turns out to be pure imagination—or
mixed imagination, which is worse. Case shows
how unwise it is for any man to rely on his un-
assisted eyesight, or his assisted prejudices, in
such matters. Dear woman has always, if she is
innocent, such a conclusive argument in reserve.
That is, so far as infantile epilogues to the fifth
act go—there is never any certitude about the
fifth act without an epilogue.

Gr.P.B. writes from Maoriland :
Henry Lawson has settled in Kaikoura (M.L.) as school-
master at the Maori pah school. If it is inspiration he is
seeking he should certainly find it in Kaikoura —for of all
picturesque and outrd spots the writer knows Kaikoura
conies first. The town, which has a population of only a
few hundred, is along continuity of line (“ length with-
out breadth ”), reminding one of a battalion of cornstalk
volunteers advancing in skirmishing order. One side of
the street fronts the sea. The other side has not
arrived yet. The long, roaring billows of the Pacific
fill the air with clamor, and frequently heave their
wind-clipped crests across the main roadway, and up
to the very doors of the houses. The town is entirely
supported by the surrounding sheep-runs, and the
staple topic for conversation or argument, apart from
wool and mutton, is the question as to how long may
elapse before the sea shall have washed away the existing
nucleus of a street. Kaikoura is the town of the glorious
“bender”—and the regular inhabitants only smile toler-
antly, with a used-to-it aspect, when they see a beer-
emburdened shepherd working-up imaginary sheep with
the aid of a pained and wondering sheep-dog. Lawson
will find the place full of “Sweeneyish” and other
characters, who only need to be limned by his facile pen
to be notorious. The scenery around is good and diversi-
fied, and should be duly appreciated by Henry, after what
he terms the “wearying sameness” of the Australian
bush ; and, unless the task of teaching the native mind
ttie mysteries of numeration and conjugation prove
to be a set-back, we may expect soon to hear from him
some of the deeper-set measures in which he is heard at
his best.
Some stories about Australia’s great
botanist, the late Baron Von Mueller: —
The simple-minded Baron was an enemy of disorder,
and w ould often pick up scraps of paper, no matter how
small, whenever he found them lying about his garden or
elsewhere. Sometimes he would call his servants and
with many a gesture say to them: “Behold how I, the
great Baron von Mueller, an English knight and a German
Baron, have to pick up the scraps of paper which you
ought to have picked up!” The Baron was much
I bothered by beggars, who would sometimes not be satis-
fied w'ith the sixpenny-bit offered them by the scientists
attendant. Then the Baron himself would come out, and
; in a long lecture tell the applicant for relief his domestic
; troubles how much he owed on his house, how much his
, house needed repairs, how long a time it was since he had
i bought a new hat for himself, &c. Then he would
soundly abuse the applicant, and, after having thus
I eased his heart, would give the poor fellow half-a-crown.
One morning about 3 a.m., the Baron was awakened by a
man who had jumped the garden-fence, and who knocked
very loudly at the front-door. The Baron got up, and at
length succeeded in awakening his attendant. The
latter, armed with an iron bar, opened the door, and
was confronted by an intoxicated person who demanded
money. The attendant closed w'ith him, while the Baron
stood wringing his hands and wanted to give the man
who had disturbed his repose half-a-crow’n to go away.
The Baron was very talkative. Sometimes he would call
in his attendant, and to him he would ease his mind by
running down certain persons. Occasionally he would
hear a noise in the kitchen, and would ask whether there
were any visitors. “ Oh, yes,” the attendant would say,
“ there is a friend of mine.” “But he doesn’t understand
German, does he?” “Yes, I believe he does.” “Oh,
well, it doesn’t matter, for, after all, I have said nothing
against the man, have I?” Mr. Guilfoyle, who succeeded
him as curator of Melb. Botanic Gardens, was an especial
enemy of the Baron’s. One day the Baron got on a tram
and, as was his custom, he turned to the man sitting next
to him and began abusingGuilfoyle in unmeasured terms.
The person addressed said nothing, and a few minutes
later the Baron found out that he had been blackguarding
Guilfoyle to Guilfoyle’s own face on that occasion.
A snake perjury that is not hackneyed.
Brown relates how, at Croydon the other day, a
big snake was camping comfortably beside him in
his blankets. The reptile went away to a neign-
boring hen-roost, collared a chicken, and then
came back, fuller and fatter, and passed the rest
of the night in Brown’s company. One or two
little improbabilities may appear in this narrative,
but the word of a Brown is sufficient.

“ Thou tightest Time : / tight Eternity ! ”

Sam. King Miller, schoolmaster and jour-
nalist, and father of several prominent officials in
N.S.W. Public Service, is dead at 66. A few
years ago he bought Mossvale Scrutineer from
Chairman of Committees M‘Court.
A bitter t’othersider, per medium of a
godless Westralian paper, proposes that a law be
passed “ to do without God for six months and
appoint John Forrest to the post.” But, then,
they’ve done it already—to a great extent, at least.
The true Westralian really doesn’t know for cer-
tain which is God and which is John Forrest.
The cable reports that the new Austrian
Reichsrath has met and smashed most of the Par-
liamentary furniture, and that the members threw
the ink-bottles at each other’s heads, and hada rous-
ing fight which lasted two hours. There are just
now 25 parties in the Austrian Legislature, and it
appears they are all more or less against the
Badeni Ministry, which refuses to resign. The
only thing wanted to make that gathering com-
plete is John M‘Elhone.

Sensation in the pulpit. Rev. W. L.
Laufman, of Cadillac, Michigan, U.S.A., recently
advertised that on a certain Sunday he would, to
illustrate an anti-tobacco sermon, kill two cats in
the pulpit of the local Methodist church. A
packed congregation rolled up to see the show.
Prominent on the pulpit were packages of fine-
cut, plug, and chewing tobacco. At a selected
point in the tirade an assistant brought up the
cats, and a Dr. Miller administered nicotine to
them. The first cat died, squealing and squirm-
ing, in a minute and a-half. The second vomited
all over the pulpit, but died in a minute and
a-quarter after getting a second dose. Then
this “ humble follower ” announced to the
thrilled congregation that next Sunday he
would kill some more cats, to show the evil effects
of alcohol, and would also have on exhibition the
stomach of a drunkard. Authority, the Medical
Record.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Gladstone as a phrase-maker in his 80’s.
In his massive letter on the Eastern Question,
published in London D.T., the G.O.M. gets off
such as these :
“ From the official to the personal conscience.” “ In-
clination does not suffice to justify silence.” “Abdul
Hamid, the Great Assassin.” “ Every extreme of wicked-
ness is sacrosanct when it passes in a Turkish garb.”
“Treadintr on the burning cindersof Armenian massacres.”
“ Lord Granville was the mildest of men, but mildness
may keep company with revolution.” “ The reign of
wrong is not eternal.” “Small of stature, but full of
fight.” (A picture of Greece and the Powers : “ A David
facing six Goliaths.”) “This bad dream of Ottoman
dominion.”
Mr. Fisher, of Messrs. Fisher and Ling-
ham, Vic. Arcade, Sydney, having bought out his
partner, who has gone back to America, will
carry on the business under the same title, and
with the same up-to-dateness. One live depart-
ment is providing the National Cash Register,
which evokes big testimonials from Messrs. Las-
setter and Co. and other well-known firms. The
Register manufacturers have supplied a full stand-
ing army of storekeepers.*
There was much excitement in Melbourne
Benevolent Asylum one day last week, when an
ancient inmate, with his snowy hair a-hanging
down his back, was lugged before the committee
to explain why he absolutely refused to be cropped,
and why he armed himself with a hunk of firewood
and beat off the asylum barber whenever that
individual bore down upon him with drawn
scissors. “Please, gentlemen, can’t I keep my
little bit of hair ? ” piteously pleaded the insur-
rectionary ancient; but the committee fiercely
commanded him to be barbered, and the victim
was ruthlessly dragged away to execution,
struggling gamely in defence of his “little bit of
hair.” No doubt the old man was clapped into a
straight waistcoat and forcibly cropped, it being
the regulation and respectable thing for criminals
and paupers to be hideously barbered. Why, The
Bulletin asks, should this pampered pauper be
allowed to keep his hair on when so many conse-
quential and solid gentlemen are as barren of hair
as a cast-iron tombstone ?

An Important Case .
A CONCERT AND MEDICAL COMPANY
COME TO GRIEF.
DETECTED IN SELLING A PINK
COLORED PILL, WHICH THEY RE-
PRESENTED TO BE DR. WILLIAMS
PINK PILLS—THE COURT GRANT AN
INJUNCTION RESTRAINING I HEM
FROM OFFERING AN IMITATION
OF THIS GREAT MEDICINE— SOME
FACTS THE PUBLIC WILL DO WELL
TO BEAR IN MIND.
“ In the Equity Court yesterday, before Mr.
Justice A. H. Simpson, Mr. Knox and Mr.
Scarvel (instructed by Messrs. Johnson, Ibn >
Simpson and Co.), appeared for George Taylor
Fulford. of New York, proprietor of Dr Williams
Pink Pills for Pale People, and applied for an
injunction restraining L. R. Davis, P. Da,vis, an
G. R. Carey, of Glen Innes, from selling any
imitations of the genuine Dr. Williams Pink
Pills. The defendants were not represented, it
was stated that the defendants had been served
with a statement of claim, and affidavits were put
in setting out that the defendants were said to
have sold Pink Pills at Glen Innes and elsewhere.
His Honor made the order granting the injunction
pending the hearing of a suit which had been
instituted to restrain the defendants selling the
pills referred to.” , ,
The above paragraph, taken from the legal
columns of the Sydney Telegraph, contains a
warning which every person in Australia in need
of a medicine will do well to heed, and shows the
care and pains the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Com-
pany takes to protect the public from imposition,
and to preserve the reputation of their famous
Pink Pills.
It is only a medicine that possesses more than
usual merit that is worth imitation. Ordinary
medicines are not subject to that kind of treat-
ment, as there is not sufficient demand for such
medicine worth while.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People have
achieved a reputation for sterling merit un-
paralleled in the history of medical science. In
every part of the Dominion the remarkable cures
wrought by the use of this great medicine have
given it a name and a fame which has made the
sale of Pink Pills simply wonderful.
It is because of this great merit, and the conse-
quent enormous demand for the medicine, that it
is being imitated by unscrupulous persons in
various parts of the colony. The imitation is
cheap, usually worthless, and is only pushed be-
cause the imitator can make much more money by
its sale than he can by the sale of the genuine
Pink Pills. Hence the pains he takes to sell the
imitation.
The Dr. Williams’ Medicine Company annually
spends thousands of pounds endeavoring to im-
press the public that the genuine Pink Pills can
only be purchased in one form —namely, in
packages enclosed in a wrapper (or label), which
bears the full trade mark, “ Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills for Pale People.” No one can buy them in
any other form, not even if they offered many
times their weight in gold for them. And yet in
the face of these continuous warnings there are
people confiding enough to permit some unscrupu-
lous dealer to convince them that he can supply
them with the genuine Pink Pills in loose form by
the dozen or hundred, or ounce, or in some other
kind of box. Any one who pretends to be able to
do this is telling an untruth. Bear this in mind
and refuse all pills that do not bear the full trade
mark, no matter if they are colored pink, and no
matter what the dealer says.
Please bear in mind also that the formula from
which Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills is compounded is
a secret known only to the company and any one
who claims he can supply you with some other pill
‘ just as good ” is guilty of misreprentation, for
he does not know the ingredients of the genuine
Pink Pills and is only trying to sell you some other
pill, because he makes more money on its sale.
The Dr. Williams’ Medicine Company is deter-
mined to spare no expense in protecting both the
public and themselves against these unscrupulous
imitators, and will always be thankful to receive
information concerning any one who offers to sell
an imitation Pink Pill purporting it to be Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills, or “the same as” the
genuine Pink Pills. Such cases will be investigated
by the company’s detective and the name of the
person giving the information will not be made
public, while any expense entailed in sending us
the information will be promptly refunded.
Ask for Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People
and take nothing else. They cure when all other
medicines fail.*
MiSS E. Tweedie,

He was a Queensland miner in New
Guinea down on his luck. Inspired by a brilliant
idea, he began trading with the natives and found
that the Papuan soul hankers after Jews’-harps.
Imported a quantity and soon leftN. Guinea 90oz.
of gold the richer for his speculation.
A South Australian police-trooper, sta-
tioned at Cockburn, on the N.S.W. boundary,
luxuriates at the expense of Govt, in a room 16ft.
x 16ft., in which he has to cook, eat, sleep, and
transact his correspondence. Also, whenever
necessary, his palace is utilised as a court-house.
The Medical Record (Lond.) advises the
growing in sickrooms of the plant of the common
purple or yellowish-white sweet-pea, the scent of
which is particularly distasteful to house-flies, and
at the same time very soothing to the patient.
In October next, a million acres of land in
the Q. Western, Central and Northern districts is
to be thrown open for selection as grazing farms.
Moreover, the Q. Lands Dept, now issues litho-
graphs and printed description of areas to let—
soil, climate, absence or presence of artesian or
other waters, &c., &c.
The Eye-sight measure per post by the in
ventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough, the
well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney, enables
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection—
long sight, short sight, old sight. Full particulars
from Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough.*
At Wagga, the other day, when it turned
out that the two parties to an assault-case had
had a regular fight ? the acting P.M. fined both
for “ riotous behaviour ” —thus actually punishing,
in the plaintiff, a man who was not before him on
any charge; “a proceeding,” blandly observed
Judge Cohen, in granting a prohibition, “ which
seems tome to be opposed to natural justice.”
And two men recently convicted in Melbourne of
stealing had a statement of the fact endorsed by
the magistrate upon certain testimonials of good
conduct which they produced. This to make
punishment life-long !

H. : “The clergy seem, as Carlyle writes
in his ‘French Revolution ’ of Chancellor Maupas,
to “ gyrate and say ‘lt will last my time.’ Now
this bart. Clarke. His most intimate reverend
says he was the best man ever he knew. So do
all the other Anglican revs., while the Dissenters
think they have known one or two as good. Now,
on the doctrine of probabilities, is it likely that
the only individual of Melb.’s 400,000 with a
million pounds should be the God-created super-
lative best? Have the clergy ransacked all the
blind alleys to be sure there is no Christ peram-
bulating there ? ’
There has been an active demand for
divorces in Melbourne recently. In most cases
the husband has been made conspicuous by his
absence, having gone out to look at the moon,
shortly after the marriage, and suddenly mislaid
himself, and disappeared from all mortal know-
ledge. In divorce, disappearances are deceitful,
and Judge A’Beckett suddenly demands corrobo-
rative evidence in such cases, and asserts that the
Court must be satisfied that the husband has left
for good before the separator will work in the
future. This is rough on the lone, lorn woman
whose husband has gone off without a report, and
is seeking rest and change in parts unknown.
The wife knows her husband has gone, and that
the domestic cupboard is bare, but she knows
nothing about the intentions of the alleged pro-
vider, and, whilst she is foraging round for proofs
that he is gone for good, and has got another wife
and family in Darkest Africa, and has no intention
of returning in 10 years or so, she will grow so old
and lank and homely that a divorce will be no
possible good to her when she gets it.

Business , Bobbery, etc.
A “ Record Reign commemoration suggestion
in favor at Broken Hill is the'establishment of a con
valescent home for workmen suffering from lead-poison
ing. Perhaps not more than half the total number of
employes “escape the lead” altogether, yet not Y, per
cent, are fit subjects for admission to the ordinarv'hos-
pital. But the undertaking is too big for the people who
five in Broken Hill; and there is no movement among the
tens of thousands outside, who benefit by these men’s un-
wholesome labors.
Vanzetti, promoter of the public crushing works
erected at Northam, W.A., is now in London with a
scheme to carry tramways and other things to the mining
camps and mines of that country. It will only cost two
millions. Finding things mighty sick in the land of the
milch-cow, he has furnished British Australasian with
an eight-column interview on the death of the boom and
the cause thereof. “ The blame may be laid down mainlv
at the door of financial corruption, ruse and chicanery
on the one side, and to rashness, inconsistency, and in-
competency on the other. . . . Everyone forgot his
calling or qualifications, and jumped off his groove in
order to enter new fields more promising in the exciting
hunt after rapid and easily-gotten fortunes. This sudden
revelation and astounding success of the moment, arrested
attention and told on the people at large ; the fever for
wealth became contagious and the temptation irresis-
tible. Capital began to flow to the new world in
the expectation that in the wink of an eye it would
double and treble. Financial institutions threw open
their ooffers. These booms, their meteoric splendor,
short duration, and inevitable collapse, are typical of the
Australian provinces, and it must be that the English in-
vestor will not profit or be advised by past experience, or
else the recurrence of similar fits of insanity and delusion
would not possibly take place at such short intervals, and
under circumstances so identical as to render repeti-
tion not only inexplicable, but almost ludicrous. The
reaction which takes place immediately after, and which
causes people otherwise rational and intelligent to rush
from one extreme to the other, is quite as unwarrantable
as the boom itself.” The salvation for Westralia, proposed
by Mr. Vanzetti, is the removing of ores to the locality
of the water supplies. This, he thinks, would lead to the
consolidation of mines, fusion of capital, concentration of
machinery, uniformity of operations, etc. Of the Northam
works he says they extract from 80 to 90 per cent, against
60 to 65 per cent, on the fields, but makes no attempt to
explain how ores treated in exactly the same way give
from 20 to 30 per cent, more gold in one place than an-
other. Is it on account of the railway journey ?
* * * *
An example of Scotch thrift: “On the Tas.
west coast, a certain Caledonian keeps a general store,
and has supplied one family with goods for the past two
and a-half years, to the value of not less than 20s. weekly.
The accounts, which invariably ended with a halfpenny,
were settled monthly by cheque, the halfpenny always
being struck out. Well, last week, the account for April
was presented (the halfpenny on this occasion being very
prominent), together with another account for Is. 3|d.
(31 items, and extending over two and a-half years), the
items reading, ‘ Balance of a/c., Jan’y, Jd.,’ ‘ Balance of
a/c., Feb’y, £d.,’ and so on for each month for 31 months,
and attached was a memo, asking for ‘ immediate settle-
ment.’ ”

The yellow man must win. Wellington (M.L.)
Hospital authorities lately made a deal for the year’s
vegetable supply. A Chinese firm tendered at fd. per lb.
for all vegetables, the lowest white man offer being 2Jd.

Churches are plentiful in the gilded West, Kal-
goorlie not being behind in this respect. One of the best
OhnrchVfii' th f t dust ibegnmed town is owned by the
£kurch of England. This body has, of course, erected a
noble building on one of its frontages to two of the
principal streets—for the praise and glory of God? Not
much . It loves too well the golden calf. The frontages
are let on building leases at enormous rentals, and the
House of God is accommodated with a site at the rear of
a pile of buildings. The worshippers are edified by
the incense which reaches them from a row of latrines
and a stable, separated from the church only by a ten-foot
right-of-way.

A Welshman has written the Argus that in
ms country an insolvent is shunned by his fellows. Mul-
hall, the statist, says about 90 percent, of all men who
start in business fail sooner or later. What a lot of shun-
ning of his friends Taffy must do.

At Thursday Island, before the Commission, a
white man testified that pearl-shell was as good a show as
pegging out a claim. When a man had planted his
nursery he had nothing to do but wait patiently till
the crop came in. Evidently he looked upon the crop as
a certainty, and had no fear of droughts.

The Melbourne real estate market is, however,
at present so limited that it can scarcely be held to exist,”
said the Argus the other day. Five years ago the Melb.
daily press was in the habit of announcing, once or twice a
week, that proputty showed signs of returning forthwith
to its “ normal” (i.e., boom-time) value.
* * * *
The Coolgardie Consolidated Gold Mines, capi-
tal, £lOO,OOO, of which £75,000 went to vendors, failed in
an attempt to shift its operations to some other mine in
Victoria. Justice Byrne held that the words “ in
Westralia, or elsewhere,” provided in the memorandum of
association, were of too vague a character to compel
shareholders who had put up money for one thing to
spend it on another. Commerce, an out-spoken London
paper, is very rough on the Consolidated “ It is almost
unnecessary to add that the prospectus held out all sorts
of sanguine promise and glowing anticipations. It shows
what a drug poetry must be in the market that men,
capable of such lofty and perfervid flights of imagination,
should find remunerative employment in writing reports
on mines. Like so many other W.A. mines, it justified
the definition of a gold-mine as a hole in the ground into
which foolish people pour their gold. The Judge pointed
out that an investigation into the history of the co. would
not be unprofitable. The clearing of the atmosphere will
not only put investors on their guard, but will be of
assistance to genuine Westralian enterprises. At present
both the good and the bad are naturally tarred with the
same'brush.” W.A. “cat” exposures may be long de-
layed, but they cannot be evaded. They are already
making some of the big boonisters squirm.
* * m *
Editor Bulletin, — The Queensland press gives
currency to a report that “ a rich reef has been found on
the Port Moresby side of the Dividing Range,” &c. There
is absolutely no foundation for the statement, and it
reflects much discredit upon its disseminators. No reef—
good, bad, or indifferent—has been found, and the excite-
ment caused here is new to us. Such statements cause
incalculable misery, as there is nothing for the poor man
to fall back on on his reaching this' country but his
chance for gold. At present nothing further is known
than has been known for the last three months. The
Government is beginning to recognise that some pro-
vision will have to be made for the sick, and is doing
what it can with its limited means.—Yours faithfully,
F. A. Roohfort. Port Moresby, New Guinea, 26th April,
1897.
• * * •
One of a party of prospectors who left Hannan’s
nearly three months ago for New Guinea tells a horrible
story of the deception practised on them, and the straits
in which they were placed in a futile attempt to reach the
alleged goldfield. The first delusion was in making
for Port Moresby, instead of going to the other side
of the island and up the Mambare. It is the ambi-
tion of Port Moresby to become the Port Jackson of N.G.
To reach the field from there a climb of 10,000 ft. and a
descent of 8000 ft. have to be made. Native carriers
can t be got, and if they could, they could only carry
their own food, and, being naked, they have a habit of
dying from cold when 10,000 ft. above sea-level. Nearly
all the prospectors who tackled it have returned except
those who died, and now a great many are hanging
around the port awaiting a chance to get back. The
Governor, it is declared, should have issued a proclama-
tion warning miners on no account to come that way, and
the shipping companies, having made money by bringing
them to such a no-man’s land, should be compelled to
take them away again.

Heidelberg (Vic.) councillors are agitating
for the establishment of a crematorium in the
local cemetery to burn all corpses accumulating at
the Austin House of Incurables, which admits
many cancer patients.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of (160 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *
The New Japan .
[F OR THE BULLETIN.]
(Ridiculously cheap Japanese matches were recently ov
sale m Sydney.)
The new Japan in triumph moves
lowards cheaper goods than Europe knew
What wonder then ! It merely proves
That “ too far West is East ” is true.
And so for Europe’s golden bars
Japan her safety-matches trucks,
And sale of other matches mars :
What then ! “Ex Oriente lux.”
They tell us that the eastern hive,
Which we so lightly deemed effete,
Is very full indeed of life,
And like to prove us obsolete.
They tell us that the brown man means
To make things for us very hard :
To work us with our own machines,
And hoist us with our own petard.
Ah, well, if such success should crown
The little men, I’ll change my guise—
I’ll do myself extremely brown,
And have a surgeon slant my eyes.
I know now how to fan a flirt,
But if she come from far Japan,
I’ll have to be far more expert,
And so must learn to flirt a fan.
Our Art, alas ! the Art we knew
No more shall things Australian vaunt,
For o’er it then in every hue
The. “ loud chrysanthemum ” shall flaunt.
The stockmen and their dusty plains,
The weary swagmen and their billies,
Must yield their place to flying cranes,
The stork, the pond, and waterlilies.
The poet shall no more rehearse
His songs by aid of beer or baccy,
He’ll have to coin his cunning verse
By inspiration sought in saki.
O quaint Japan ! O pleasant land !
Why sacrifice your sweet ideal ?
Why strive to wear the Western brand,
So crudely and absurdly real ?
Why strive to mix in Western strife
And all the trouble that it brings ?
Why not teach us your pleasant life,
Your happy use of simple things ?
What shall you gain when all is done,
But Western greed and Western pain ?
Whall you win when all is won
But ceaseless toil of hand and brain ?
And in the changes that attend,
No greater gain to us attaches ;
We lose a very pleasant friend,
We gain—-a peddler of cheap matches.
Sydney.

Love in a Cottage .
[F OR THE BULLETIN.]
We sit in the dusk with each other
(For candles are eightpence a pound),
And I hear of the letter from “ Mother,*
And how Mrs. M‘Lean has “been round,”
How the pretty new hat she was flashing
Was cheaper than Mary’s by far ;
How the woman was here for the washing,
And how big our chrysanthemums are ;
How bread has gone up in a hurry,
And “ the man ” says that coals will be dear,
And that she doesn’t like me to worry,
But the winter will shortly be here ;
How she blistered her hand in the baking
(I must kiss it, to banish the pain . . .)
Blessed Lord ! am I sleeping or waking ?
For I ve seen the old fellows again.
I met them to-day in the city—
All three of them—happy as kings ;
And their talk was unhampered and witty,
For they know not the prices of things.
Con pays (it is true) at the laundries,
But that is his only expense ;
All Roley’s disbursements are “ Sundries,”
And Mao. lights his fire with the fence.
They are wealthy just now—gold and glory
For nearly a day they have known ;
“ Some ass has accepted a story,
And Con takes his oath it’s his own.”
And Mac. sold a picture—“ Behold him !
Charles Xerxes M'Ketizie, R.I. !”
“ The rottenest tripe ! ” “So I told him ;
“ That’s all that the beggars will buy.”
Dead, dead are those seasons of clamour,
When the world was untrammelled and young,
And every new girl was a glamour
That lived to be painted and sung.
Was it worth it, to give up the turban
And lead a monogamous life,
For the joys of a villa suburban
And a file of accounts for a wife ?
Eheu —what's the finish I—fugaces /
(I never did Latin at school),
I am fresh from the sight of their faces,
And I’m sorry—that I was a fool.
For the worst ways of life are the clearest,
And the man that pays rent is an ass ;
And I wish I had never . . . what, dearest?
Yes, you gave me the bill for the gas.
Vic.
