Caulfield and Whitechapel .
[F OR THE BULLETIN.]
“I don’t care if I die to-morra —I’ve seen London!”
I stopped to listen to Twanltey Mullins, the
Caulfield citizen known to fame as a jockey in the
old days, and more recently for having been in
charge of Chopsticks, the celebrated “ lepper,” on
his voyage to England in the Ballaarat.
“I’ll be willin’ to die ternight,” continued
Twankey, “ and happy, too. London’s good
enough for me. . . . Why, blimey! things
happen in London as would drive the bobbies
here balmy. . . . One night I seen a lot of fac-
tory girls dahnemg on the side of the road,
near a barrel-organ. The man was turning the
’andle like mad, and the girls was dahneing
lovely—high-kicking and all. And the bobbies
never so much as arst them to move on, even—
much less run ’em in. . . . And there was a little
white donkey ’arnessed to the organ . . . and
a girl’s petticoat come off, and wot does she do
but put it on the donkey’s forelegs and tie the
strings behind his neck. Lord ! it was lovely !
When the dahnee was done, I sez to a little girl
as stopped near me :
“ ‘ Get ’em to do it again, and I’ll stand drinks.’
“So they dahneed again. Then my little girl
took me by the arm, and ses :
“ ‘ Come on ! ’
“ ‘Wot ! ’ ses I, ‘ ain’t yer mates cornin’ too ? ’
“‘Lord !’ she says, ‘are ye goin’ to treat the
crowd ? ’
‘ ‘ Sc* she gets her mates together, and along we
goes. The first pub. we come to, they won’t serve
us.
“ ‘ Never mind,’ ses the girls ; ‘we know a de-
cent place. ’
“So we all goes along. In the pub, I asks them
wot they’ll ’ave.
“ ‘ Oh, we’ll have a pot of beer,’ says my little
girl.
“‘Wot,’ ses I, ‘one pot between a dozen of
you ! ’
“ ‘ All right! ’ ses my little girl ; ‘ three pots.’
“ So they ’ad their three pots, and I ’ad a little
pinch o’ w’isky. Then they ’ad another three
pots and another. And wot d’yer think the lot
cost me ? It cost me a bob !
“ So then we goes back to the organ and we ’as
more dahnein’ and some singin’. When it was
gettin’ a bit late, I stoops and w’ispers to my
little girl.
“She looks at me sort of scared.
“ ‘ No, mister,’ she says ; ‘ we’ll dahnee for yer,
we’ll sing for yer, and we’ll drink yer beer for yer
—but anythin’ more means workus for us ! ’
“Lord, I didn’t think there was so much virtue
in the world !
“ So we ’ad three more pots of beer and a pinch of
w’isky, an I goes back to my doss.
“I saw my little girl and her mates lots of times
after that, and they always give me a dahnee—
and when I was cornin’ back ’ome they all lines up
on the docks, and gi ves me three cheers, and my
little girl throws her arms around my neck, and
ses :
“ ‘Lord, mister ! yer the biggest toff that ever
came to these parts—and we’re very sorry yer
goin ? ’
“ So I don’t care if I die this minnit—becos I’ve
seen London! ”
* * * *
And now I hear that Twankey has gone
back to London with still another hurdle-racer.
F.
Poison Fever.
[F OR THE BULLETIN.]
Giulo was an Italian, and owned a small Queens-
land sheep-run. He was hospitable to his equals
and generous to his dependents, but, high and
low, he’d poison their dogs when he got a chance.
He had begun in the early days by laying baits
for the dingoes that “ worried ” his sheep and the
crows that killed his lambs. The habit became a
mania; he poisoned domestic cats and dogs,
dropped baits in his neighbors’ paddocks for their
dogs, and all along the road to town for anybody s
dogs. Part of the regular morning work at the
station was to cut baits and pepper them with
strychnine. These were either laid by
given for that purpose to one of the “ hands
who would take them into the first paddock and
disgustedly throw them away. All the hands
grumbled that their best dogs had been “ pizened,”
except the short and fat Mrs. Williamson, wife of
the boundary-rider (Giulo was afraid of her
tongue), and Bill Dixon, the horse-breaker, who
looked sharply after his little terrier —it was a
gift, and the one soft spot in Bill’s hard heart.
The boss’s dread of Mrs. Williamson had alone
saved her sheepdog, but one morning his valor
overcame his discretion, and, after a visit from
him, the Williamsons were busy cutting off their
dog’s tail and forcing tobacco-juice down its
throat, the rough and ready bush “first aid to
the poisoned.” But in vain : and that night Bill
Dixon waxed very funny at their loss, and Giulo,
after an excited interview with Mrs. Williamson,
during which she held the floor, spluttered to his
wife, “ By de gods de little English woman is in
a pelter.”
Bill continued to poke borak as he passed
Williamson’s ; till one evening they noticed that
he went by silently, followed by his terrier with a
couple of inches of tail missing and a very sickly
look about its jaws. About a week after that, one
ear was gone, and Bill looked gloomy. Later on
the other ear went, and after a spell of three or
four weeks that dog trotted home behind a cursing
horsebreaker, with no tail left at all.
Speculation now waxed fierce among the neigh-
bors as to what Bill would cut off next. One
night they found Bill, with a tear in his eye, over
a stiff and cold terrier. “ Went off while I was
considerin’ whether to chop off a paw or a junk of
his lip,” he explained ; and shaking his fist at
the homestead, he added, “I’ll settle with that
there Italian before the devil yet, and when
he’s in hell, may all the strychnine that ever
growed in beans be rammed down his ugly throat,
and may all the torturin’s and twistm’s of the
poor beasts he’s pizened be ”
“ By de gods, de very man I was looking for.
Bill, I want you to take some baits out in de
morning. ”
“ Right you are, sir. Goodnight, sir.”
EUBOTAH.
In Italy, the church marriage is invalid in
law, and the civil marriage is no marriage in the
eyes of the Church. Consequently, a man may
have practically two spouses—the civil wife’s
children “born out of wedlock,” according to the
Church, and those of the ecclesiastical wife illegi-
timate in the eyes of the state. According to
both authorities there would be a wife and a con-
cubine, the difference being as to which would be
which.
In Blind Alley .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A narrow lane within five minutes walk of thb
Post Office. Not a clean lane—the gutters trea-
sure much filth and garbage. Not a respectable
lane : and, in point of fact, not a lane at all—a
cul-de-sac. It is used as a front and back yard by
the occupants of huddled houses on each side,
and washing hangs limply on hay-band lines swung
from house to house, crossing and recrossing the
way. Ihe washing ’does not tend to increaw
one , g respect f or the wives of Blind Alley, most of

INFLUENZA CONQUERED.
A Remarkable Case that vividly shows the won-
derful Health-restoring Powers of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills.
(From the Orangeville Banner.)
There is no doubt at all that many people are
prejudiced against proprietary medicines, and
equally, no doubt, many look upon the testi-
monials published as much in the nature of an
exaggerated puff. If the Banner had been tinc-
tured with this feeling it has, so far as one medi-
cine is concerned, had its doubts removed. We
refer to Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, concerning the
curative qualities of which strong claims have been
made, and proofs advanced in their support which
seemed equally strong. But it is when one comes
across in their own locality a case almost rivaling
any that has been made public, that doubt disap-
pears and conviction follows. Such a case the
Banner came across and investigated, and now
gives the facts. The case is that of Miss Sarah
Langford, an estimable young lady who resides in
the neighborhood of Camilla. We were told she
had been brought near to death’s door, and had
I2U(.
ft.
<■
WBII*
BWi
been restored to
health through the
agency of Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills.
We decided, how-
ever, to cast hear-
say aside, and in-
vestigate for our-
selves.
We found Miss
Langford the pic-
ture of health and
good spirits, at her
pleasant home in
Camilla, In re-
sponse to our in-
quiries as to her ill-
ness and the cause
of her recovery, she
expressed her wil-
lingness to satisfy
our curiosity, and
as she added, relate her experience for the good of
others afflicted as she was. Her story, very
briefly, was as follows t —
“ I had influenza in the Spring of 1894. I did
not seem to get over the effects of the attack, and
as the summer progressed, became weak and list-
less. Any kind of work became a burden to me.
After pumping a pail of water from the well, I
would have to stand to hold my hand over my
heart for a moment or so, it would flutter so vio-
lently. I could not go upstairs without difficulty,
and towards the last would have to rest on the
steps, and when I got to the top, lie down until I
could recover my breath. I became a mere
skeleton; my cheeks were like wax and my lips
colorless. I lost all appetite, and my meals often
went untasted. Medicine seemed to have no effect
upon me. I was getting weaker all the time, and
at last began to give up hope of recovery. My
parents were, of course, in great distress, and I
knew by the looks and actions of friends who
called to see me that they thought I was doomed
to an early death. Then a dear lady friend died,
and I managed, out of love for her, to drag myself
to her funeral. The sensation of seeing her laid
away, believing that I would soon follow her, was
a strange one. Shortly after this an aunt of mine,
Mrs. Wm. Henderson, of Toronto, came to visit
at our place. My condition troubled her very
much, and she insisted on my trying Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills. To please her I consented, but with
little hope of any good result. The effect, how-
ever, was wonderful, and a pleasing surprise to
me. I soon began to feel more cheerful and
seemed to feel stronger. Then my appetite began
to improve and the color return to my cheeks and
lips. Prom that hour I steadily gained strength,
and was soon enjoying my former excellent health,
slnce £?. ln expressing my belief that to
Ur. Wimams Pink Pills do I owe my recovery.”
Inquiry among neighbors corroborated Miss
-Langford s story as to her illness and remarkable
recovery In her case, at least, Dr. Williams’
l ink Pills have given a striking proof that they
possess wonderful merits.

Pale and Sallow Girls.
What can be more distressing than to see a girl
drooping and fading in the springtime of youth ’
Instead of bright eyes, glowing, rosy cheeks, and
an elastic step, there are dull eyes, pale, sallow or
greenish complexion, and a languidness of step
that bespeaks disease and an early death if proper
treatment is not promptly resorted to and per-
sisted m until the impoverished blood is enriched
and the functions of life become regular. Upon
parents rests a great responsibility at the time
their daughters are budding into womanhood. If
your daughter is pale, complains of weakness, is
out ” upon the slightest exertion; if she is
troubled with headache or backache, pain in the
side ; if her temper is fitful and her appetite poor,
she is in a condition of extreme peril, a fit subject
for the development of that most dreaded of all
diseases consumption. If you notice any of the
symptoms, lose no time in procuring Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills. They will assist the patient
to develop properly and regularly ; they will en-
rich the blood, and restore health’s roses to the
cheeks; bright eyes and lightness of step will
surely follow their use, and all danger of con-
sumption and premature death will be averted.
Wise and prudent mothers will insist upon their
daughters taking Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills upon
the approach of the period of. puberty, and thus
avoid all chances of disease or early decay.
